Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Al-Balkhi's Argument and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes al-Balkhi's argument on divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Balkhi's argument that Allah cannot create actions identical to human actions and the refutation that moral categories are relative to human intention and do not apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Moral categories like obedience and disobedience are relative to human intention, not applicable to Allah's actions.

Al-Jurjani responds to an objection from al-Balkhi that if Allah creates a human-like action, it must be classified as obedience, disobedience, or foolishness—all improper for Allah. The answer shows that these classifications are not intrinsic to the action but arise from human purpose and intention, thus they do not constrain divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read al-Balkhi's claim and the three categories he lists (obedience, disobedience, foolishness).
Notice the assumption that any action must fall into one of these.
2
Understand the refutation
The answer states that these categories are 'considerations' relative to us, not properties of the action itself.
The action's essence can be similar; accidents differ.
3
Examine the objection about purpose
A potential objection: even if created, the action would still have benefit or harm. The answer: Allah's actions are devoid of purpose, so no such attribution.
Frivolity is not an issue for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة ( الخامسة : ( أبو القاسم ( البلخي ومتابعوه قالوا : لا يقدر على مثل فعل العبد لأنه إما طاعة ) مشتملة على مصلحة ) أو معصية ) مشتملة على مفسدة (أو سفه خال عنهما أو مشتمل على متساويين منهما والكل محال منه تعالى . ( والجواب : أنها ) أي ما ذكرتموه من صفات الأفعال ) اعتبارات تعرض للفعل بالنسبة إلينا وصدوره بحسب قصدنا ودواعينا ( وأما فعله تعالى فمنزه عن هذه الاعتبارات ( فجاز أن يصدر عنه تعالى مثل فعل العبد مجرداً عنها، فإن الاختلاف بالعوارض لا ينافي التماثل في الماهية ولما كان لقائل أن يقول : ما صدر عنه من أمثال أفعالنا إما أن يشتمل على مصلحة أو مفسدة أو يخلو عنهما وعلى التقادير يكون متصفاً بشيء من الاعتبارات المذكورة أجاب عنه بقوله : ( وهو) أي ذلك المثل الصادر عنه ( خال عن الغرض كسائر أفعاله ( المنزهة عن الأغراض فلا يتجه أن يقال : هناك مصلحة أو مفسدة ) ولا يلزم من عدم ثبوت الغرض ( العبث ) إنما يلزم ذلك إذا كان الفعل ممن شأنه أن يتبع فعله الغرض لا ممن تعالى عن ذلك . قوله : ( قالوا لا يقدر على مثل فعل العبد ) حتى لو حرك جوهراً إلى حيز وحركة العبد إلى ذلك الحيز لم تتماثل الحركتان . قوله : ( أو سفه خال عنهما إلخ) فيه دفع لاعتراض شارح المقاصد حيث قال : عبارة المواقف ليست على ما ينبغي لأن السفه وإن جاز أن يجعل شاملا للعبث فلا خفاء في شموله المعصية أيضاً، ووجه الدفع ظاهر من تفسير الشارح. قوله : ( أو يخلو عنهما ) الظاهر أنه أدرج فيه المشتمل على المتساويين منهما بناء على أنه أراد بالمصلحة والمفسدة المحضة منهما أو الغالبة فالخلو عنهما يشمل صورتين كما لا يخفى لكن أفرده بالذكر فيما سبق لزيادة التوضيح . قوله : ( فلا يتجه أن يقال : هناك مصلحة) ويمكن أن يجاب أيضاً بأنه مشتمل على مصلحة ولا نسلم أن كل مشتمل على المصلحة طاعة بل هي امتثال وتعظيم ولا يتصف به فعل الرب
The fifth group: Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi and his followers said: He is not able to create the like of the servant's action, because it is either obedience comprising benefit, or disobedience comprising harm, or foolishness devoid of both, or comprising equal amounts of both, and all of that is impossible from Him. The answer: It (i.e., what you mentioned of the qualities of actions) are considerations that accrue to the action relative to us and its occurrence according to our intention and motivations. As for His action, exalted be He, it is transcendent above these considerations. So it is permissible that from Him, exalted be He, issues the like of the servant's action stripped of them, for difference by accidents does not negate similarity in essence. And since one might say: What issues from Him of the likes of our actions either comprises benefit or harm or is devoid of both, and in all cases it is characterized by something of the mentioned considerations, He answered it by His saying: It (i.e., that likeness issuing from Him) is devoid of purpose like the rest of His actions transcendent above purposes, so it cannot be said that there is benefit or harm. And from the non-establishment of purpose, frivolity does not necessarily follow. That only follows if the action is from one whose action customarily follows purpose, not from one who is transcendent above that. His saying: (They said: He is not able to create the like of the servant's action) even if He moves a substance to a location and the servant's movement to that location, the two movements would not be similar. His saying: (or foolishness devoid of both, etc.) contains a rebuttal of the objection of the author of Sharh al-Maqasid when he said: The expression of al-Mawaqif is not as it should be because foolishness, even if it may be made to include frivolity, there is no doubt that it also includes disobedience, and the rebuttal's direction is apparent from the commentator's interpretation. His saying: (or devoid of both) apparent is that he included in it the one comprising the two equal ones, based on his intending by benefit and harm the pure or prevalent ones, so being devoid of both includes two cases, as is not hidden, but he mentioned it separately earlier for further clarification. His saying: (So it cannot be said: There is benefit) and it may also be answered that it comprises benefit, and we do not concede that everything comprising benefit is obedience, rather it is compliance and veneration, and the act of the Lord is not described by it.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the fifth group (al-Balkhi and his followers) that Allah cannot create an action exactly like that of a human servant, because such an action would necessarily fall into one of three categories: obedience (with benefit), disobedience (with harm), or foolishness (devoid of both or with equal benefit and harm). All of these are impossible for Allah because obedience and disobedience involve moral valuation, and foolishness is purposeless. The answer distinguishes human actions, which acquire these qualities relative to human intention and purpose, from Allah's actions, which are transcendent and free from such considerations. Therefore, Allah can create an action that is the same in essence (māhiyya) as a human action, even if it differs in accidental qualities. A potential objection is raised: if Allah creates an action similar to ours, it must still have some benefit or harm or be neutral, thus falling under the same categories. The response is that Allah's actions are devoid of purpose (gharaḍ), as are all His actions, so the categories of benefit or harm do not apply. Lack of purpose does not entail frivolity (ʿabath) for Allah, only for beings whose actions are normally purposeful. The commentator then clarifies three points: First, the group's statement that Allah cannot create the like means even if the same movement is performed, they are not truly similar because of divine transcendence. Second, the inclusion of 'foolishness' in the original text is defended against a criticism from the author of Sharh al-Maqasid, who claimed the term is too broad; the commentator shows it is properly applied to actions devoid of benefit and harm. Third, the phrase 'devoid of both' actually covers two cases due to nuances in interpreting benefit and harm, but the earlier separate mention of the equal-case was for clarity. Finally, an additional answer is given: even if the action contained benefit, that does not make it 'obedience' for Allah, since obedience requires compliance and veneration, which do not apply to the Divine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All actions must be morally categorized as obedience, disobedience, or foolishness.
Correction: Moral categories are relative to human intention; Allah's actions transcend them.

Checkpoint
According to al-Balkhi, why can't Allah create an action identical to a human action?
A) Because it would be perfect, which is impossible for Allah.
B) Because it would be purposeless, which is impossible for Allah.
C) Because it would be obedience, disobedience, or foolishness, all impossible for Allah.
D) Because it would be imperfect, which is impossible for Allah.

Key Takeaway
Allah can create actions identical in essence to human actions, but the moral qualities are relative and do not apply to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate your own actions as obedience or disobedience, do you ever assume that Allah must view them the same way? How does realizing His transcendence above such categories change your fear and hope of Him?
?
You often ask, 'Why did this happen to me?' — assuming there must be a purpose that benefits or harms you. What would it mean to truly believe that Allah's actions are devoid of human purpose, and how does that reshape your trust in Him?
?
If your intentions are the source of moral value in your deeds, how much do you purify them knowing that only He sees the essence? What one thing will you change today to act only for His sake, stripping away the human considerations?

Clarifications on al-Balkhi's Argument
Al-Jurjani provides textual clarifications and defends the argument's wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's clarifications on specific phrases in al-Balkhi's argument, including the rebuttal of a criticism from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Core Idea
Careful textual analysis defends the coherence of theological arguments.

After presenting the main argument and answer, al-Jurjani provides detailed clarifications on the wording, addressing potential objections and explaining the scope of terms like 'foolishness' and 'devoid of both'.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarifications
Read the commentator's explanations on: (1) the meaning of 'not able to create the like' (2) the term 'foolishness' (3) the phrase 'devoid of both'.
Notice how these clarifications answer potential misunderstandings.
2
Understand the rebuttal of Sharh al-Maqasid
The commentator defends the original wording of al-Mawaqif against an objection that 'foolishness' also includes disobedience.
The defense uses the interpretation of 'foolishness' as devoid of benefit and harm.
3
Explore the additional answer
An extra answer is given: even if the action has benefit, that does not make it 'obedience' for Allah.
This further distinguishes divine acts from human moral categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( والجواب : أنها ) أي ما ذكرتموه من صفات الأفعال ) اعتبارات تعرض للفعل بالنسبة إلينا وصدوره بحسب قصدنا ودواعينا ( وأما فعله تعالى فمنزه عن هذه الاعتبارات ( فجاز أن يصدر عنه تعالى مثل فعل العبد مجرداً عنها، فإن الاختلاف بالعوارض لا ينافي التماثل في الماهية ولما كان لقائل أن يقول : ما صدر عنه من أمثال أفعالنا إما أن يشتمل على مصلحة أو مفسدة أو يخلو عنهما وعلى التقادير يكون متصفاً بشيء من الاعتبارات المذكورة أجاب عنه بقوله : ( وهو) أي ذلك المثل الصادر عنه ( خال عن الغرض كسائر أفعاله ( المنزهة عن الأغراض فلا يتجه أن يقال : هناك مصلحة أو مفسدة ) ولا يلزم من عدم ثبوت الغرض ( العبث ) إنما يلزم ذلك إذا كان الفعل ممن شأنه أن يتبع فعله الغرض لا ممن تعالى عن ذلك . قوله : ( قالوا لا يقدر على مثل فعل العبد ) حتى لو حرك جوهراً إلى حيز وحركة العبد إلى ذلك الحيز لم تتماثل الحركتان . قوله : ( أو سفه خال عنهما إلخ) فيه دفع لاعتراض شارح المقاصد حيث قال : عبارة المواقف ليست على ما ينبغي لأن السفه وإن جاز أن يجعل شاملا للعبث فلا خفاء في شموله المعصية أيضاً، ووجه الدفع ظاهر من تفسير الشارح. قوله : ( أو يخلو عنهما ) الظاهر أنه أدرج فيه المشتمل على المتساويين منهما بناء على أنه أراد بالمصلحة والمفسدة المحضة منهما أو الغالبة فالخلو عنهما يشمل صورتين كما لا يخفى لكن أفرده بالذكر فيما سبق لزيادة التوضيح . قوله : ( فلا يتجه أن يقال : هناك مصلحة) ويمكن أن يجاب أيضاً بأنه مشتمل على مصلحة ولا نسلم أن كل مشتمل على المصلحة طاعة بل هي امتثال وتعظيم ولا يتصف به فعل الرب
The fifth group: Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi and his followers said: He is not able to create the like of the servant's action, because it is either obedience comprising benefit, or disobedience comprising harm, or foolishness devoid of both, or comprising equal amounts of both, and all of that is impossible from Him. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'foolishness' in the argument includes disobedience, making the classification redundant.
Correction: Foolishness here means devoid of benefit and harm, not including disobedience.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator argue against the objection from Sharh al-Maqasid?
A) That 'foolishness' includes disobedience.
B) That 'foolishness' is correctly defined as devoid of benefit and harm, not including disobedience.
C) That 'foolishness' is synonymous with frivolity.
D) That 'foolishness' should be removed from the argument.

Key Takeaway
Textual clarifications ensure precise understanding of theological arguments and defend against misinterpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator warns against assuming that 'foolishness' includes disobedience. In your own life, have you ever used a broad term to describe Allah's actions without realizing it might imply something unworthy? Will you commit to examining your words about Allah more carefully from this day forward?
?
The passage clarifies that even an action containing benefit is not necessarily obedience when done by Allah. How often do you judge Allah's actions by expecting them to conform to your human understanding of 'benefit'? Are you willing to accept that His wisdom may be beyond your perception, even when it seems to lack benefit from your perspective?
?
The argument states that even if Allah moved a substance to the same location as a servant's movement, the two movements would not be similar due to divine transcendence. How does this truth affect your view of your own actions? Do you realize that your best deeds, no matter how righteous, can never be compared to Allah's actions? How should this humble you in worship?

Refutation of the Jubbaiyya's Argument from Mutual Prevention
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the Jubbaiyya's argument from mutual prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jubbaiyya's argument from mutual prevention and Al-Jurjani's refutation, including the role of created power and the distinction between gradational and univocal power.

Core Idea
The Jubbaiyya's argument fails because created power has no effect; even if it did, Allah's power is gradationally superior.

The Jubbaiyya argued that Allah cannot create the same action as a human because it would lead to logical contradiction. Al-Jurjani refutes by challenging their premise that created power has any real effect, and then argues that even if it did, Allah's power is stronger, not equal. This passage is a classic example of how kalam refines theological arguments through precise logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: argument, objection, reply, and main answer.
2
Identify the Jubbaiyya's argument
Find the three alternatives (both occur, neither occurs, one occurs) and why each is problematic. This is the core of their proof.
The argument is a classic tamānuʿ proof.
3
Analyze the objection and reply
Understand the objection that Allah's power is more general. Then see how the reply shows that generality does not prevent conflict in a specific object.
4
Grasp the final refutation
Al-Jurjani's answer: the argument assumes created power has effect (already refuted). Even if it did, equality is denied because Allah's power is gradationally superior. Note the condition about tashkīk vs. mutawāṭiʾa.
This highlights the flexibility of kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة ( السادسة : الجبائية قالوا : لا يقدر على عين فعل العبد بدليل التمانع وهو أنه لو أراد الله تعالى فعلاً) من أفعال العبد يوجد فيه ( وأراد العبد عدمه ) منه لزم ( إما وقوعهما فيجتمع النقيضان أولا وقوعهما فيرتفع النقيضان أو وقوع أحدهما فلا قدرة للآخر على مراده والمقدر خلافه . ( لا يقال : يقع مقدور الله لأن قدرته أعم ( من قدرة العبد فلا يتصور بينهما مقاومة كما يتصور في قدرتي إلهين ( لأنا نقول : معنى كون قدرته أعم تعلقها بغير هذا المقدور ولا أثر له في هذا المقدور فهما في هذا المقدور سواء ) فيتقاومان فيه . ( والجواب : أنه مبني على تأثير القدرة الحادثة وقد بينا بطلانه فراجع ما تقدم وعلى تقدير تأثيرها فتساويهما في هذا المقدور ممنوع ، بل الله تعالى أقدر عليه من العبد فتأثير قدرته فيه يمنع من تأثير قدرة العبد فيه ولا يلزم من ذلك انتفاء قدرته بالكلية، نعم يثبت فيه نوع عجز وذلك ينافي الألوهية دون العبدية. قوله : (بل الله تعالى أقدر عليه من العبد ) هذا الكلام إنما يتمشى إذ ثبت أن القدرة مقولة على ما تحتها بالتشكيك وأما إذا كانت متواطئة فلا.
The sixth group: al-Jubbaiyya said: He is not able to create the very action of the servant, by the proof of mutual prevention (tamānuʿ), which is that if Allah, exalted be He, willed an action from the servant's actions to exist, and the servant willed its non-existence from him, then either both occur, so the two opposites would be conjoined, or neither occurs, so the two opposites would be removed, or one of them occurs, so the other has no power over his intention and what is decreed is its opposite. It cannot be said: What God wills occurs because His power is more general than the servant's power, so no resistance between them is conceivable as is conceivable between the powers of two gods; for we say: The meaning of His power being more general is its relation to other than this object, and it has no effect in this object, so they are equal in this object, and they resist each other in it. The answer: It is based on the effect of the created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha), and we have already demonstrated its invalidity, so refer back to what has preceded; and on the assumption of its effect, their equality in this object is denied. Rather, Allah, exalted be He, is more powerful over it than the servant, so the effect of His power in it prevents the effect of the servant's power in it, and the total negation of the servant's power does not follow from that. Yes, a type of incapacity is established in it, and that is incompatible with divinity but not with servanthood. His saying: (Rather, Allah, exalted be He, is more powerful over it than the servant) this statement is only valid if it is established that power is predicated on what is under it by way of gradation (tashkīk); but if it is univocal (mutawāṭiʾa), then no.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the sixth group (al-Jubbaiyya) that Allah cannot create the exact same action as a servant, using the proof of mutual prevention (tamānuʿ). They reason: if Allah wills an action of the servant to exist, and the servant wills its non-existence, then one of three consequences follows: (1) both willed events occur, conjoining two opposites; (2) neither occurs, removing both opposites; or (3) one occurs, rendering the other powerless over his intention. The first two are impossible, and the third entails that either Allah or the servant is not fully powerful. An objection is raised: Allah's power is more general (aʿamm) than the servant's, so no conflict arises, unlike between two gods. The reply is: generality means His power pertains to other objects, but in this particular object, both powers are equal, so they do conflict. The main answer: this argument assumes that created power has an effect (taʾthīr), which has already been refuted. Even if it did, equality in this object is denied: Allah is more powerful (aqdar), so His power's effect prevents the servant's power's effect. This does not totally negate the servant's power but establishes a type of incapacity (ʿajz) in that specific action, which is incompatible with divinity (ulūhiyya) but not with servanthood (ʿubudiyya). The commentator then notes that the argument that Allah is 'more powerful' holds only if power is gradational (tashkīk), i.e., admitting degrees; if power is univocal (mutawāṭiʾa), meaning applied equally to all agents, then the reasoning fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from tamānuʿ is only used to prove the oneness of God; any conflict between two powers implies weakness.
Correction: Here, the tamānuʿ is applied to divine vs. human power, but the refutation shows that a specific limitation on the servant's power does not negate divine omnipotence, as it is only a type of incapacity compatible with servanthood.

Checkpoint
What is the primary premise of the Jubbaiyya's argument from tamānuʿ?
A) Allah's power is more general than the servant's, so no conflict arises.
B) If Allah wills a certain action and the servant wills its non-existence, a logical impossibility results.
C) The servant's power is independent and equal to Allah's.
D) Allah cannot will evil actions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani refutes the Jubbaiyya by denying the efficacy of created power and asserting Allah's gradational superiority, showing that a logical proof against divine power must be carefully examined for hidden premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the Jubbaiyya's argument proves nothing about Allah's power because it assumes human power is effective. But in your daily life, do you act as if your own power is the decisive factor in your successes and failures? What would it mean for you to truly believe that your power has no real effect — that every outcome is under Allah's direct control? Will you examine one area of your life today where you feel in control and consciously remind yourself that your power is derivative and limited, and that only Allah's power is truly effective?
?
The passage concludes that a type of incapacity in a specific action is compatible with servanthood but not with divinity. Are you willing to accept your own limitations and 'incapacities' as part of being a servant of Allah, or do you secretly resent them as flaws? When you cannot achieve what you want — whether in worship, work, or relationships — do you see that as a failure of your own will, or as a reminder that you are a servant who depends entirely on Allah? How can you transform that feeling of helplessness into gratitude for Allah's sufficiency?
?
Al-Jurjani mentions that the argument only holds if power is gradational (tashkīk); if it is univocal, the comparison fails. In your understanding of Allah's power, do you ever unconsciously compare it to human power as if they are the same kind of thing — just more of it? How does the concept of tashkīk challenge you to rethink your mental image of Allah's omnipotence? Take a moment to reflect: Do you truly believe that Allah's power is not just greater in degree, but different in kind? How can this belief shape your humility and awe before Him?

The First Proof: Divine Knowledge from Precision
Al-Jurjani presents the mutakallimun's first proof of divine knowledge from the perfection of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof of divine knowledge used by the mutakallimun, based on the precision of Allah's actions, and the objection raised against it.

Core Idea
The perfection of creation directly proves that its Creator is knowing.

This lesson presents the first of two methods used by Islamic theologians to prove that Allah is knowing. The argument is built on observing the precision and wisdom in creation, which necessarily implies a knowledgeable Maker. We will also examine a key objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: introduction, research, proof, objection, gloss.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof consists of a minor premise (Allah's actions are precise) and a major premise (whoever's actions are precise is knowledgeable). Recognize how each is supported.
The minor is shown through observation; the major is deemed necessary (ḍarūrī).
3
Understand the objection
An objector challenges the definition of 'precise'. Two interpretations are given: absolute benefit (denied) or partial benefit (insufficient). See how this affects the proof.
The objection is not directly answered in this passage, but the commentator clarifies later.
4
Reflect on the commentator's glosses
The commentator adds three glosses: another proof from ignorance, a note on the nature of knowledge as a superadded attribute, and the meaning of 'writing' as prose composition.
These clarify and extend the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : في علمه تعالى وفيه بحثان: البحث الأول : في إثباته وهو متفق عليه بيننا الحكماء، وإنما نفاه (شرذمة من قدماء الفلاسفة ) لا يعبأ بهم، وسنذكره لكن المسلك ) في إثبات كونه تعالى عالما ) مختلف ، أما المتكلمون فلهم مسلكان : الأول أن فعله تعالى (متقن أي محكم خال عن وجوه الخلل ومشتمل على حكم ومصالح متكثرة ( وكل من فعله متقن فهو عالم، أما الأول ( أعني إتقان فعله ( فظاهر لمن نظر في الآفاق والأنفس وتأمل ارتباط العلويات بالسفليات سيما إذا تأمل ( في الحيوانات وما هديت إليه من مصالحها وأعطيت من الآلات المناسبة لها، ويعين على ذلك علم التشريح ومنافع خلقة الإنسان وأعضائه التي قد كسرت عليها المجلدات، وأما الثاني ) وهو أن كان فعله متقناً كان عالماً ( فضروري وينبه عليه أن من رأى خطاً حسناً يتضمن ألفاظاً عذبة رشيقة تدل على معان دقيقة مؤنقة علم بالضرورة أن كاتبه عالم، وكذلك من سمع خطاباً منتظماً مناسباً للمقام من شخص يضطر إلى أن بأنه عالم فإن قيل : المتقن إن أردت به الموافق للمصلحة من جمع الوجوه فممنوع ) إن فعله تعالى متقن ( إذ لا شيء من مفردات العالم ومركباته إلا ويشتمل على مفسدة ما ) ويتضمن ويمكن تصوره على وجه أكمل مما هو عليه ( أو الموافق ) للمصلحة ) من بعض الوجوه فلا يدل على العلم إذ ما من أثر إلا ويمكن أن ينتفع به قوله : (البحث الأول في إثباته قد استدل على ثبوت العلم له تعالى بالنسبة إلى الكليات والجزئيات بأن عدم العلم عما من شأنه ذلك جهل والجهل نقص يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه فوجب ثبوت العلم العام له وهذا ظاهر إذا أثبت أولاً كون العلم بجميع ما ذكر من شأنه تعالى . قوله : (وكل من فعله متقن فهو عالم لا يخفى أن اتذان الفعل وكذا قدرة الفاعل إنما تدل على انكشاف المفعول للفاعل، وأما أن للفاعل صفة موجودة زائدة على ذاته فلا . قوله : (علم بالضرورة أن كاتبه (عالم الكتابة يقال في عرف الأدباء لإنشاء النثر، كما أن الشعر يقال لإنشاء النظم، والظاهر أنه المراد هاهنا لا الخط.
The third topic: On His knowledge, exalted be He. It contains two investigations: The first investigation: On proving it, and it is agreed upon between us and the philosophers; only a handful of ancient philosophers denied it, no need to pay attention to them, and we will mention it. But the method of proving that He, exalted be He, is knowing is different. As for the mutakallimūn, they have two methods: The first: that His action, exalted be He, is precise (mutqan), i.e., well-ordered, free from aspects of defect, and comprising wisdoms and many benefits; and everyone whose action is precise is knowledgeable. As for the first (i.e., the precision of His action), it is apparent to whoever looks at the horizons and the souls, and contemplates the connection of the celestial and terrestrial things, especially if he contemplates the animals and what they have been guided to of their benefits and given of suitable instruments, and the science of anatomy and the benefits of the creation of humankind and its organs, concerning which volumes have been written, helps in that. As for the second (i.e., that if his action is precise, he is knowledgeable), it is necessary (ḍarūrī), and what indicates it is that someone who sees beautiful handwriting comprising sweet, graceful words indicating delicate, refined meanings knows necessarily that its writer is knowledgeable. Similarly, someone who hears a well-organized speech appropriate to the situation from a person is compelled to know that he is knowledgeable. For if it is said: 'Precise' – if you mean by it that which conforms to benefit in all respects, then it is denied that His action, exalted be He, is precise, for there is nothing among the individual entities of the world and their compounds except that it comprises some harm, and it contains what can be imagined in a more perfect way than it is; or (if you mean) that which conforms to benefit in some respects, then it does not indicate knowledge, for there is no effect except that benefit can be derived from it. His saying: (The first investigation on proving it: He has argued for the establishment of knowledge for Him, exalted be He, with respect to universals and particulars by [the argument] that lack of knowledge of what is such that it is in His nature to know is ignorance, and ignorance is a deficiency from which it is necessary to declare Allah, exalted be He, transcendent, so the establishment of general knowledge for Him is necessary. And this is apparent if it is first established that knowledge of all that is mentioned is in His nature. His saying: (And everyone whose action is precise is knowledgeable) it is not hidden that the perfection of the action and likewise the power of the agent only indicate the disclosure of the effect to the agent, but as for the agent having an existing attribute superadded to His essence, then no. His saying: (knows necessarily that its writer is knowledgeable) 'writing' in the terminology of men of letters is used for composing prose, just as 'poetry' is used for composing verse, and apparent that this is what is intended here, not handwriting.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage introduces the third major topic: divine knowledge (ʿilm). It states that the first research (bahth) is on proving that God is knowing, a point agreed upon by Islamic theologians and philosophers, except for a small group of ancient philosophers. The mutakallimūn use two methods; only the first is presented here. The first method: Allah's actions are precise (mutqan), meaning well-ordered, free from defects, and full of wisdom and benefits. Anyone whose actions are precise is knowledgeable. The minor premise—Allah's actions are precise—is evident from observing the universe (horizons) and human souls, including the connection between celestial and terrestrial realms, the design of animals with suitable organs, and human anatomy. The major premise—that precision indicates knowledge—is intuitive (ḍarūrī). An example: seeing beautiful handwriting that conveys eloquent meanings immediately tells you the writer is knowledgeable; similarly, hearing a well-structured speech reveals the speaker's knowledge. An objection then follows: if 'precise' means fully beneficial in every way, it is denied that Allah's actions are precise, because every creature has some harm and could be imagined more perfectly; if it means beneficial in some respects, that does not prove knowledge, since anything can be beneficial in some way. The commentator then provides three glosses. First, he mentions another proof of God's knowledge from the impossibility of ignorance in Him, which is a deficiency. Second, he clarifies that the argument from precision only shows that the agent is aware of his product, not necessarily that knowledge is a distinct superadded attribute. Third, he explains that 'writing' (kitāba) here refers to composing prose, not handwriting, as per literary convention.

Misconception: The argument from precision proves that knowledge is a separate attribute added to God's essence.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the proof only indicates that God is aware of His actions, not necessarily that knowledge is a superadded attribute.

Checkpoint

B) Whoever performs precise actions is knowledgeable.
C) The universe contains many benefits.

Key Takeaway
The precision and order in creation serve as necessary indicators of the Creator's knowledge, a foundational argument in Islamic theology.

?
The argument states that beautiful handwriting immediately tells you the writer is knowledgeable. How often do you let the 'handwriting' of Allah's creation actually move you to acknowledge His knowledge? When was the last time you paused at a sunset or a biological process and felt compelled to say, 'My Lord is Knowing'?

First Objection and Answer: Definition of 'Mutqan'
Objection: If 'mutqan' means beneficial without defect, then not all actions are mutqan; answer: it means astonishing craftsmanship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection challenges the proof from precision by questioning the definition of 'mutqan', and how the author defends it by redefining 'mutqan' as astonishing craftsmanship rather than absolute perfection.

Core Idea
The proof from precision relies on astonishing design, not absolute flawlessness.

This lesson presents a critical objection to the proof from divine precision. The objector argues that if 'mutqan' means perfect in all aspects, then no creation qualifies, but if it means merely beneficial, then it does not prove knowledge. The answer redefines 'mutqan' as marvellous craftsmanship that astounds the intellect, thereby preserving the proof's force. Understanding this exchange clarifies the logical basis for using observable design as evidence of the Creator's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection, noting the two possible meanings of 'mutqan' that the objector considers.
The objector presents a dilemma: either 'mutqan' means beneficial in all respects (which is false) or beneficial in some respects (which is too weak to prove knowledge).
2
Analyze the answer's redefinition
Examine how the author redefines 'mutqan' as 'marvellous workmanship and wonderful arrangement that confounds intellects'. Notice that this definition avoids the dilemma by focusing on the quality of design rather than absolute perfection.
The author does not claim that creation is free from defect; rather, its intricate design clearly indicates knowledge.
3
Consider the writing and speech analogy
Reflect on the example of writing and speech: even if one could write more beautifully, the existing writing still indicates knowledge. This illustrates that the proof does not require the highest possible perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المتقن إن أردت به الموافق للمصلحة من جمع الوجوه فممنوع ) إن فعله تعالى متقن ( إذ لا شيء من مفردات العالم ومركباته إلا ويشتمل على مفسدة ما ) ويتضمن ويمكن تصوره على وجه أكمل مما هو عليه ( أو الموافق ) للمصلحة ) من بعض الوجوه فلا يدل على العلم إذ ما من أثر إلا ويمكن أن ينتفع به
والجواب عن الأول: أن المراد بالمتقن ما تشاهده من الصنيع الغريب والترتيب العجيب) الذي تتحير فيه العقول ولا تهتدي إلى كمال ما فيه من المصالح والمنافع ولا شك في دلالته على علم الصانع ) وتوضيحه ما ذكرنا في مثال الكتابة والخطاب إذ لا يشترط في الدلالة على العلم خلوه عن كل خلل واشتماله على كل كمال ) حتى لو أمكن أن يكتب أحسن ( أو يتكلم بأفصح منه لم يدل على علم .
For if it is said: 'Mutqan' – if you mean by it what is in accordance with benefit from all aspects, then it is denied that His action, may He be exalted, is mutqan, for there is no individual thing or composite in the world except that it includes some harm and entails and can be conceived in a more perfect manner than it is; or [if you mean] what is in accordance with benefit from some aspects, then it does not indicate knowledge, for there is no effect except that it can be benefited from. And the answer to the first: that what is meant by 'mutqan' is what you observe of marvellous workmanship and wonderful arrangement that confounds intellects and cannot attain to the perfection of its benefits and interests, and there is no doubt of its indication of the knowledge of the Maker. And its clarification is what we mentioned in the example of writing and speech, for it is not required in the indication of knowledge that it be free from every defect and inclusive of every perfection, such that even if it were possible to write more beautifully or speak more eloquently, that would not prevent it from indicating knowledge.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector challenges the proof by questioning the definition of 'mutqan' (precise). If 'mutqan' means 'in accordance with benefit from all aspects,' then it is false that every act of God is mutqan, because nothing in the world is free from some harm and could be conceived more perfectly. If it means 'in accordance with benefit from some aspects,' then any effect, even fire burning, has benefit, but that does not indicate knowledge. Thus the proof fails. The answer redefines 'mutqan' as marvellous and wonderful workmanship that astounds the intellect, not absolute perfection. Such design clearly indicates knowledge, as shown by the earlier examples of writing and speech; even if one could write better, that does not negate the knowledge shown in the existing writing. The answer emphasises that the indication of knowledge does not require complete flawlessness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Precision in creation must mean absolute perfection with no defect whatsoever.
Correction: The author clarifies that precision means astonishing design that clearly points to knowledge, even if the object is not free from all defects or could be more perfect.

Checkpoint
How does the answer define 'mutqan' to refute the objection?
A) As absolutely free from defect and harm.
B) As what is in accordance with benefit from all aspects.
C) As marvellous workmanship and wonderful arrangement that confounds intellects.
D) As any action that can be benefited from.

Key Takeaway
The proof from precision does not demand absolute flawlessness; it points to the astonishing design that only a wise Creator could produce.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it take for you to deliberately shift your gaze from flaws to the marvellous workmanship?
?
The objector insisted that if everything isn't perfectly beneficial, then the proof fails. In your own life, do you dismiss God's wisdom because you experience harm or lack? Can you see the 'wonderful arrangement' even in trials? Will you choose today to trust that His knowledge is displayed in every detail?
?
The author says that even if something could be more perfect, it still indicates knowledge. Are you waiting for a 'more perfect' sign—better health, easier circumstances, clearer guidance—before you acknowledge God's hand in your life? The Second Objection and Answer: Bees and Spiders
Objection from animal precision and its answer via possible divine creation of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from animal behavior is answered by appealing to God's possible creation of knowledge in animals.

Core Idea
Animals' precise actions don't disprove God's knowledge; God may grant them knowledge.

The second objection to the precision argument comes from the intricate structures bees and spiders produce seemingly without knowledge. In this passage, Sanusi presents the objection and then counters it by asserting that we cannot deny that God may create knowledge in these animals. This answer preserves the proof for God's knowledge while acknowledging the impressive natural behavior we observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify how the objector uses bees and spiders to argue against the proof from precision. Note the geometric details mentioned.
The objector assumes that precision without apparent knowledge refutes the argument.
2
Understand the answer
Notice that the answer does not claim animals actually have knowledge, but only that it is possible (jawaz) for God to create it.
Possibility is sufficient to refute the objection, which assumed impossibility.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the answer only needs to show possibility, not certainty, to refute the objection. This is a logical move.
The proof for God's knowledge is not undermined by counterexamples whose possibility remains open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) نقول أيضاً : (إنه ) أي دليلك على إثبات علمه ( منقوض بفعل النحل لتلك البيوت المسدسة ( المتساوية ( بلا فرجار ومسطر، واختيارها للمسدس لأنه أوسع من ( المثلث و ( المربع) والمخمس (ولا يقع بينها) أي بين المسدسات فرج كما يقع بين المدورات وما سواها من المضلعات ( وهذا ) الذي ذكرناه ( لا يعرفه إلا الحذاق من أهل الهندسة، وكذلك العنكبوت تلك تنسج البيوت ) وتجعل لها سدى ولحمة على تناسب هندسي ( بلا آلة مع أنه لا علم لهما ) بما يصدر عنهما وما يتضمنه من الحكم . ) والجواب عن الثاني: أنا لا نسلم عدم علم النحل والعنكبوت بما يفعله لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فيهما علماً بذلك الفعل
قوله : ( لأنه أوسع من المثلث أي إذا كان مجموع خطوط المسدس مساويا لمجموع خطوط المثلث وأخويه مثلا كان المسدس أوسع قوله : ( ولا يقع بينهما فرج كما بين المدورات وما سواها فإن قلت : هذا الكلام يدل على عدم وقوع الفرج بين المسدسات ووقوعها بين المربعات وما أشار إليه في أول موقف الجوهر من أن الجسم يتركب من المربع بلا خلو الفرج بخلاف سائر المضلعات يدل على عكس ذلك فبينهما تدافع قلت قد ذكرت هناك أن مراده أن المسدسات إذا كانت متفاوتة في المقدار يلزم خلو الفرج وإن لم يلزم إذا كانت متساوية فيه . وأما المربعات فلا يلزم فيها ذلك أصلا وبهذا يتأتى التلفيق بين ادعاء لزوم الفرج في المسدسات هناك. ونفيه هاهنا . وأما التدافع بين كلاميه بالنسبة إلى المربع فدفعه أن يستثنى المربع هاهنا بقرينة ما ذكرناه هناك بقي الكلام في أن المثلث يدخل في عموم ما سواها مع أنه مثل المسدس كما بينا هناك ولا قرينة لاستثنائه
قوله : ( والجواب عن الثاني إلخ) وفي أبكار الأفكار أن هذا الجواب عند من يعترف بكون الحيوانات هي الفاعلة لأفعالها الاختيارية ومن قال : إنها مخلوقة لله تعالى فالجواب عنده أن الإحكام والإتقان ليس مستنداً إلى تلك الحيوانات بل إلى الله سبحانه فيجب أن تكون معلومة لله تعالى لا لها. ولعل المصنف إنما لم يتعرض له لأن الكسب أيضاً يقتضي العلم بالمكسوب القصدي كما دل عليه مثال الكتاب والخطاب أيضاً .
قوله : ( لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى إنما ذكر في التعليل الجواز مع أن ظاهره يدل على أن الإتقان يفيد جواز علم المتقن لا القطع به كما هو المدعى لأن مبنى النقض توهم استحالة
And we also say: 'It — that is, your proof for establishing His knowledge — is refuted by the action of the bees for those hexagonal houses, equal without a compass and ruler, and its preference for the hexagon because it is wider than the triangle and square and pentagon, and there does not occur between them — that is, between the hexagons — a gap as occurs between circles and other polygons. And this that we mentioned is not known except by the skilled among mathematicians. And likewise the spider weaves houses and makes for them warp and woof in a geometric proportion without a tool, while they have no knowledge of what is produced by them and the wisdoms it contains.' And the answer to the second: we do not concede the absence of knowledge in bees and spiders of what they do, because it is possible that God, may He be exalted, creates knowledge in them of that action. His saying: 'because it is wider than the triangle' — that is, if the sum of the sides of the hexagon equals the sum of the sides of the triangle and its two counterparts, for example, then the hexagon is wider. His saying: 'and there does not occur between them a gap as occurs between circles and what is other than them' — for if you say: this statement indicates the non-occurrence of gaps between hexagons and their occurrence between squares, while what he indicated in the first observation on substance — that the body is composed of the square without a gap, unlike other polygons — indicates the opposite, so there is a contradiction between them. I say: I have mentioned there that his intent is that if the hexagons are different in size, then a gap necessarily occurs, though it does not occur if they are equal. As for squares, that does not occur at all. And by this the reconciliation is achieved between the claim of occurrence of gaps in hexagons there and its negation here. As for the contradiction between his two statements regarding the square, its rejection is that the square is excepted here by the indication of what we mentioned there. The discussion remains that the triangle is included in the general 'other than them' while it is like the hexagon as we showed there, and there is no indication for its exception. His saying: 'and the answer to the second, etc.' And in Abkār al-Afkār that this answer is according to one who acknowledges that animals are the agents of their voluntary actions. But one who says they are created by God, then the answer is that the precision and perfection are not attributable to those animals but to God, so they must be known to God, not to them. Perhaps the author did not address this because acquisition (kasb) also requires knowledge of the intended acquired thing, as indicated by the example of writing and speech. His saying: 'because it is possible that God creates' — He only mentioned possibility in the explanation, whereas its apparent meaning indicates that precision yields probability of knowledge, not certainty, as is claimed, because the basis of the refutation is the supposition of impossibility.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage contains the second objection and its answer, along with extensive commentary. The objector argues that the proof from precision is refuted by the actions of bees and spiders, which produce highly geometric and skilful structures without apparent knowledge. The answer denies that bees and spiders lack knowledge; God may create knowledge in them for these actions. The commentator then explains the geometric point about hexagons and squares, resolving an apparent contradiction between statements in different parts of the work. He further notes that some theologians hold that animals are not true agents but instruments of God, so the precision comes from God and indicates His knowledge, not theirs. The final marginal note observes that the answer only claims possibility (jawāz) that God creates knowledge in animals, not certainty, but that suffices to refute the objection because the objection assumes impossibility. The overall point is that the counterexample of animals does not undermine the proof for God's knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The precise actions of animals imply that precision does not require knowledge in the agent.
Correction: The answer shows that the agent (animal) may be granted knowledge by God, so precision can still indicate knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does the answer respond to the objection from bees and spiders?
A) It denies that bees and spiders produce precise structures.
B) It asserts that bees and spiders have voluntary knowledge of their actions.
C) It states that it is possible that God creates knowledge in them for that action.
D) It argues that the precision is not real but apparent.

Key Takeaway
The objection from animal behavior does not undermine the proof for God's knowledge because we cannot rule out that God creates knowledge in them for those actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector sees precision in nature and denies God's knowledge because the agents lack apparent knowledge. But the answer says God can grant them knowledge. In your own life, when you see order and precision—in your body, your circumstances, or the world around you—do you attribute it to mere mechanism, or do you see the hand of God directly or indirectly giving knowledge? What would change in your gratitude and reliance if you truly believed that every intricate detail is either from God's direct action or from knowledge He placed? How can you train your mind to see His role today?
?
The answer in this passage does not prove that animals actually have knowledge; it only says it is possible. Sometimes we demand certainty about God's actions, but here the argument works on mere possibility. Are there areas in your faith where you insist on proof beyond possibility? How does the use of 'jawaz' (possibility) as a valid defense challenge your expectation of how God should reveal Himself? Can you accept that God's ways may be beyond your certainty, and still trust?

Divine Power Implies Divine Knowledge
Proof of God's knowledge based on His power with objections and defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that God's power necessitates His knowledge, the objection from sleeping agents, and the response based on the distinction between little and much skillful action.

Core Idea
Intentional action requires knowledge; necessity limits the scope of the exception.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the second proof for God's knowledge, building on His established power. He argues that a powerful agent who acts with intention must know what he does. An objection is raised: sometimes sleepers perform skillful acts, yet they are not knowing. The response explains that necessity itself distinguishes—allowing a little but not much. This lesson unpacks the proof and the debate around it.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Start by reading the opening of the passage where Sanusi states the proof: God is powerful, and every powerful agent is knowing.
The proof hinges on the definition of a powerful agent as one who acts with intention and choice.
2
Identify the objection
Look for the objection introduced by 'no yuqal': the claim is denied because sleepers and the unaware can produce skillful actions while being considered powerful.
This objection comes from the Mu'tazila and some Ash'aris.
3
Understand the answer
The answer is that necessity distinguishes: it allows a little skillful action from a non-knowing agent, but not much.
The phrase 'الضرورة فارقة' is key. Note that the objection also mentions 'if a little, then much'—the answer cuts this inference.
4
Explore the commentary discussion
Read the commentary from Sharh al-Maqasid about the method of power vs. method of craftsmanship. It addresses a deeper question about an intermediate created being.
This part shows the sophistication of the debate and how the proof is defended.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Conclude by considering why this proof is considered solid, and how the objection actually reinforces the necessity of knowledge for complex actions.
The truth is that will implies knowledge of the intended, regardless of quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلك الثاني : أنه تعالى قادر ، لما مر ، وكل قادر فهو عالم ) لأن القادر هو الذي يفعل بالقصد والاختيار ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع العلم ( لا يقال: ) كون كل قادر عالماً ممنوع إذ (قد يصدر عن النائم والغافل ( مع كونهما قادرين عند المعتزلة وكثير من الأشاعرة ( فعل قليل متقن اتفاقاً وإذا جاز ذلك جاز صدور الكثير عنه لأن حكم الشيء حكم مثله عبرة بالقلة والكثرة ( لأنا نقول : لا نسلم الملازمة إذا الضرورة فارقة ) فإنها تجوز صدور قليل من المتقن عن قادر غير عالم ولا تجوز صدور كتب عنه. وأما من جعل النوم ضداً للقدرة فالسؤال ساقط عنه ( وأما الحكماء فلهم) قوله : ( الثاني أنه تعالى قادر إلخ ) قال في شرح المقاصد : المحققون من المتكلمين على أن طريقة القدرة تيار أوكد وأوثق من طريقة الإتقان والإحكام لأن عليها سؤالاً صعباً. وهو أنه لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى موجوداً يستند إليه تلك الأفعال المتقنة والمحكمة ويكون له العلم والقدرة ودفعه بأن إيجاد مثل ذلك الموجود وإيجاد العلم والقدرة فيه يكون أيضاً فعلاً محكماً بل أحكم فيكون فاعله عالما لا يتم إلا ببيان أنه قادر مختار ، إذ الإيجاب من غير قصد لا يدل على العلم فترجع طريقة الإتقان إلى طريقة القدرة مع أنه كاف في إثبات المطلوب. ويرد عليه ما قيل من أن لنا أن ندفع ذلك السؤال من غير توسيط القدرة بأن يقال : لا يجوز أن الباري تعالى موجودا استند إليه تلك الأفعال لأن ذلك الموجود المستند إليه بطريق الإيجاب إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً والأول باطل لما تبين من حدوث ما سوى ذات الله تعالى وصفاته والثاني أيضا باطل لأن استناد الحادث إلى الموجوب إنما يكون بتسلسل الحوادث، وقد ثبت استحالته وكون هذا الكلام دليلاً على قادريته تعالى لا يستلزم رجوع طريقة الإتقان إلى طريقة القدرة لأنا نثبت بهذا الكلام مطلوبنا، ولا نجعل قادريته تعالى من جملة مقدمات الدليل على أنه لو تم ما ذكره لم يحتج إلى توسيط قوله : لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى إلخ إذ يكفي أن يقال : من كان فعله متقنا إنما يلزم كونه عالما إذا كان صدور الفعل عنه بالقصد والاختيار. قوله : ( لأن القادر هو الذي إلخ) فإن قلت: هذا البيان يعم القدرة على القليل والكثير فكيف يتوجه السؤال ؟ والجواب : قلت السؤال المذكور ينبغي أن يحمل على المعارضة وبهذا يظهر توجيهه لكن الحق أن استلزام الإرادة للعلم بالمراد ضروري فلا فرق بين كون المراد قليلاً وكثيراً كما أشرنا إليه في عاشر مقاصد القدرة من موقف الأعراض. قوله : (فعل قليل متقن أشار الشارح المحقق بزيادة قيد الإتقان إلى ورود السؤال على المسلك الأول أيضاً. قوله : (فالسؤال ساقط عنه ) أي السؤال بالنائم والغافل لأن الغفلة كالنوم في كونه مضاد للقدرة ويحتمل أن يكون مراده إبقاء السؤال بالغافل .
He follows the second method: that He Most High is powerful (qadir), as has passed, and every powerful agent is knowing ('alim) because the powerful agent is one who acts with intention (qasd) and choice (ikhtiyar), and that is inconceivable except with knowledge. It cannot be said: that every powerful agent is knowing is denied, for sometimes a little skillful action occurs from a sleeper or one who is unaware (ghafil), while they are powerful according to the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris, and if that is permissible, then the occurrence of much from him is also permissible because the ruling of a thing is like its equivalent, regardless of whether it is little or much. For we say: we do not accept the implication, for necessity (darura) distinguishes—it permits the occurrence of a little skillful action from a powerful agent without knowledge, but does not permit the occurrence of many such actions from him. As for one who considers sleep to be contrary to power, the objection does not apply to him. As for the philosophers, they have [their own view]. His saying: 'The second: that He is powerful, etc.'—said in Sharh al-Maqasid: The investigators among the theologians hold that the method of power is more solid and reliable than the method of skillfulness and craftsmanship because it faces a difficult question: why is it not permissible that the Creator necessitates a being to which those skillfully made actions are attributed, and that being has knowledge and power? The refutation by saying that bringing such a being into existence and creating knowledge and power in it is itself a skillfully made action, indeed more skillfully made, so its agent must be knowing—that is not complete without establishing that He is a powerful chooser, for necessitation without intention does not indicate knowledge. Thus the method of craftsmanship returns to the method of power, while it suffices to prove the required. And it is rebutted by what is said: we can refute that question without mediation of power by saying: it is not permissible that God exists as a being to which those actions are attributed, because that being, which is attributed to by way of necessitation, is either eternal (qadim) or originated (hadith). The first is invalid because everything other than God's essence and attributes is originated. The second is also invalid because the attribution of the originated to the necessitator occurs only through an infinite chain of events, and its impossibility is established. And this statement being a proof of His power does not entail a return of the method of craftsmanship to the method of power, because we prove our required through this statement, and we do not make His power part of the premises of the proof, so if what he mentioned were complete, he would not need to mediate his saying: 'why is it not permissible that God necessitates...' etc., for it suffices to say: whoever's action is skillfully made only implies that he is knowing if the action issues from him with intention and choice. His saying: 'because the powerful agent is one who...'—for if you say: this explanation applies to power over both little and much, so how does the objection arise? And the answer: I say: the aforementioned objection should be taken as a challenge (mu'arada). By this its direction becomes clear, but the truth is that the implication of will for knowledge of the intended is necessary, so there is no difference whether the intended is little or much, as we indicated in the tenth discourse on power from the Station of Accidents. His saying: 'a little skillful action'—the verifying commentator, by adding the qualification of skillfulness (itqan), indicates that the objection also applies to the first method. His saying: 'the objection does not apply to him'—i.e., the objection from the sleeper and the unaware, because unawareness (ghafla) is like sleep in being contrary to power. It is possible that his intent is to preserve the objection from the unaware.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for God's knowledge: God is powerful (qadir) as established earlier, and every powerful agent is knowing, because acting with intention requires knowledge. An objection is raised: sometimes a sleeping or unaware person performs a skillful action despite being powerful (according to Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris). The answer distinguishes by necessity: little skillful action can occur without knowledge, but much cannot. The commentary from Sharh al-Maqasid discusses the superiority of the method of power over the method of craftsmanship, addressing a potential counterargument about an intermediate being. It also resolves an objection about the scope of power, concluding that the implication of will for knowledge is necessary regardless of quantity. Finally, a note clarifies that adding 'متقن' to the objection makes it applicable also to the first method.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A powerful agent can perform any number of skillful actions without knowledge, as seen in sleepers and the unaware.
Correction: Necessity distinguishes: only a limited amount of skillful action can occur without knowledge; much cannot. Therefore, God, who produces immense skillful creations, must be knowing.

Checkpoint

A) Because the sleeper is not truly powerful
B) Because necessity distinguishes, permitting a little but not much
C) Because the sleeper's action is not really skillful
D) Because the sleeper has knowledge unconsciously

Key Takeaway
God's power implies His knowledge because intentional action requires knowledge, and the objection of limited actions from sleepers is overridden by the necessity that distinguishes between little and much skillful action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform your daily prayers, do you do so with deliberate intention and choice, or are you sometimes like a 'sleeper' going through motions? This lesson teaches that intentional action requires knowledge. If your prayer lacks intention, what does that say about your knowledge of the One you are praying to? Will you today, before your next prayer, consciously set your intention and choice to truly know Him through that act?
?
The objection uses a 'little skillful action' to argue that knowledge is not necessary. But necessity distinguishes—little is permissible, much is not. Think of one small sin you habitually commit, excusing it as 'insignificant.' Does that small action reflect a lack of knowledge of God's majesty? What one small righteous action can you intentionally do today to replace that habit and prove that you know your Lord?
?
The proof hinges on the fact that God acts with intention and choice. If God's every act is intentional, how should that transform your response to trials? Can you truly accept a difficulty from God without questioning His knowledge of your situation? What will you intentionally choose to believe about God's wisdom in your current struggle?

The Second Proof for God's Knowledge: Power and the Sleeping/Heedless Objection
Proof for God's knowledge from power with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof for God's knowledge from power is defended against the objection that sleeping and heedless persons sometimes produce perfect acts.

Core Idea
God's perfect, vast acts require knowledge, while small accidental perfections do not.

This passage presents a clever argument for God's knowledge, but it also anticipates a sharp objection. The response hinges on a distinction between a little and much perfect action, which saves the argument while accounting for apparent counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis linking power and knowledge
Start by reading the thesis: God is powerful, and every powerful being acts with intention and choice, which requires knowledge. Understand why this claim is central to the proof.
The thesis is stated very concisely.
2
Understand the objection using sleeping/heedless
Next, read the objection: the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris argue that sleeping and heedless persons sometimes produce perfect acts without knowledge. If a little is possible, much should also be possible, challenging the thesis.
This objection targets the claim that every powerful being is knowledgeable.
3
See the response: necessity distinguishes quantity
Finally, read the response: the implication is denied because intuitive necessity (al-ḍarūrah) differentiates between a little perfect act and much perfect act. A little can happen accidentally, but much cannot.
The response preserves the argument for God, whose acts are vast and perfectly ordered.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider how the response uses a quantitative distinction to refute the objection. Why is this distinction logically valid? How does it apply specifically to God?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلك الثاني: أنه تعالى قادر، لما مر، وكل قادر فهو عالم لأن القادر هو الذي يفعل بالقصد والاختيار ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع العلم. لا يقال: كون كل قادر عالماً ممنوع إذ قد يصدر عن النائم والغافل مع كونهما قادرين عند المعتزلة وكثير من الأشاعرة فعل قليل متقن اتفاقاً، وإذا جاز ذلك جاز صدور الكثير عنه لأن حكم الشيء حكم مثله عبرة بالقلة والكثرة. لأنا نقول: لا نسلم الملازمة إذا الضرورة فارقة، فإنها تجوز صدور قليل من المتقن عن قادر غير عالم ولا تجوز صدور كثير عنه.
The second way: that He, exalted, is powerful, as previously mentioned, and every powerful being is knowledgeable because the powerful one is the one who acts with intention and choice, and that cannot be conceived except with knowledge. It should not be said: 'That every powerful being is knowledgeable is denied, for a small perfect act occasionally issues from the sleeping and heedless, while they are powerful according to the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris, and if that is permissible, then the issue of much from him is permissible, because the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like, regardless of smallness and greatness.' For we say: 'We do not accept the implication, because necessity is distinguishing; for it allows the issue of a small perfect act from a powerful being who is not knowledgeable, but it does not allow the issue of much from him.'
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's knowledge using the argument from power, alongside an objection and its refutation. It follows a standard kalam structure: thesis, objection, response. The thesis: God is powerful (proved earlier) and every powerful being is knowledgeable because acting by intention and choice requires knowledge. The objection: not every powerful being is knowledgeable, as sleeping and heedless persons sometimes produce perfect acts despite lacking knowledge; if a little is possible, much should be possible. The response: the implication is denied because necessity (i.e., intuitive judgment) distinguishes between a little and much—a small perfect act can accidentally occur from an ignorant powerful agent, but a large quantity of perfectly ordered acts cannot. This preserves the argument for God, whose acts are vast and perfectly ordered.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the sleeping/heedless objection disproves the argument entirely, showing that perfect acts can occur without knowledge.
Correction: The response shows that only a small quantity of perfect acts can occur accidentally; a large quantity (like God's creation) requires knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The proof from power for God's knowledge stands because necessity distinguishes between small accidental perfections and vast ordered acts, which require knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The sleeping and heedless are excused for small mistakes because they lack awareness. But you know that your intentional acts are seen by the All-Knowing. How does that awareness change the way you act when no one is watching?

The Sleeping and Wise Objection
Commentary on the sleeping and wise objections to the proof from perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sleeping objection falls away for those who consider sleep opposite to power, and how the wise claim knowledge from perfect acts.

Core Idea
Sleep opposite to power cancels the objection; wise rely on perfect acts.

This lesson examines two specific objections to the proof from perfection: one based on the state of sleep, and another raised by philosophers. The commentator explains why the first objection is irrelevant for those who see sleep as opposite to power, and how the wise attempt to claim knowledge through their own perfect actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the sleeping objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the two parts: first about those who consider sleep opposite to power, then about the wise.
The phrase 'فالسؤال ساقط عنه' is key.
2
Understand the sleeping objection
The sleeping objection questions whether a sleeping person can be considered powerful. The commentator says if one views sleep as opposite to power, then the sleeping person is not powerful, so the objection does not apply.
3
Analyze the wise objection
The wise (philosophers) claim knowledge of the perfection of actions. The commentator argues that if their perfect acts show knowledge, the same applies to God, but the foundation is blocked otherwise.
The term 'منع المبنى' refers to denying the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من جعل النوم ضداً للقدرة فالسؤال ساقط عنه. وأما الحكماء فلهم علمها وإلا فلا وجه للقطع بعدمه بعد تسليم إمكانه فأشار إلى منع المبنى وادعاء علمهما أيضاً لإتقان فعلهما.
قوله: (الثاني أنه تعالى قادر إلخ) قال في شرح المقاصد: المحققون من المتكلمين على أنه يوجب أن طريقة القدرة تيار أوكد وأوثق من طريقة الإتقان والإحكام لأن عليها سؤالاً صعباً. وهو لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى موجوداً يستند إليه تلك الأفعال المتقنة والمحكمة ويكون له العلم والقدرة ودفعه بأن إيجاد مثل ذلك الموجود وإيجاد العلم والقدرة فيه يكون أيضاً فعلاً محكماً بل أحكم فيكون فاعله عالما لا يتم إلا ببيان أنه قادر مختار، إذ الإيجاب من غير قصد لا يدل على العلم فترجع طريقة الإتقان إلى طريقة القدرة مع أنه كاف في إثبات المطلوب. ويرد عليه ما قيل من أن لنا أن ندفع ذلك السؤال من غير توسيط القدرة بأن يقال: لا يجوز أن الباري تعالى موجودا استند إليه تلك الأفعال لأن ذلك الموجود المستند إليه بطريق الإيجاب إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً والأول باطل لما تبين من حدوث ما سوى ذات الله تعالى وصفاته والثاني أيضا باطل لأن استناد الحادث إلى الموجوب إنما يكون بتسلسل الحوادث، وقد ثبت استحالته وكون هذا الكلام دليلاً على قادريته تعالى لا يستلزم رجوع طريقة الإتقان إلى طريقة القدرة لأنا نثبت بهذا الكلام مطلوبنا، ولا نجعل قادريته تعالى من جملة مقدمات الدليل على أنه لو تم ما ذكره لم يحتج إلى توسيط قوله: لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى إلخ إذ يكفي أن يقال: من كان فعله متقنا إنما يلزم كونه عالما إذا كان صدور الفعل عنه بالقصد والاختيار.
قوله: (لأن القادر هو الذي إلخ) فإن قلت: هذا البيان يعم القدرة على القليل والكثير فكيف يتوجه السؤال؟ والجواب: قلت السؤال المذكور ينبغي أن يحمل على المعارضة وبهذا يظهر توجيهه لكن الحق أن استلزام الإرادة للعلم بالمراد ضروري فلا فرق بين كون المراد قليلاً وكثيراً كما أشرنا إليه في عاشر مقاصد القدرة من موقف الأعراض.
قوله: (فعل قليل متقن أشار الشارح المحقق بزيادة قيد الإتقان إلى ورود السؤال على المسلك الأول أيضاً.
قوله: (فالسؤال ساقط عنه) أي السؤال بالنائم والغافل لأن الغفلة كالنوم في كونه مضاد للقدرة ويحتمل أن يكون مراده إبقاء السؤال بالغافل.
As for one who considers sleep to be opposite to power, the question falls away from him. And as for the wise, they have knowledge of them, otherwise there is no reason to be certain of its non-existence after admitting its possibility; so he pointed to denying the foundation and claiming their knowledge also because of the perfection of their actions.
His saying: (The second, that He is powerful, etc.) He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: The verifiers among the theologians hold that it entails that the method of power is stronger and more firmly grounded than the method of perfection and precision, because there is a difficult question upon it: why is it not permissible that the Creator, exalted, should bring into existence an existent to which those perfect and precise actions are attributed, and which possesses knowledge and power? And its refutation, by saying that bringing into existence such an existent and bringing into existence knowledge and power in it is also a precise action, indeed more precise, so its agent is knowledgeable, cannot be completed except by showing that He is powerful and choosing, because necessitation without intention does not indicate knowledge; so the method of perfection returns to the method of power, even though it is sufficient to prove what is sought. And there is objected to it what has been said: that we can refute that question without intermediating power, by saying: it is not permissible that the Creator, exalted, [bring into existence] an existent to which those actions are attributed because that existent attributed to it by way of necessitation is either eternal or originated; the first is false because it has been shown that everything other than God's essence and attributes is originated; the second is also false because the attribution of an originated thing to the necessitator can only be through an infinite regress of originated things, and its impossibility has been established. And that this statement is a proof of His power, exalted, does not necessitate the return of the method of perfection to the method of power, because we prove our sought by this statement, and we do not make His power, exalted, part of the premises of the proof. For if what he mentioned were complete, he would not need to intermediate his saying: 'Why is it not permissible that the Creator, exalted, [bring into existence] etc.', because it suffices to say: 'One whose act is perfect, it only necessarily follows that he is knowledgeable if the act issues from him with intention and choice.'
His saying: (Because the powerful one is the one who, etc.) If you say: This statement covers power over the little and the much, so how is the question directed? The answer: I say: The mentioned question should be understood as an objection, and by this its direction becomes apparent. But the truth is that the entailment of will for knowledge of the intended is necessary, so there is no difference between the intended being little or much, as we have indicated in the tenth section on power from the station of accidents.
His saying: (A small perfect act) The verifying commentator indicated by adding the condition of perfection that the question also applies to the first way.
His saying: (Then the question falls away from him) i.e., the question by the sleeping and heedless, because heedlessness is like sleep in being opposite to power, and it is possible that his intention is to retain the question by the heedless.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second proof for God's knowledge, addressing further objections and comparing methods. It begins by noting that for those who consider sleep an opposite of power (i.e., negating it), the objection about the sleeping drops. Then it discusses the wise (presumably philosophers) who claim knowledge for themselves; the point is that if their perfect acts indicate knowledge, then the same would apply to God. The commentator then cites Sharh al-Maqasid, arguing that the method from power is stronger than the method from perfection because it faces a difficult question: why not posit an intermediary? The refutation requires showing that the intermediary would itself be a perfect act implying a knowledgeable agent. The commentary then responds to a counter-objection that the question can be deflected without invoking power, by arguing that any intermediary must be either eternal (impossible) or originated (leading to infinite regress). The commentator concludes that the method from power does not reduce to perfection but is self-sufficient. Further comments clarify that the entailment of will for knowledge is necessary regardless of quantity, and that adding 'perfect' to the objection shows it also applies to the first method. Finally, the comment on 'falling away' explains that those who consider sleep opposite to power are immune, but heedlessness may still be an issue.

ضد
قدرة
حكماء
إتقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for whom does the sleeping question fall away?
A) For those who consider sleep opposite to power.
B) For the wise.
C) For those who admit the possibility of knowledge.
D) For the commentator.

Key Takeaway
The sleeping objection is neutralized if sleep is seen as opposite to power; the wise objection is countered by denying their claim or using their own logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that an objection can fall apart when its underlying assumptions are examined. What assumptions do you hold that might be keeping you from fully submitting to God's attributes? Are you willing to let them go?
?
The wise claim knowledge from their perfect actions. Do you base your confidence in your own deeds or in God's perfection? How would your life change if you truly saw every good in yourself as a reflection of God's perfection?
?
If sleep is opposite to power, then a sleeping person cannot be the source of perfect acts. When you feel 'asleep' spiritually, are you still attributing good actions to yourself rather than to God? What would it look like to wake up and see God's power at work?

The Method of Power vs. Perfection
Extended discussion on the superiority of the power method over the perfection method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the method of power is considered stronger than the method of perfection, and how the counter-objection involving an intermediary is refuted.

Core Idea
Method of power is stronger; perfection depends on intention and choice.

This lesson delves into a comparison between two methods of proving God's knowledge: the method from perfection (examining perfect acts) and the method from power (focusing on God's ability to act). The commentator argues that the power method is more robust because the perfection method faces a difficult question about the possibility of an intermediary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two methods
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning of the paragraph to the end. Identify the main claim and the supporting arguments.
The passage is long; take your time.
2
Understand the difficult question
The difficult question is: why can't God create an intermediary being that performs the perfect acts? This questions the inference from perfect action to God's knowledge.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation says the intermediary itself would be a perfect act, implying a knowledgeable creator. But this requires showing the creator is powerful and choosing, not just necessary.
The term 'إيجاب من غير قصد' is key.
4
Examine the counter-objection
A counter-argument claims the intermediary is impossible: it would be either eternal (impossible) or originated (leading to infinite regress). This avoids using the power method.
The counter-argument uses the impossibility of infinite regress.
5
Consider the commentator's response
The commentator argues that the counter-argument does not make the perfection method independent; it still implies God's power indirectly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من جعل النوم ضداً للقدرة فالسؤال ساقط عنه. وأما الحكماء فلهم علمها وإلا فلا وجه للقطع بعدمه بعد تسليم إمكانه فأشار إلى منع المبنى وادعاء علمهما أيضاً لإتقان فعلهما.
قوله: (الثاني أنه تعالى قادر إلخ) قال في شرح المقاصد: المحققون من المتكلمين على أنه يوجب أن طريقة القدرة تيار أوكد وأوثق من طريقة الإتقان والإحكام لأن عليها سؤالاً صعباً. وهو لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى موجوداً يستند إليه تلك الأفعال المتقنة والمحكمة ويكون له العلم والقدرة ودفعه بأن إيجاد مثل ذلك الموجود وإيجاد العلم والقدرة فيه يكون أيضاً فعلاً محكماً بل أحكم فيكون فاعله عالما لا يتم إلا ببيان أنه قادر مختار، إذ الإيجاب من غير قصد لا يدل على العلم فترجع طريقة الإتقان إلى طريقة القدرة مع أنه كاف في إثبات المطلوب. ويرد عليه ما قيل من أن لنا أن ندفع ذلك السؤال من غير توسيط القدرة بأن يقال: لا يجوز أن الباري تعالى موجودا استند إليه تلك الأفعال لأن ذلك الموجود المستند إليه بطريق الإيجاب إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً والأول باطل لما تبين من حدوث ما سوى ذات الله تعالى وصفاته والثاني أيضا باطل لأن استناد الحادث إلى الموجوب إنما يكون بتسلسل الحوادث، وقد ثبت استحالته وكون هذا الكلام دليلاً على قادريته تعالى لا يستلزم رجوع طريقة الإتقان إلى طريقة القدرة لأنا نثبت بهذا الكلام مطلوبنا، ولا نجعل قادريته تعالى من جملة مقدمات الدليل على أنه لو تم ما ذكره لم يحتج إلى توسيط قوله: لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى إلخ إذ يكفي أن يقال: من كان فعله متقنا إنما يلزم كونه عالما إذا كان صدور الفعل عنه بالقصد والاختيار.
قوله: (لأن القادر هو الذي إلخ) فإن قلت: هذا البيان يعم القدرة على القليل والكثير فكيف يتوجه السؤال؟ والجواب: قلت السؤال المذكور ينبغي أن يحمل على المعارضة وبهذا يظهر توجيهه لكن الحق أن استلزام الإرادة للعلم بالمراد ضروري فلا فرق بين كون المراد قليلاً وكثيراً كما أشرنا إليه في عاشر مقاصد القدرة من موقف الأعراض.
قوله: (فعل قليل متقن أشار الشارح المحقق بزيادة قيد الإتقان إلى ورود السؤال على المسلك الأول أيضاً.
قوله: (فالسؤال ساقط عنه) أي السؤال بالنائم والغافل لأن الغفلة كالنوم في كونه مضاد للقدرة ويحتمل أن يكون مراده إبقاء السؤال بالغافل.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second proof for God's knowledge, addressing further objections and comparing methods.
