Knowability of the Infinite: First Response and Its Limitation
Objection and first response to the knowability of the infinite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response to the objection that the infinite is not knowable and why it provides only summary (ijmālī) knowledge, not the detailed knowledge in dispute.

Core Idea
The infinite is knowable only collectively, not in detail.

This passage presents an objection that Allah cannot know the infinite because it lacks distinction. The first response argues that the infinite is conceivable through its attribute of infinity, as a totality. However, a subtle objection shows this only provides summary knowledge, not detailed knowledge — leaving the real dispute unresolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first response
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection (the fourth group's argument) and the first response. Notice how the response pivots to considering the infinite as a totality.
The response introduces the concept of 'considering the infinite qua infinite'.
2
Identify the objection to the first response
Notice the objection that the attribute 'infinity' is accidental to the subject, and the dispute is about the subject itself, not the attribute.
This is a key nuance.
3
Understand the clarification: summary vs detailed knowledge
The author clarifies that the first response provides only summary (ijmālī) knowledge, which is not disputed; the real issue is detailed (tafsīlī) knowledge.
This sets up the need for a second response (not covered here).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : ) من تلك الفرق . ( من قال : إنه لا يعقل غير المتناهي إذ المعقول متميز عن غيره لأن العلم إما نفس التميز أو صفة توجبه ولأنه لو لم يتميز عن غيره لم يكن هو بالمعقولية أولى منه وغير المتناهي غير متميز عن غيره بوجه من الوجوه وإلا لكان له حد وطرف به يتميز وينفصل عن الغير وإذا كان له طرف فليس غير متناه هذا خلف. والجواب من وجهين: الأول إنه معقول من حيث إنه غير متناه يعني أن المجموع من حيث هو مجموع متميز عن غيره بوصف اللاتناهي ومعقول بحسبه وإن كانت آحاده غير متميزة كما ذكرتم وفيه نظر لأن ذلك الوصف أعني اللاتناهي أمر واحد عارض لغير المتناهي وهو غير ما صدق عليه أنه غير متناه والنزاع إنما وقع فيه لأنه الموصوف باللاتناهي لا في ذلك المفهوم العارض لأنه موصوف بالوحدة ولما اتجه أن يقال : المراد أن مجموع ما صدق عليه معقول باعتبار عارضه لا أن عارضه معقول في نفسه، أشار إلى دفعه فقال : وبالجملة فالنزاع في غير المتناهي تفصيلاً لا إجمالاً وما ذكرتم علم إجمالي لا منازعة فيه لأحد كيف ولا بد منه في الحكم بعد تميزه.
The fourth (of those sects): those who said: He does not conceive of the infinite because the conceivable is distinct from other, for knowledge is either distinction itself or a quality that necessitates it, and because if it were not distinct from other, it would not be more entitled to conceivability. The infinite is not distinct from other in any respect; otherwise it would have a limit and boundary by which it is distinguished and separated from other, and if it has a boundary then it is not infinite—this is a contradiction. The answer is from two aspects: the first is that it is conceivable insofar as it is infinite—meaning the totality as such is distinguished from other by the attribute of infinity and is conceivable in that respect, even if its individuals are not distinct as you mentioned. But there is objection to that because that attribute—i.e., infinity—is a single accidental property of the infinite, and it is other than that to which the attribute ‘infinite’ applies; the dispute is about that subject, not about that accidental concept because it is qualified by unity. Since it might be said that what is meant is that the totality of what the attribute applies to is conceivable by virtue of its accident, not that the accident itself is conceivable, he indicated its refutation by saying: in summary, the dispute is about the infinite in detail, not in summary; what you mentioned is summary knowledge, which no one disputes—how could it be otherwise when it is necessary for judgment after its distinction?
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth group's argument that Allah cannot know the infinite because the infinite has no boundary to be distinguished. The author then gives the first of two responses: the infinite is conceivable from the perspective of its attribute of infinity, i.e., the totality is distinguished by that attribute. However, an objector notes that the attribute ‘infinity’ is accidental to the infinite subject, which is the real point of contention. The sharh clarifies that the first response actually provides summary (ijmālī) knowledge of the infinite, which is uncontroversial; the real dispute is about detailed knowledge (tafsīlī). Thus the first response is insufficient, and the author moves on.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the attribute 'infinity' with the infinite subject itself, thinking the dispute is about the attribute.
Correction: The dispute is about the subject (the infinite), not the accidental attribute. The attribute of infinity is a single concept, but the subject is what is being debated.

Key Takeaway

The Infinite: Distinctness Without Limits
Author gives second response and correct answer on distinction of infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the infinite can be known in detail even without boundaries, and how distinction does not require a limit.

Core Idea
Distinction does not require a limit; there are many ways to be distinct.

Sanusi considers a second response to the problem of knowing the infinite, but then sets it aside in favor of a more fundamental insight. The key is to realize that distinctness does not depend on having a physical boundary. This passage clarifies how the infinite can be distinctly conceived without sacrificing coherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the shift from the second response to the correct answer.
2
Identify the three parts
Break the passage into three sections: the second response, the author's objection (implication of detailed knowledge), and the correct answer (rejection of necessary limit).
3
Reflect on 'ways of distinction'
Consider what other ways of distinction could exist besides limits. Think of mathematical or conceptual examples.
This reinforces the core idea that limits are not the only way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : المعقول كل واحد واحد من غير المتناهي وإنه متميز عن غيره من تلك الآحاد ومن غيرها ولا يضر عدم تميز الكل من حيث هو كل ولما لزم من هذا الجواب كون غير المتناهي معلوماً له تعالى تفصيلاً لا إجمالاً على عكس الجواب الأول أعرض عنه أيضاً فقال : والحق أن نقول : لا نسلم أن المعقول المتميز يجب أن يكون له حد ونهاية يمتاز به عن غيره وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو كان تعقله بتميزه وانفصاله عن غيره بالحد والنهاية وإنه ممنوع وجوه التميز لا تنحصر في الحد.
The second (aspect): the conceivable is each one (individual) of the infinite, and it is distinct from other individuals and from others, and the lack of distinction of the whole as such does not harm. Since this answer necessitates that the infinite is known to Him—exalted—in detail, not in summary, contrary to the first answer, he also turned away from it and said: The truth is that we say: we do not grant that the distinct conceivable must have a limit and boundary by which it is distinguished from other; it would only be so if its conception is by its distinction and separation from other by limit and boundary, and that is not granted; the ways of distinction are not confined to limit.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the second response and the correct answer regarding knowledge of the infinite. The second response argues that each individual of the infinite is conceivable and distinct, so detailed knowledge is possible even if the whole is not distinct. However, the author notes that this conflicts with the first response (which gave summary knowledge) and leaves it aside. The correct answer (al-ḥaqq) is that the premise that distinctness requires a limit is false; there are multiple ways of being distinct, not just by boundary. Thus the objection that the infinite cannot be known because it lacks a boundary is refuted.

Key Terms

تفصيلاً
وجوه التميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical limits are the only way things can be distinguished.
Correction: Distinction can be by many ways, not just by limit or boundary.

Checkpoint

A) The infinite is not distinct at all.
B) Distinction requires a limit, but the infinite has no limit.
C) Distinction does not require a limit; there are other ways.
D) The infinite is known only in summary.

Key Takeaway
Distinctness does not require a physical limit; therefore the infinite can be known distinctly.

Allah's Knowledge of Changing Particulars
Refutation of philosophers' denial of divine knowledge of changing particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that Allah does not know changing particulars and the refutation that this implies ignorance, rendering it disbelief.

Core Idea
Denying Allah's knowledge of particulars implies ignorance, not perfection.

The philosophers argued that if Allah knew changing particulars, His knowledge would either become false or change—both deemed impossible. However, this reasoning fails because it would entail ignorance in Allah, which is why the view was declared disbelief. Let's examine the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and the English translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the philosophers' claim, then the refutation.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Identify the scenario with Zayd and the two alternatives (remaining knowledge or change). Understand why they think this leads to impossibility.
They aim to show that divine knowledge cannot encompass changing particulars.
3
Understand the refutation
The author states that denying such knowledge implies ignorance, which is a deficiency in Allah. Therefore the philosophers were declared disbelievers.
This is the key point: the denial itself is problematic.
4
Consider the attempted excuse
The philosophers excuse that perception of corporeal particulars requires corporeal instruments, which is not a perfection for Allah. The author rejects this as unproven for the Necessary Being.
This shows that even their excuse is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : ( منها ( من قال : ( وهم جمهور الفلاسفة ( لا يعلم الجزئيات المتغيرة وإلا فإذا علم مثلاً أن زيداً في الدار الآن ثم خرج زيد فإما أن يبقى علمه بأن زيداً في الدار فيكون جهلاً أو يعلم أنه خرج فيلزم التغير. قوله : ( لا يعلم الجزئيات المتغيرة لا شك أن هذا يستلزم جهل تلك الجزئيات من بعض الوجوه، ولهذا كفرت الفلاسفة فلا وجه لنفي علمه بالجزئيات المتغيرة بناء على لزوم التغير أو الجهل على تقدير العلم بها، وإلا فلا بد من الفرق بين الجهلين حتى يلزم أحدهما دون الآخر وقد يعتذر عنه بأن إدراك الجزئيات الجسمانية من حيث هي جزئيات جسمانية وإن كان كمالاً للموجود إلا أنه ليس كمالاً مطلقاً لأنه نقصاناً يوجب من وجه لاستلزامه التجسم والتركب فلا استحالة في عدم ثبوته للواجب تعالى وأنت خبير بأن هذا الاستلزام بسبب أن إدراك الجزئيات الجسمانية محتاج إلى آلات جسمانية وقد تحققت أن هذا الاحتياج غير مسلم سيما بالنسبة إلى الواجب تعالى.
The fifth (of them): those who said—and they are the majority of philosophers—He does not know changing particulars; otherwise, if He knows for example that Zayd is in the house now then Zayd leaves, either His knowledge that Zayd is in the house remains, which would be ignorance, or He knows that he left, which necessitates change. His statement: (He does not know changing particulars.) There is no doubt that this entails ignorance of those particulars in some respects, and for this reason the philosophers were declared disbelievers. So there is no basis for denying His knowledge of changing particulars due to the implication of change or ignorance assuming knowledge of them. Otherwise, a distinction between the two ignorances must be made so that one follows and not the other. It may be excused by saying that the perception of corporeal particulars insofar as they are corporeal particulars, although it is a perfection for an existent, is not an absolute perfection because it entails a deficiency from one aspect due to its implying corporeality and composition, so there is no impossibility in it not being established for the Necessary—exalted. But you are aware that this implication is due to the perception of corporeal particulars requiring corporeal instruments, and it is established that this need is not granted, especially with respect to the Necessary—exalted.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth group's view, that of most philosophers, that Allah does not know changing particulars. Their argument: if He knows Zayd is in the house now, then when Zayd leaves, either the old knowledge remains (falsehood) or new knowledge occurs (change). The sharh refutes this by stating that denying such knowledge implies ignorance, which is why they were declared disbelievers. The suggestion that such knowledge is not an absolute perfection because it requires corporeal instruments is rejected because that dependency is not proven for the Necessary Being.

Key Terms
الجزئيات المتغيرة
التغير
الجهل
التجسم
آلات جسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine knowledge must change when its object changes, implying mutability in Allah.
Correction: Divine knowledge is eternal and unchanging, encompassing all temporal states without change in the knower.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the philosophers' denial of Allah's knowledge of changing particulars to be disbelief?
A) Because it denies a necessary attribute of Allah.
B) Because it implies ignorance in Allah.
C) Because they reject revelation.
D) Because it leads to atheism.

Key Takeaway
Denying Allah's knowledge of changing particulars is disbelief because it implies ignorance, and the philosophers' excuse concerning corporeality is unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The author says 'you are aware that this need is not granted' regarding corporeal instruments for Allah. Is there any area in your life where you unconsciously limit Allah's attributes to human-like limitations? What would it look like to truly internalize that Allah's knowledge requires no instruments?
?

Are you willing to submit to the paradox that His knowledge is unchanging yet includes every changing event in your life?

The Infinite and Its Distinguishability
The author presents and refutes the objection that the infinite is indistinguishable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the infinite is indistinguishable and the three responses given by kalām scholars, including the position that distinction does not require a physical limit.

Core Idea
God knows the infinite in detail and in summary; distinction needs no limit.

This lesson addresses a classical objection to God's knowledge of the infinite. If the infinite has no end, how can it be known as a distinct thing? The author presents three answers, culminating in the insight that distinction need not involve a physical boundary. Let's examine the argument and its refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and identify the core problem
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the initial objection: that the infinite cannot be distinguished because it would require a limit.
The objection is presented as a conditional: if it had a limit, it would be finite.
2
Analyze the first two responses
The first answer relies on distinction by the attribute of infiniteness itself; the second relies on distinction of individual parts. Notice how each approach attempts to save God's knowledge.
The first answer is criticized because the attribute is accidental; the second implies detailed knowledge.
3
Understand the third and definitive response
The third answer rejects the premise that distinction requires a limit. It argues that modes of distinction are not limited to physical boundaries, thus the infinite can be known without contradiction.
This is the author's chosen position, reconciling both summary and detailed knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : في علمه تعالى ) وغير المتناهي غير متميز عن غيره ) بوجه من الوجوه ) وإلا لكان له حد ) وطرف (به يتميز ) وينفصل ) عن الغير ) وإذا كان له طرف ( فليس غير متناه هذا خلف . والجواب من وجهين: الأول إنه معقول من حيث إنه غير متناه يعني أن المجموع من حيث هو مجموع متميز عن غيره بوصف اللاتناهي ومعقول بحسبه وإن كانت آحاده غير متميزة كما ذكرتم ) وفيه نظر لأن ذلك ( الوصف أعني اللاتناهي ( أمر واحد عارض لغير المتناهي وهو غير ما صدق عليه أنه غير متناه والنزاع إنما وقع فيه ) لأنه الموصوف باللاتناهي لا في ذلك المفهوم العارض لأنه موصوف بالوحدة ولما اتجه أن يقال : المراد أن مجموع ما صدق عليه معقول باعتبار عارضه لا أن عارضه معقول في نفسه، أشار إلى دفعه فقال : ( وبالجملة فالنزاع في غير المتناهي تفصيلاً لا إجمالاً ) وما ذكرتم علم إجمالي لا منازعة فيه لأحد كيف ولا بد منه في الحكم بعد تميزه ( الثاني : المعقول كل واحد واحد ) من غير المتناهي ( وإنه متميز عن غيره ) من تلك الآحاد ومن غيرها ) ولا يضر) في تميز كل واحد واحد ( عدم تميز الكل من حيث هو كل . لأن قوله : ( تفصيلاً لا إجمالاً إلخ يريد أن الجواب الأول كان دالاً على أنه تعالى يعلم غير المتناهي إجمالاً لا تفصيلاً. وهذا الجواب دال على أنه تعالى يعلمه تفصيلاً لا إجمالاً. والحق أنه تعالى يعلمه إجمالاً وتفصيلاً. فلذا أعرض عنهما وأجاب عن أصل الاستدلال بوجه لا محذور فيه . ولما لزم من هذا الجواب كون غير المتناهي معلوماً له تعالى تفصيلاً لا إجمالاً على عكس الجواب الأول أعرض عنه أيضاً فقال : ( والحق أن نقول : لا نسلم أن ) المعقول ( المتميز) يجب أن يكون له حد ونهاية ( يمتاز به عن غيره ( وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو كان تعقله بتميزه ) وانفصاله عن غيره ( بالحد والنهاية وإنه ممنوع ) وجوه التميز لا تنحصر في الحد .
The Fourth Observatory – The Third Objective: On His knowledge. And the infinite is not distinguished from other than it in any way, otherwise it would have a limit and an end by which it is distinguished and separated from other than it; and if it has an end, it is not infinite—this is a contradiction. The answer is from two aspects: The first: it is intellectually conceivable inasmuch as it is infinite, meaning the whole inasmuch as it is a whole is distinguished from other than it by the description of infiniteness and is intellectually conceivable accordingly, even if its individuals are not distinguished as you mentioned. But there is a consideration in this, because that description—namely infiniteness—is a single accident of the infinite, and it is other than that of which it is true that it is infinite, and the dispute is only regarding the latter because it is described by infiniteness, not regarding that accidental concept because it is described by unity. And when it was directed that it be said: the intended meaning is that the totality of that of which it is true is intellectually conceivable with respect to its accident, not that its accident is intellectually conceivable in itself, he pointed to repelling it and said: In short, the dispute is about the infinite in detail, not in summary, and what you mentioned is summary knowledge, which no one disputes—indeed, it is necessary in judgment after its distinction. The second: each single individual of the infinite is intellectually conceivable and is distinguished from other individuals and from other things, and the lack of distinction of the whole as a whole does not harm the distinction of each single individual. Because his saying (in detail not in summary, etc.) means that the first answer indicated that He, exalted, knows the infinite summarily not in detail, and this answer indicates that He knows it in detail not summarily, and the truth is that He knows it both summarily and in detail. Hence he turned away from both and answered the original argument with an aspect that has no problem. And since this answer necessitated that the infinite is known to Him, exalted, in detail not summarily, contrary to the first answer, he also turned away from it and said: The truth is that we say: we do not accept that the intellectually conceivable, distinguished entity must have a limit and an end by which it is distinguished from other things; it is only so if its conceiving is by its distinction and separation from other things by limit and end, and this is rejected—the modes of distinction are not limited to limit.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against God's knowledge of the infinite. The objection states that the infinite cannot be distinguished from anything else, because if it had a limit, it would be finite. The author gives three answers. The first answer: the infinite as a whole is distinguishable by the attribute of infiniteness itself; however, a counter-argument is raised that this attribute is an accident of the infinite and not the subject itself. The author then clarifies that the dispute is about the subject, not the accident, and that the objection mistakenly assumes summary knowledge suffices, while the real issue is detailed distinction. The second answer: each individual of the infinite is distinct and knowable; the indistinctness of the whole does not affect the distinctness of its parts. A marginal note explains that the first answer implies summary knowledge and the second implies detailed knowledge, but the true position is that God knows the infinite both ways. Hence the third answer: the premise that distinction requires a limit is rejected; distinction can occur without a physical boundary.

Key Terms
غير المتناهي
تميز
حد
معقول
إجمالي
تفصيلي
عارض
وصف اللاتناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite cannot be known at all by God because it lacks distinction.
Correction: The passage shows that the infinite can be known both in summary and in detail, and distinction need not involve a physical limit.

Checkpoint
What is the third answer given by the author to the objection that the infinite is indistinguishable?
A) The infinite is known only summarily.
B) Each individual of the infinite is distinct and known.
C) Distinction does not require a limit or end.
D) The infinite has no attribute.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of the infinite encompasses both summary and detail, and distinction can occur without physical limit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God knows every detail of your infinite thoughts and actions. What is one secret you have been hiding from others that you now realize you must confess to God and seek His forgiveness for?
?
The passage teaches that distinction does not require a physical limit. Your soul is distinct from your body without a spatial boundary. How does this truth change your view of your accountability before God? What will you do today to prepare for that meeting?

God's Knowledge of Changing Particulars: The Philosophers' Objection and the Answer of Relations
Philosophers' objection and the answer via relation change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God cannot know changing particulars without implying change or ignorance, and the primary response that change occurs only in relations (الإضافات).

Core Idea
God's knowledge of changing events does not change Him, only His relations change.

The philosophers argued that if God knows a changing particular like Zayd leaving the house, His knowledge would either change (implying change in His essence) or remain the same (implying ignorance). Both are impossible for God. In this passage, the author presents the objection and then provides the key response: change is only in relations, not in God's essence or attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Identify the dilemma: if God knows Zayd in the house and then Zayd leaves, either God's knowledge changes or stays the same. Both entail deficiencies.
This is the core challenge that the answer must resolve.
2
Understand the meaning of 'relations' (الإضافات)
The answer states that change is only in relations. Knowledge is either a pure relation or a real attribute with a relation. In both cases, change occurs in a conceptual notion, not in an existing attribute.
This avoids attributing change to God's essence.
3
See how change in relations avoids change in God's essence
Even if the relation itself changes (like the mirror analogy later), no change occurs in God's eternal attribute itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : ( منها ( من قال : ( وهم جمهور الفلاسفة ( لا يعلم الجزئيات المتغيرة وإلا فإذا علم ( مثلاً ) أن زيداً في الدار الآن ثم خرج زيد ) عنها ( فإما أن يزول ذلك العلم ويعلم أنه ليس في الدار ويبقى ذلك العلم بحاله والأول يوجب التغير في ذاته من صفة إلى أخرى ) والثاني : ) يوجب (الجهل) وكلاهما نقص يجب تنزيهه تعالى قالوا : وكذا لا يعلم الجزئيات المتشكلة وإن لم تكن متغيرة كأجرام الأفلاك الثابتة على أشكالها لأن إدراكها إنما يكون بآلات جسمانية وكذا الحال في الجزئيات المتشكلة المتغيرة إذ قد اجتمع فيها المانعان بخلاف الجزئيات التي ليست متشكلة ولا متغيرة فإنه يعلمها بلا محذور كذاته تعالى وذوات العقول ) والجواب منع لزوم التغير فيه بل التغير إنما هو (في الإضافات ) لأن العلم عندنا إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقية ذات إضافة، فعلى الأول يتغير نفس العلم وعلى الثاني يتغير إضافاته فقط وعلى التقديرين لا يلزم تغير في صفة موجودة بل في مفهوم اعتباري وهو جائز وإدراك المتشكل إنما يحتاج إلى آلة جسمانية. قوله : ( يوجب التغير في ذاته من صفة إلى صفة هذا إنما هو على رأي القائلين بالصفة من الفلاسفة أو كلام إلزامي كما نبهت عليه في الدرس السابق، ولا وجه لحمل الصفة على الحالة لأن بطلان التغير فيها ممنوع عند الفلاسفة أيضا، ويؤيده أن المصنف أجاب بمنع لزوم التغير وصرح الشارح بأنه لا يلزم التغير في صفة موجودة . قوله : ( لأن العلم عندنا إضافة إلخ ) رد هذا الجواب بأنه لو كان علمه تعالى إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقية ذات إضافة بدون الصورة لزم أن لا يكون الباري تعالى عالما بالحوادث قبل وجودها في الخارج إذ لا وجود لها في الخارج وهو ظاهر ولا في العاقل لأن المفروض أن لا صورة ولا تحقق للإضافة سواء كانت إضافة الذات أو إضافة الصفة قبل تحقق المضاف إليه . وأجيب عنه تارة بالتزام الوجود الذهني وإن لم يكن العلم عبارة عنه وقد عرفت في موقف الأعراض ما فيه تمنع أن الإضافة متوقفة على تحقق المضاف إليه بل على امتيازه الذي لا يتوقف على تحققه أصلا والحق أن المقام على القول بعدم تمايز المعدومات كما هو المشهور من رأي أهل السنة لا يخلو عن إشكال واعلم أن الجواب بكون العلم إضافة محضة إنما هو من طرف بعض المتكلمين إذ قد سبق أن علم الله تعالى عند الأشاعرة صفة موجودة قديمة فتجويز كونه أمرا اعتبارياً لا يلائمه ولو أسقط لفظ عندنا لكان أظهر. قوله : (بل) في مفهوم اعتباري وهو (جائز توضيح هذا على ما ذكره الرازي وغيره أن العلم القديم كالمرآة التي تتوارد عليها الصور المختلفة فعند ورودها يتغير تعلق المرآة لا نفسها واعترض عليه بأن اللائح منه أن العلم صفة ينكشف بها الحادث عند حدوثه لا قبله، وهو مذهب أبي الحسين حيث قال : علمه تعالى بالحوادث يحدث عند حدوثها ويزول عند زوالها والقوم قد نسبوه إلى تجهيل الصانع تعالى عن ذلك وإن كان له وجه دفع سيذكره عن قريب. قوله : ( وإدراك المتشكل إنما يحتاج إلخ) هذا كلام تنزلي وإلا فقد صرح الشارح في مباحث القوى الباطنة أن ارتسام ما له امتداد في النفس إنما يستحيل إذا كان حلول الصور فيها
The fifth: among them is he who said (and they are the majority of the philosophers) that He does not know changing particulars; otherwise, if He knows, for example, that Zayd is in the house now, then Zayd leaves it, then either that knowledge ceases and He knows that he is not in the house, or that knowledge remains as it was. The first necessitates change in His essence from one attribute to another, and the second necessitates ignorance, and both are deficiencies from which He, exalted, must be exonerated. They said: and likewise He does not know formed particulars even if they are not changing, like the bodies of the spheres fixed in their forms, because their perception is only by bodily instruments; and the same applies to formed changing particulars, since both impediments are present in them, unlike particulars that are neither formed nor changing, for He knows them without impediment, like His own essence and the essences of intellects. The answer: prevention of the necessity of change in Him; rather, change is only in relations, because knowledge according to us is either a pure relation or a real attribute with relation. On the first, the knowledge itself changes; on the second, only its relations change; and in both cases, change does not occur in an existing attribute but in a conceptual notion, and that is permissible. And perception of the formed only requires a bodily instrument. His saying: (it necessitates change in His essence from attribute to attribute) – this is only according to the view of those who affirm attributes among the philosophers, or it is a binding argument as I pointed out in the previous lesson; there is no reason to interpret the attribute as a state because the invalidity of change in it is also rejected by the philosophers, and it is supported by the fact that the author answered by preventing the necessity of change and the commentator stated that change does not occur in an existing attribute. His saying: (because knowledge according to us is a relation, etc.) – this answer is refuted by saying: if His knowledge, exalted, were a pure relation or a real attribute with relation without the form, it would necessitate that the Creator is not knowledgeable of events before their existence in the external world, since they have no existence in the external world (which is obvious) nor in the intellect, because it is assumed that there is no form and no realization of the relation, whether it be a relation of the essence or of the attribute, before the realization of the related thing. And it was answered sometimes by committing to mental existence, even if knowledge is not an expression of it, and you have known in the position of accidents what is in it. Another answer: it is prevented that the relation depends on the realization of the related thing; rather, it depends on its distinction, which does not depend on its realization at all. The truth is that, on the view of the non-distinction of non-existents as is well-known from the position of the Ahl al-Sunna, this matter is not free from difficulty. And know that the answer that knowledge is a pure relation is only from some theologians, for it has preceded that God's knowledge according to the Ash'aris is an existing eternal attribute, so deeming it a conceptual thing is not compatible with it; if the word 'according to us' were dropped, it would be clearer. His saying: (but in a conceptual notion and it is permissible) – clarification of this according to what al-Razi and others mentioned: that eternal knowledge is like a mirror onto which different images successively appear; when they appear, the relation of the mirror changes, not the mirror itself. It was objected that this implies that knowledge is an attribute by which the event is disclosed at its occurrence, not before it, which is the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn, who said: His knowledge, exalted, of events arises at their occurrence and ceases at their cessation; and the people have attributed to him the making ignorant of the Maker (exalted is He above that), although it has a way of rebuttal which he will mention soon. His saying: (and perception of the formed only requires, etc.) – this is a concessive statement; otherwise, the commentator has stated in the discussions of the internal faculties that the impression of that which has extension in the soul is impossible only if the forms inhere in it.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage addresses the philosophers' argument that God cannot know changing particulars without implying change or ignorance in Him. The objection uses the example of Zayd being in the house: if God knows Zayd is there, then after Zayd leaves, either God's knowledge changes (change in His essence) or remains the same (ignorance). The philosophers also argue that perception of formed (shaped) particulars requires bodily instruments, so God cannot know them either. The answer first denies that change occurs in God; rather, change is only in relations (الإضافات). The author explains that knowledge is either a pure relation or a real attribute with a relation; in both cases, the change is in a conceptual relation, not in any existing attribute. The need for bodily instruments is irrelevant. Extensive commentary follows: the first answer (change in relation) is defended against objections about the nature of knowledge and the problem of knowing non-existent events. Some replied by affirming mental existence or by denying that the relation depends on the existence of the related thing. The comment by al-Razi likens eternal knowledge to a mirror that receives different images without itself changing. This leads to a discussion of whether knowledge is a real entity or a relation, with the Ash'ari position that God's knowledge is an eternal attribute. Finally, the comment on perception of formed particulars is noted as a concessive argument; the true position is that the impossibility of impression of extension in the soul only applies if forms inhere, which does not apply to God's knowledge.

جزئيات متغيرة
تغير
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge of changing events necessitates change in His essence or ignorance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the relations (الإضافات) change, not God's essence or existing attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the primary answer given in this passage, what actually changes when God knows a changing particular like Zayd leaving the house?
A) God's essence changes.
B) God's knowledge attribute changes.
C) Only the relations (الإضافات) change.
D) Nothing changes at all.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection is answered by showing that change occurs only in conceptual relations, not in God's essence or attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's knowledge of your changing circumstances—your successes, failures, sins, and repentance—does not cause Him any deficiency or change, how would that transform your daily reliance on Him? Are you still anxious that your past mistakes define you before God, or do you trust that His knowledge of them is eternally perfect and without need of adjustment? Make a concrete decision this week to replace one anxious thought with the calm certainty that God's relation to your actions is purely relational, not essential.
?
The philosophers claimed that knowledge of changing particulars requires bodily instruments. You might not articulate it that way, but do you sometimes act as if God cannot truly understand your specific, messy, changing life? When you pray, do you assume He already knows your situation in its precise temporality, or do you think you need to inform Him? Commit to praying today with the confidence that His knowledge of your immediate condition is direct and relational, not second-hand.
?
Consider the philosophers' fear that change in God is a deficiency. Are you ever afraid that your own changing states—your moods, doubts, or spiritual ups and downs—diminish your standing with God? The lesson shows that change in relation does not affect God's essence. How might you apply this to yourself: your changing spiritual states are only relational adjustments, not marks of deficiency? Will you stop judging your spiritual worth by your momentary feelings and instead anchor yourself in the immutable reality of God's knowledge of you?

Is God's Knowledge a Real Attribute or a Pure Relation? The Ash'ari Position and Refutations
Commentary on the nature of divine knowledge and the problem of non-existent objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether divine knowledge is a real eternal attribute or a pure relation, and the problems involved in knowing non-existent events.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is an eternal attribute, and change occurs only in its relations, not in the attribute itself.

This passage delves into the nature of God's knowledge. Some theologians argued that divine knowledge is a pure relation, while others (especially Ash'aris) maintain it is a real existing attribute. The commentary explores objections to the relation view, particularly regarding knowledge of non-existent events, and defends the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on the first part of the objection
Note that the objection about change in essence is only valid for those who affirm real attributes (philosophers) or is a binding argument. The author's answer prevents change in an existing attribute.
2
Understand the objection to the relation answer regarding non-existent things
If divine knowledge is a pure relation or a real attribute with relation without a form, then God would not know events before they exist, since there is no form and no relation is realized.
This challenges the adequacy of the relation answer.
3
See the Ash'ari defense: knowledge is an existing attribute, relation depends on distinction not existence
Two responses are given: (1) mental existence, (2) relation depends on distinction (امتياز) not existence. The Ash'ari position is that God's knowledge is an eternal attribute, not a pure relation.
The passage concludes that on the view of non-distinction of non-existents, there is difficulty.

Another answer: it is prevented that the relation depends on the realization of the related thing; rather, it depends on its distinction, which does not depend on its realization at all. The truth is that, on the view of the non-distinction of non-existents as is well-known from the position of the Ahl al-Sunna, this matter is not free from difficulty. And know that the answer that knowledge is a pure relation is only from some theologians, for it has preceded that God's knowledge according to the Ash'aris is an existing eternal attribute, so deeming it a conceptual thing is not compatible with it; if the word 'according to us' were dropped, it would be clearer. His saying: (but in a conceptual notion and it is permissible) – clarification of this according to what al-Razi and others mentioned: that eternal knowledge is like a mirror onto which different images successively appear; when they appear, the relation of the mirror changes, not the mirror itself. It was objected that this implies that knowledge is an attribute by which the event is disclosed at its occurrence, not before it, which is the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn, who said: His knowledge, exalted, of events arises at their occurrence and ceases at their cessation; and the people have attributed to him the making ignorant of the Maker (exalted is He above that), although it has a way of rebuttal which he will mention soon. His saying: (and perception of the formed only requires, etc.) – this is a concessive statement; otherwise, the commentator has stated in the discussions of the internal faculties that the impression of that which has extension in the soul is impossible only if the forms inhere in it.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage addresses the philosophers' argument that God cannot know changing particulars without implying change or ignorance in Him. The objection uses the example of Zayd being in the house: if God knows Zayd is there, then after Zayd leaves, either God's knowledge changes (change in His essence) or remains the same (ignorance). The philosophers also argue that perception of formed (shaped) particulars requires bodily instruments, so God cannot know them either. The answer first denies that change occurs in God; rather, change is only in relations (الإضافات). The author explains that knowledge is either a pure relation or a real attribute with a relation; in both cases, the change is in a conceptual relation, not in any existing attribute. The need for bodily instruments is irrelevant. Extensive commentary follows: the first answer (change in relation) is defended against objections about the nature of knowledge and the problem of knowing non-existent events. Some replied by affirming mental existence or by denying that the relation depends on the existence of the related thing. The comment by al-Razi likens eternal knowledge to a mirror that receives different images without itself changing. This leads to a discussion of whether knowledge is a real entity or a relation, with the Ash'ari position that God's knowledge is an eternal attribute. Finally, the comment on perception of formed particulars is noted as a concessive argument; the true position is that the impossibility of impression of extension in the soul only applies if forms inhere, which does not apply to God's knowledge.

امتياز
معدومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge as a relation means He does not know non-existent events.
Correction: The Ash'ari position maintains that God's knowledge is an eternal attribute, and the relation depends on distinction, not existence, so non-existent events are still known.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position as presented in this passage, what is God's knowledge?
A) A pure relation.
B) A real existing eternal attribute.
C) A state that changes.
D) A mental existence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view holds that God's knowledge is an eternal existing attribute, and change occurs only in its relations, not in the attribute itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging, He has always known every sin you would commit and every excuse you would make. Does that fact make you feel hopelessly exposed, or does it drive you to sincere repentance? What specific action will you take today because of this truth?
?
The theologians debated how God knows non-existent things. But for you, does your belief that God knows your future decisions lead you to live more carefully or more carelessly? Honestly, which assumption governs your daily choices?
?
This lesson admits that the matter is not free from difficulty. When you encounter a theological truth that unsettles your understanding, do you submit to revelation or retreat into comfortable denial? What is one area of your faith where you need to trust God's knowledge more than your own logic?

The Mirror Analogy and Knowledge of Formed Particulars
Explanation of the mirror analogy and discussion of formed particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mirror analogy for divine knowledge and how formed particulars are known without bodily instruments.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is like a mirror that reflects images without changing itself.

In this section, al-Razi uses the analogy of a mirror to explain how God's knowledge remains unchanged while the objects of knowledge change. The passage also addresses the objection about formed (shaped) particulars, stating that perception of such objects requires bodily instruments only in the case of physical perception, not for divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the mirror analogy explanation
Al-Razi likens eternal knowledge to a mirror onto which different images successively appear. The relation of the mirror changes, but not the mirror itself.
This illustrates how change in relations does not affect the knower.
2
Understand the objection raised by Abu al-Husayn
The objection is that this implies knowledge only occurs at the time of the event, which is the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn that knowledge arises and ceases with events.
This is considered a form of making God ignorant, though a rebuttal exists.
3
See the concession about formed particulars
The author notes that the need for bodily instruments is only for physical perception; for divine knowledge, formed particulars can be known without such instruments.
This addresses the second part of the philosophers' objection.

The fifth: among them is he who said (and they are the majority of the philosophers) that He does not know changing particulars; otherwise, if He knows, for example, that Zayd is in the house now, then Zayd leaves it, then either that knowledge ceases and He knows that he is not in the house, or that knowledge remains as it was. The first necessitates change in His essence from one attribute to another, and the second necessitates ignorance, and both are deficiencies from which He, exalted, must be exonerated. They said: and likewise He does not know formed particulars even if they are not changing, like the bodies of the spheres fixed in their forms, because their perception is only by bodily instruments; and the same applies to formed changing particulars, since both impediments are present in them, unlike particulars that are neither formed nor changing, for He knows them without impediment, like His own essence and the essences of intellects. The answer: prevention of the necessity of change in Him; rather, change is only in relations, because knowledge according to us is either a pure relation or a real attribute with relation. On the first, the knowledge itself changes; on the second, only its relations change; and in both cases, change does not occur in an existing attribute but in a conceptual notion, and that is permissible. And perception of the formed only requires a bodily instrument. His saying: (but in a conceptual notion and it is permissible) – clarification of this according to what al-Razi and others mentioned: that eternal knowledge is like a mirror onto which different images successively appear; when they appear, the relation of the mirror changes, not the mirror itself. This passage addresses the philosophers' argument that God cannot know changing particulars without implying change or ignorance in Him. intermediate
6 min
Change in God's Knowledge Is Only in Relations
Objection and answer about God's knowledge of changing particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical dilemma that God's knowledge of changing particulars seems to necessitate change in His essence, and the kalam answer that change occurs only in the relations of knowledge, not in the essence.

Core Idea
Change in God's knowledge is only in relations, not in His essence.

This passage presents a key objection against God's knowledge of changing particulars: if God knows something that changes, His knowledge must either cease or become false, implying change in His essence. The answer, based on the nature of knowledge as relation, shows that change only affects the relation, not an attribute. Understanding this objection and answer is foundational for the Islamic theological conception of God's omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the dilemma: two impossible options if God knows changing things.
Notice the two horns: either knowledge ceases (change) or remains (ignorance).
2
Identify the two possibilities in the objection
The objection argues that knowledge of changing particulars forces either change in God's essence or ignorance. Find the exact wording for each.
The text uses 'first necessitates change... second necessitates ignorance'.
3
Analyze the answer: change in relations only
The answer denies that change applies to the essence. Instead, change occurs only in the relation (إضافة) because knowledge is either a pure relation or a real attribute with relation.
On both views, no existing attribute changes—only a conceptual notion changes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: it preserves God's immutability while affirming knowledge of changing particulars.
This is a classic kalam defense of divine omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنها ( فإما أن يزول ذلك العلم ويعلم أنه ليس في الدار ويبقى ذلك العلم بحاله والأول يوجب التغير في ذاته من صفة إلى أخرى ) والثاني : ) يوجب (الجهل) وكلاهما نقص يجب تنزيهه تعالى قالوا : وكذا لا يعلم الجزئيات المتشكلة وإن لم تكن متغيرة كأجرام الأفلاك الثابتة على أشكالها لأن إدراكها إنما يكون بآلات جسمانية وكذا الحال في الجزئيات المتشكلة المتغيرة إذ قد اجتمع فيها المانعان بخلاف الجزئيات التي ليست متشكلة ولا متغيرة فإنه يعلمها بلا محذور كذاته تعالى وذوات العقول ) والجواب منع لزوم التغير فيه بل التغير إنما هو (في الإضافات ) لأن العلم عندنا إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقية ذات إضافة، فعلى الأول يتغير نفس العلم وعلى الثاني يتغير إضافاته فقط وعلى التقديرين لا يلزم تغير في صفة موجودة بل في مفهوم اعتباري وهو جائز

قوله : ( يوجب التغير في ذاته من صفة إلى صفة هذا إنما هو على رأي القائلين بالصفة من الفلاسفة أو كلام إلزامي كما نبهت عليه في الدرس السابق، ولا وجه لحمل الصفة على الحالة لأن بطلان التغير فيها ممنوع عند الفلاسفة أيضا، ويؤيده أن المصنف أجاب بمنع لزوم التغير وصرح الشارح بأنه لا يلزم التغير في صفة موجودة . قوله : ( لأن العلم عندنا إضافة إلخ ) رد هذا الجواب بأنه لو كان علمه تعالى إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقية ذات إضافة بدون الصورة لزم أن لا يكون الباري تعالى عالما بالحوادث قبل وجودها في الخارج إذ لا وجود لها في الخارج وهو ظاهر ولا في العاقل لأن المفروض أن لا صورة ولا تحقق للإضافة سواء كانت إضافة الذات أو إضافة الصفة قبل تحقق المضاف إليه . وأجيب عنه تارة بالتزام الوجود الذهني وإن لم يكن العلم عبارة عنه وقد عرفت في موقف الأعراض ما فيه تمنع أن الإضافة متوقفة على تحقق المضاف إليه بل على امتيازه الذي لا يتوقف على تحققه أصلا والحق أن المقام على القول بعدم تمايز المعدومات كما هو المشهور من رأي أهل السنة لا يخلو عن إشكال واعلم أن الجواب بكون العلم إضافة محضة إنما هو من طرف بعض المتكلمين إذ قد سبق أن علم الله تعالى عند الأشاعرة صفة موجودة قديمة فتجويز كونه أمرا اعتبارياً لا يلائمه ولو أسقط لفظ عندنا لكان أظهر.
Concerning it: (Either that knowledge ceases and He knows that it is not in the house, and that knowledge remains in its state; the first necessitates change in His essence from one attribute to another, and the second necessitates ignorance, and both are deficiencies from which He must be exalted.) They said: And similarly, He does not know formed particulars even if they are not changing, like the bodies of the fixed spheres in their forms, because their perception is only by physical instruments. And so it is with changing formed particulars, since both obstacles are present in them. Unlike particulars that are neither formed nor changing, He knows them without difficulty, like His own essence and the essences of intellects. The answer: denial of the necessity of change in that, rather change is only in relations. Because knowledge, according to us, is a pure relation or a real attribute possessing relation. On the first view, the knowledge itself changes; on the second, only its relations change. In both cases, no change occurs in an existing attribute, but only in a conceptual notion, and that is permissible.

His statement: ('It necessitates change in His essence from attribute to attribute' – this is only according to the view of those who affirm attributes among the philosophers, or it is an argument of commitment, as I alerted in the previous lesson. There is no basis for carrying the attribute to mean state, because the invalidity of change in it is also denied by the philosophers. It is supported that the author answered by denying the necessity of change, and the commentator explicitly stated that no change occurs in an existing attribute.) His statement: ('Because knowledge according to us is a relation' etc.) refuted this answer by saying that if His knowledge were a pure relation or a real attribute having relation without the form, then the Exalted would not know events before their existence in the external world, since they have no external existence – that is clear – nor in the intellect, because there is no form and no realization of the relation, whether it is a relation of the essence or a relation of the attribute, before the realization of the object of relation. It was answered sometimes by affirming mental existence, even if knowledge is not an expression of it – and you have known in the Station of Accidents what is in it. [Another answer:] Denial that the relation is dependent on the realization of the object of relation, but rather on its distinctness, which is not dependent on its realization at all. The truth is that the matter, according to the well-known view of the Ahl al-Sunna that non-existents are not distinct, is not free from difficulty. And know that the answer that knowledge is a pure relation is only from some theologians, since it has preceded that God's knowledge according to the Ash'ariyya is an eternal existing attribute, so allowing it to be a conceptual matter is inconsistent. If the word 'according to us' were dropped, it would be clearer.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning whether God's knowledge of changing particulars entails change in His essence. The objection states that if God knows a particular that changes, either His knowledge ceases (necessitating change in His essence from one attribute to another) or the knowledge remains while the object is gone (necessitating ignorance). Both are impossible. The answer denies that knowledge necessitates change in essence, because knowledge is either a pure relation or a real attribute with relation. In either case, change occurs only in the relation, not in an existing attribute. The subsequent commentary explains that the objection is based on a certain philosophical view of attributes. A further objection is raised: if knowledge is a pure relation without a form (صورة), then God would not know things before they exist. Two answers are given: one appeals to mental existence; another denies that a relation depends on the existence of its object, only on its distinctness. The passage concludes that the view of knowledge as a pure relation is not fully consistent with Ash'ari doctrine, where God's knowledge is an eternal existing attribute.

إضافة
صفة حقيقية
مفهوم اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That change in relation implies change in God's essence.
Correction: The passage shows that change in relation does not affect the essence; only a conceptual notion changes.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what changes when God knows a changing particular?
A) His essence
B) His attribute of knowledge
C) Only the relation (إضافة)
D) Nothing changes

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of changing particulars involves only a change in relation, not in His essence or attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin or repent, do you believe God's knowledge of you changes? Does that make you feel as if you are constantly disappointing a God who expected something different? How would your relationship with Him change if you truly internalized that only the relation changes, not His essence? This week, when you struggle with sin, speak to God as one who knows you perfectly yet unchangingly, and ask for the strength to return to Him without fear of altering His nature.
?
You likely worry about what others think of you because their opinion changes with your actions. But God's knowledge of you does not change in His essence. Are you more concerned with human opinions that change or with God's unchanging knowledge? Today, find one moment where you can act solely for God's gaze, ignoring how it might shift others' views of you.
?
The objection in this lesson implies that if God's knowledge changed, He would be deficient. Do you sometimes live as if God's knowledge is deficient—as if He is learning about your mistakes or being surprised by your choices? What would it look like to trust that God's knowledge of all your future failures and successes is already perfect and unchanging? Commit this week to confessing one sin you have been hiding, precisely because you know God already sees it and His essence is unchanged.

How God Knows Non-Existent Things
Further objection and answers about God's knowledge of non-existent things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if God's knowledge is a pure relation without a form, then He cannot know things before they exist, and the responses using mental existence or relation based on distinctness.

Core Idea
God knows non-existent things through mental existence or relation to their distinctness.

The previous answer that knowledge is a pure relation raises a further difficulty: how can God know things that do not yet exist? This passage presents two responses: one appealing to mental existence, another affirming that relation depends only on the distinctness of the object, not its actual existence. Both aim to preserve God's knowledge of future contingents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and responses
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the objection that if knowledge is a pure relation without a form (صورة), God cannot know events before they exist.
The objection notes no external existence and no mental existence either.
2
Identify the first answer: mental existence
The first response affirms that non-existent things have mental existence (الوجود الذهني), which serves as the object of relation.
This is a common Ash'ari solution.
3
Identify the second answer: relation depends on distinctness
The second response denies that relation depends on the existence of the object; it only requires its distinctness (التميز), which does not require actual existence.
This avoids the need for mental existence.
4
Consider the concluding note
The passage notes that the pure-relation view is not fully consistent with Ash'ari doctrine, where God's knowledge is an eternal existing attribute.
This shows the complexity of kalam discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنها ( فإما أن يزول ذلك العلم ويعلم أنه ليس في الدار ويبقى ذلك العلم بحاله والأول يوجب التغير في ذاته من صفة إلى أخرى ) والثاني : ) يوجب (الجهل) وكلاهما نقص يجب تنزيهه تعالى قالوا : وكذا لا يعلم الجزئيات المتشكلة وإن لم تكن متغيرة كأجرام الأفلاك الثابتة على أشكالها لأن إدراكها إنما يكون بآلات جسمانية وكذا الحال في الجزئيات المتشكلة المتغيرة إذ قد اجتمع فيها المانعان بخلاف الجزئيات التي ليست متشكلة ولا متغيرة فإنه يعلمها بلا محذور كذاته تعالى وذوات العقول ) والجواب منع لزوم التغير فيه بل التغير إنما هو (في الإضافات ) لأن العلم عندنا إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقية ذات إضافة، فعلى الأول يتغير نفس العلم وعلى الثاني يتغير إضافاته فقط وعلى التقديرين لا يلزم تغير في صفة موجودة بل في مفهوم اعتباري وهو جائز

قوله : ( يوجب التغير في ذاته من صفة إلى صفة هذا إنما هو على رأي القائلين بالصفة من الفلاسفة أو كلام إلزامي كما نبهت عليه في الدرس السابق، ولا وجه لحمل الصفة على الحالة لأن بطلان التغير فيها ممنوع عند الفلاسفة أيضا، ويؤيده أن المصنف أجاب بمنع لزوم التغير وصرح الشارح بأنه لا يلزم التغير في صفة موجودة . قوله : ( لأن العلم عندنا إضافة إلخ ) رد هذا الجواب بأنه لو كان علمه تعالى إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقية ذات إضافة بدون الصورة لزم أن لا يكون الباري تعالى عالما بالحوادث قبل وجودها في الخارج إذ لا وجود لها في الخارج وهو ظاهر ولا في العاقل لأن المفروض أن لا صورة ولا تحقق للإضافة سواء كانت إضافة الذات أو إضافة الصفة قبل تحقق المضاف إليه . وأجيب عنه تارة بالتزام الوجود الذهني وإن لم يكن العلم عبارة عنه وقد عرفت في موقف الأعراض ما فيه تمنع أن الإضافة متوقفة على تحقق المضاف إليه بل على امتيازه الذي لا يتوقف على تحققه أصلا والحق أن المقام على القول بعدم تمايز المعدومات كما هو المشهور من رأي أهل السنة لا يخلو عن إشكال واعلم أن الجواب بكون العلم إضافة محضة إنما هو من طرف بعض المتكلمين إذ قد سبق أن علم الله تعالى عند الأشاعرة صفة موجودة قديمة فتجويز كونه أمرا اعتبارياً لا يلائمه ولو أسقط لفظ عندنا لكان أظهر.
Concerning it: (Either that knowledge ceases and He knows that it is not in the house, and that knowledge remains in its state; the first necessitates change in His essence from one attribute to another, and the second necessitates ignorance, and both are deficiencies from which He must be exalted.) They said: And similarly, He does not know formed particulars even if they are not changing, like the bodies of the fixed spheres in their forms, because their perception is only by physical instruments. And so it is with changing formed particulars, since both obstacles are present in them. Unlike particulars that are neither formed nor changing, He knows them without difficulty, like His own essence and the essences of intellects. The answer: denial of the necessity of change in that, rather change is only in relations. Because knowledge, according to us, is a pure relation or a real attribute possessing relation. On the first view, the knowledge itself changes; on the second, only its relations change. In both cases, no change occurs in an existing attribute, but only in a conceptual notion, and that is permissible.

His statement: ('It necessitates change in His essence from attribute to attribute' – this is only according to the view of those who affirm attributes among the philosophers, or it is an argument of commitment, as I alerted in the previous lesson. There is no basis for carrying the attribute to mean state, because the invalidity of change in it is also denied by the philosophers. It is supported that the author answered by denying the necessity of change, and the commentator explicitly stated that no change occurs in an existing attribute.) His statement: ('Because knowledge according to us is a relation' etc.) refuted this answer by saying that if His knowledge were a pure relation or a real attribute having relation without the form, then the Exalted would not know events before their existence in the external world, since they have no external existence – that is clear – nor in the intellect, because there is no form and no realization of the relation, whether it is a relation of the essence or a relation of the attribute, before the realization of the object of relation. It was answered sometimes by affirming mental existence, even if knowledge is not an expression of it – and you have known in the Station of Accidents what is in it. [Another answer:] Denial that the relation is dependent on the realization of the object of relation, but rather on its distinctness, which is not dependent on its realization at all. The truth is that the matter, according to the well-known view of the Ahl al-Sunna that non-existents are not distinct, is not free from difficulty. And know that the answer that knowledge is a pure relation is only from some theologians, since it has preceded that God's knowledge according to the Ash'ariyya is an eternal existing attribute, so allowing it to be a conceptual matter is inconsistent. If the word 'according to us' were dropped, it would be clearer.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning whether God's knowledge of changing particulars entails change in His essence. The objection states that if God knows a particular that changes, either His knowledge ceases (necessitating change in His essence from one attribute to another) or the knowledge remains while the object is gone (necessitating ignorance). Both are impossible. The answer denies that knowledge necessitates change in essence, because knowledge is either a pure relation or a real attribute with relation. In either case, change occurs only in the relation, not in an existing attribute. The subsequent commentary explains that the objection is based on a certain philosophical view of attributes. A further objection is raised: if knowledge is a pure relation without a form (صورة), then God would not know things before they exist. Two answers are given: one appeals to mental existence; another denies that a relation depends on the existence of its object, only on its distinctness. The passage concludes that the view of knowledge as a pure relation is not fully consistent with Ash'ari doctrine, where God's knowledge is an eternal existing attribute.

إضافة
صفة موجودة

Correction: God can know non-existent things through mental existence or by relation to their distinctness.

God's Knowledge of Extended Things: No Physical Instrument Needed
Discussion of whether God needs a physical instrument for perceiving extended things, with answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God does not need a physical instrument to perceive extended (mushakkala) particulars, because His knowledge is a pure relation, not a form inhering in a soul.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of extended things is relational, not formal, so no physical instrument is needed.

This passage addresses an important objection in kalam: if perception of extended things requires a physical instrument, how can God know extended particulars without a body? The commentator offers a concessive explanation that clarifies the issue. The key distinction is whether knowledge involves a form (صورة) inhering in the soul or is a pure relation (إضافة محضة).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concessive tone: 'هذا كلام تنزلي' indicates the argument is presented for the sake of discussion.
The passage is short but dense with technical terms.
2
Understand the key distinction
Identify the two models of knowledge: (1) knowledge as acquisition of a form in the soul, which would require a physical instrument for extended things, and (2) knowledge as a pure relation or real attribute with relation, which does not require a physical instrument.
This distinction is crucial for the argument.
3
Apply to divine knowledge
Reflect on how this distinction applies to God. Since God's knowledge is not a form but a relation (إضافة), He can know extended things without any physical instrument.
This preserves divine simplicity and transcendence.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the checkpoint question. It directly asks when a physical instrument is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإدراك المتشكل إنما يحتاج إلخ) هذا كلام تنزلي وإلا فقد صرح الشارح في مباحث القوى الباطنة أن ارتسام ما له امتداد في النفس إنما يستحيل إذا كان حلول الصور فيها جسمانية إذا كان العلم حصول الصورة وأما إذا كان إضافة محضة أو صفة حقيقة ذات إضافة بدون الصورة فلا حاجة إليها.
His statement: 'And perception of the formed [extended] only requires etc.' This is a concessive statement; otherwise, the commentator has stated in the discussions of the internal faculties that the impression of what has extension in the soul is only impossible if it is a solution of forms in it as a body, if knowledge is the acquisition of the form. But if it is a pure relation or a real attribute with relation without the form, then there is no need for it.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a classification and answer regarding whether God's knowledge of extended (mushakkala) particulars requires a physical instrument. The objection is that perception of extended things needs a physical instrument. The answer explains that this is a concessive statement; actually, the impossibility of an extended form in the soul applies only if knowledge involves the form (صورة) residing in the soul as a bodily inherence. However, if knowledge is a pure relation or a real attribute with relation, no such form is needed, and thus no physical instrument is required. This aligns with the view that God's knowledge is not a form but a relation.

Correction: God does not need a physical instrument because His knowledge of extended particulars is a pure relation or attribute with relation, not a form inhering in the soul.

God's Knowledge of Future and Past is One Continuous Knowledge
Response that God's knowledge of something's future existence and its past existence is a single continuous knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge of a thing's future existence and its past existence is the same, and how this answers the objection that knowledge of changing particulars would change.

Core Idea
God's knowledge never changes because He never forgets.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the joint answer of the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya to a key objection against God's knowledge of particulars. The objection was that if knowledge corresponds to its object, then knowledge of something before and after it exists would be different. The response is that God's knowledge is continuous and unchanging, unlike human knowledge which is interrupted by forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the use of the example of Zayd entering the city.
2
Understand the example
The passage uses the example of knowing that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow. When tomorrow comes, the same knowledge tells you he has entered, provided the knowledge is continuous.
This illustrates the core idea of continuous knowledge.
3
Contrast human and divine knowledge
Humans need new knowledge because they forget. God has no forgetfulness, so His knowledge does not need renewal.
This is the key distinction that resolves the objection.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that change in the known (from non-existence to existence) does not cause change in God's knowledge, because His knowledge is one continuous knowledge.
This defends God's omniscience and immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجاب عنه مشايخ المعتزلة وكثير من الأشاعرة بأن العلم بأنه وجد الشيء والعلم بأنه سيوجد واحد، فإن من علم أن زيداً سيدخل البلد غداً فعند حصول الغد يعلم بهذا العلم أنه دخل البلد الآن إذا كان علمه هذا مستمراً بلا غفلة مزيلة له، وإنما يحتاج أحدنا إلى علم آخر متجدد يعلم به أنه دخل الآن لطريان الغفلة الأول، والباري تعالى يمتنع عليه الغفلة فكان علمه بأنه وجد عين علمه بأنه سيوجد، فلا يلزم من تغير المعلوم من عدم إلى وجود تغير في علمه
And the masters of the Mu'tazila and many of the Ash'ariyya answered that the knowledge that the thing has existed and the knowledge that it will exist are one. For whoever knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, when tomorrow comes, he knows by this same knowledge that he has entered the city now, if his knowledge is continuous without forgetfulness removing it. We [humans] only need another renewed knowledge to know that he entered now because of the occurrence of the first forgetfulness. But the Exalted One is impossible to have forgetfulness, so His knowledge that it has existed is the very same as His knowledge that it will exist. Thus, from the change of the known from non-existence to existence, no change in His knowledge follows.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the objection that knowledge of a changing particular would change. The Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya argue that God's knowledge of a thing's future existence and its past existence is one continuous knowledge. The example given: if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, then when tomorrow comes, the same knowledge tells him that Zayd has entered now, provided the knowledge is continuous without forgetfulness. Humans need a new knowledge because they forget, but God never forgets. Therefore, His knowledge that something will exist is identical to His knowledge that it has existed. Hence, the change in the known (from non-existence to existence) does not cause any change in His knowledge.

Key Terms
علم مستمر
غفلة
تجدد العلم
بارى تعالى
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of future and past are necessarily different types of knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that if knowledge is continuous and free from forgetfulness, knowledge of future and past can be identical. God, being free from forgetfulness, has one continuous knowledge for both.

Checkpoint

D) Because time passes

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of an event before and after it occurs is the same continuous knowledge, because God never forgets.

God's knowledge is timeless
Explanation that God's knowledge encompasses all times equally, without being bound by temporal distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge is not temporal and why past, present, and future do not apply to Him.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless, encompassing all times equally.

This passage draws from the philosophers to explain that God's knowledge is not like ours. Human knowledge is tied to time, but God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all moments uniformly. The author explains why temporal distinctions like past and future have no meaning for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the philosophical terminology used.
2
Understand temporal vs. timeless
Identify the key contrast: human knowledge is temporal (occurring in time), while divine knowledge is not specified to any time.
The terms 'temporal knowledge' (علم زماني) and 'present/past/future' are central.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy with space: just as God is not spatial, so His relation to all places is equal; similarly, He is not temporal.
4
Connect to broader theology
Think how this understanding of God's knowledge supports His omniscience and immutability.
This lesson reinforces the doctrine of God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الذي ذكروه مأخوذ من قول الحكماء : علمه تعالى ليس علماً زمانياً أي واقعاً في زمان كعلم أحدنا بالحوادث المختصة بأزمنة متعينة فإنه واقع في زمان مخصوص فما حدث منها في ذلك الزمان كان حاضرا عنده وما حدث قبله أو بعده كان ماضياً أو مستقبلاً، وأما علمه تعالى فلا اختصاص له بزمان أصلاً فلا يكون ثمة حال وماض ومستقبل فإن هذه صفات عارضة للزمان بالقياس إلى ما يختص بجزء منه إذ الحال معناه زمان حكمي هذا والماضي زمان هو قبل زمان حكمي هذا والمستقبل زمان هو بعد زمان حكمي هذا فمن كان علمه أزلياً محيطاً بالزمان وغير محتاج في وجوده إليه وغير مختص بجزء معين من أجزائه لا يتصور في حقه حال ولا ماض ولا مستقبل فالله سبحانه وتعالى عالم عندهم بجميع الحوادث الجزئية وأزمنتها الواقعة هي فيها لا من حيث أن بعضها واقع الآن وبعضها في الماضي وبعضها في المستقبل فإن العلم بها من هذه الحيثية يتغير بل يعلمها علماً متعالياً عن الدخول تحت الأزمنة ثابتاً أبد الدهر، وتوضيحه أنه تعالى لما لم يكن مكانياً كانت نسبته إلى جميع الأمكنة على سواء فليس فيها بالقياس إليه كحلول الأعراض في محالها وهو ممنوع وتلخيصه أن كون الشيء ذا مقدار إنما هو بحسب الوجود الخارجي وانطباعه في النفس بحسب الوجود الذهني فلا محذور ولا حاجة إلى آلة جسمانية.
And this that they mentioned is taken from the saying of the philosophers: His knowledge, exalted be He, is not temporal knowledge, i.e., occurring in time like the knowledge of any one of us of events specific to determined times, for it occurs in a particular time. What occurs in that time is present to him, and what occurred before or after is past or future. But His knowledge has no specification to any time at all, so there is no present, past, or future. For these are attributes accidental to time in relation to what is specific to a part of it. The meaning of present is 'this hypothetical time', the past is the time before this hypothetical time, and the future is the time after this hypothetical time. So one whose knowledge is eternal, encompassing time, not needing time for its existence, and not specific to any particular part of time—in His regard, no present, past, or future is conceivable. Thus God, may He be blessed and exalted, according to them knows all particular events and their times in which they occur, not from the aspect that some are occurring now, some in the past, and some in the future—for knowledge from that aspect would change. Rather, He knows them with a knowledge transcending entering under times, fixed forever. And its clarification is that since He is not spatial, His relation to all places is equal, so there is no spatial relation to Him like the inherence of accidents in their loci—and that is impossible. And the summary is that a thing being extended is only according to external existence, and its impression in the soul is according to mental existence, so there is no difficulty and no need for a physical instrument.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the philosophical basis for the Mu'tazila/Ash'ari answer, drawn from the philosophers. It states that God's knowledge is not temporal; it does not occur in time as human knowledge does. Present, past, and future are defined relative to a hypothetical 'now' in time. Since God's knowledge is eternal, encompasses all time, and is not dependent on time, these temporal distinctions do not apply. He knows all events and their times timelessly, not as occurring now or later. The clarification uses an analogy with space: just as God is not spatial, so His relation to all places is equal; similarly, He is not temporal. The final part ties back to the earlier discussion of extended things, concluding that extension is only external, and in mental existence there is no need for a physical instrument.

Key Terms
علم زماني
حال
ماض
مستقبل
زمان حكمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God knows events in a temporal sequence.
Correction: God's knowledge is timeless; He knows all events without past, present, or future.

Checkpoint
Why is there no past or future for God?
A) Because He forgets
B) Because His knowledge is not bound by time
C) Because He only knows the present
D) Because time does not exist

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge encompasses all times equally, free from temporal limitations.

?

?
Human knowledge changes; divine knowledge does not. When you think about the times you have doubted God's love because He 'knew' your failures in advance, you are projecting human temporality onto God. How would your prayer life change if you stopped trying to inform God of your needs and instead thanked Him for already knowing them and providing for you before you asked? Name one specific need you will bring to Him tonight, acknowledging His timeless provision.
