Defending Divine Immutability: The Rejoinder on Knowledge of Non-Existence
Objection and rejoinder on change in divine knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rejoinder defends the Mu'tazila answer against the charge that divine knowledge would change when something transitions from non-existence to existence.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge of non-existence persists as knowledge of past non-existence.

The objection claims that knowledge of existence and non-existence are different, so God's knowledge must change. But the rejoinder cleverly shows that the original knowledge of non-existence does not cease; it simply becomes knowledge that the thing was non-existent. This preserves both God's omniscience and immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim that knowledge of non-existence and knowledge of existence are different, and why this would imply change.
Notice the example of Zayd being non-existent then existent.
2
Understand the rejoinder's distinction
The rejoinder says the first knowledge (that something will exist) includes knowledge of its present non-existence. After existence, this knowledge becomes knowledge that it was non-existent.
The key phrase is 'علم بأنه كان معدوماً'.
3
See how change is avoided
The original knowledge does not cease; it only changes its temporal reference. The second knowledge is new, but the first persists, so no alteration occurs.
This resolves the objection without admitting change in God.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
This defense upholds divine immutability while maintaining comprehensive knowledge of all temporal states.
This is a classic Ash'ari/Mu'tazila point of agreement on omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقد أجاب عنه مشايخ المعتزلة اعترض عليه بأنه إن سلم أن العلم بأنه وجد وسيوجد واحد لكن لا شك أن العلم بأنه معدوم والعلم بأنه موجود متغايران فإذا كان زيد معدوماً علم أنه معدوم وإذا وجد علم أنه موجود فيلزم التغير ولا يدفعه هذا الجواب وقد يجاب بأن العلم بأنه وجد والعلم بأنه سيوجد إذا كان واحدا فقيل : وجوده يعلم أنه معدوم ويعلم أنه سيوجد، فإذا وجد يعلم بالعلم الأول أنه كان معدوماً ويعلم بالعلم الثاني أنه موجود فلم يزل العلم بأنه معدوم بل علم بهذا العلم أنه كان معدوماً فأين يلزم التغير. والحق أن ما سيذكره الشارح بقوله وتوضيحه إلخ يشير إلى توجيه جواب المشايخ ويدفع عنه الاعتراض المذكور.
His statement: 'And the masters of the Mu'tazila answered'—an objection was raised against it that even if it is granted that the knowledge that it has existed and the knowledge that it will exist are one, yet there is no doubt that the knowledge that it is non-existent and the knowledge that it is existent are different. So if Zayd is non-existent, He knows that he is non-existent, and when he exists, He knows that he exists, so change follows, and this answer does not prevent it. And it may be answered that if the knowledge that it has existed and the knowledge that it will exist are one, then it is said: In its state of non-existence, He knows that it is non-existent and knows that it will exist. Then when it exists, He knows by the first knowledge that it was non-existent and knows by the second knowledge that it is existent. So the knowledge that it is non-existent did not cease; rather, by this knowledge He knows that it was non-existent. So where does change follow? And the truth is that what the commentator will mention with his statement 'and its clarification' etc. points to a direction of the answer of the masters and repels the aforementioned objection.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an objection to the Mu'tazila/Ash'ari answer and a rejoinder. The objector argues that even if knowledge of future and past existence are one, knowledge of non-existence and existence are clearly different. When Zayd is non-existent, God knows him as non-existent; when he exists, God knows him as existent. This seems to imply change. The rejoinder explains that the knowledge that something will exist (which includes its current non-existence) persists after existence as knowledge that it was non-existent. Meanwhile, a new knowledge of existence is added. The original knowledge does not cease; it merely becomes knowledge of past non-existence. Thus, no change occurs in the original knowledge. The passage concludes that the commentator's later clarification will further support this.

Correction: The rejoinder shows that knowledge of non-existence does not disappear; it becomes knowledge of past non-existence, so no change in God.

The Mu'tazila and Ash'ari Answer on Divine Knowledge Not Changing
Mu'tazila and Ash'ari argue that God's knowledge of existence and future existence is one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari argue that God's knowledge does not change with events, and how the concept of non-temporal knowledge resolves the apparent change.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not temporal, so past, present, and future do not apply to it.

In this passage, the author presents the response of Mu'tazila and Ash'ari scholars to the objection that divine knowledge would have to change as events unfold. They argue that because God is free from forgetfulness and His knowledge is eternal and non-temporal, His knowledge of an event before and after its occurrence is identical. The passage then draws on philosophical insights to explain that God's knowledge transcends time categories like past, present, and future.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally before analyzing.
Pay attention to the analogy about Zayd entering the city.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the key point: God's knowledge is continuous because He has no forgetfulness. Compare with human knowledge that requires renewal.
3
Understand the philosophical basis
The passage explains that God's knowledge is not temporal – it does not occur in time. Therefore, categories like past, present, and future do not apply, so no change occurs.
Consider the analogy with space: God's relation to all places is equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجاب عنه مشايخ المعتزلة وكثير من الأشاعرة بأن العلم بأنه وجد الشيء والعلم بأنه سيوجد واحد فإن من علم أن زيداً سيدخل البلد غداً فعند حصول الغد يعلم بهذا العلم أنه دخل البلد الآن إذا كان علمه هذا مستمراً بلا غفلة مزيلة له وإنما يحتاج أحدنا إلى علم آخر متجدد يعلم به أنه دخل الآن لطريان الغفلة الأول، والباري تعالى يمتنع عليه الغفلة فكان علمه بأنه وجد عين علمه بأنه سيوجد فلا يلزم من تغير المعلوم من عدم إلى وجود تغير في علمه وهذا الذي ذكروه مأخوذ من قول الحكماء : علمه تعالى ليس علماً زمانياً أي واقعاً في زمان كعلم أحدنا بالحوادث المختصة بأزمنة متعينة فإنه واقع في زمان مخصوص فما حدث منها في ذلك الزمان كان حاضرا عنده وما حدث قبله أو بعده كان ماضياً أو مستقبلاً، وأما علمه تعالى فلا اختصاص له بزمان أصلاً فلا يكون ثمة حال وماض ومستقبل فإن هذه صفات عارضة للزمان بالقياس إلى ما يختص بجزء منه إذ الحال معناه زمان حكمي هذا والماضي زمان هو قبل زمان حكمي هذا والمستقبل زمان هو ابعد زمان حكمي هذا فمن كان علمه أزلياً محيطاً بالزمان وغير محتاج في وجوده إليه وغير مختص بجزء معين من أجزائه لا يتصور في حقه حال ولا ماض ولا مستقبل فالله سبحانه وتعالى عالم عندهم بجميع الحوادث الجزئية وأزمنتها الواقعة هي فيها لا من حيث أن بعضها واقع الآن وبعضها في الماضي وبعضها في المستقبل فإن العلم بها من هذه الحيثية يتغير بل يعلمها علماً متعالياً عن الدخول تحت الأزمنة ثابتاً أبد الدهر، وتوضيحه أنه تعالى لما لم يكن مكانياً كانت نسبته إلى جميع الأمكنة على سواء فليس فيها بالقياس إليه قريب وبعيد ومتوسط
And the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila and many of the Ash'ari answered concerning it that the knowledge that it exists and the knowledge that it will exist are one, for whoever knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, when tomorrow arrives he knows by that same knowledge that he has entered the city now, if his knowledge is continuous without forgetfulness removing it. Each of us only needs another renewed knowledge by which he knows that he has entered now due to the occurrence of the first forgetfulness. But the Exalted Creator is impossible for forgetfulness, so His knowledge that it exists is exactly His knowledge that it will exist, and so change of the known from non-existence to existence does not necessitate change in His knowledge. And this which they mentioned is taken from the saying of the philosophers: His knowledge, exalted, is not temporal knowledge, i.e., occurring in time like the knowledge of any of us of events specific to particular times, for it occurs in a specific time, so what happened in that time was present to him, and what happened before or after it was past or future. As for His knowledge, exalted, it has no connection to time at all, so there is no present, past, or future. For these are attributes accidental to time in relation to what is specific to a part of it, since the present means this governing time, the past is the time before this governing time, and the future is the time after this governing time. So whoever's knowledge is eternal, encompassing time, not needing it for its existence, and not specific to any particular part of its parts, neither present, nor past, nor future is conceivable with respect to him. Thus God, glorified and exalted, is knower in their view of all particular events and their times in which they occur, not from the aspect that some of them are happening now, some in the past, and some in the future—for knowledge from this aspect changes—but He knows them with a knowledge exalted above entering into times, established forever. And its clarification: since He, exalted, is not spatial, His relation to all places is equal, so there is none with respect to Him near, far, or intermediate.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the answer of the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari scholars to an objection regarding divine knowledge and change. They argue that God's knowledge of a thing's existence and its future existence are identical because God is never subject to forgetfulness. Unlike humans, who need renewed knowledge when an event occurs due to forgetfulness, God's knowledge is continuous. Therefore, when a thing comes into existence, God's knowledge does not change—it remains the same knowledge He had of it before. This position is then supported by the philosophers' view that God's knowledge is not temporal. For a temporal knower, events are classified as past, present, or future relative to a reference point in time. But God's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all times equally, so past, present, and future do not apply to Him. He knows all particular events and their times, but not under the aspect of 'now', 'past', or 'future', as that would imply change. Instead, His knowledge is transcendent and immutable. The passage concludes with an analogy: just as God's relation to all places is equal because He is not spatial, so His relation to all times is equal because He is not temporal.

علم زماني
غفلة
حال
ماض
مستقبل
علم أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge must change as events happen.
Correction: The passage argues that because God's knowledge is eternal and not temporal, it does not change. The change is only in the known object, not in the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint

B) Because God is not subject to forgetfulness, so His knowledge is continuous.
C) Because events are illusory.
D) Because God learns new things as they happen.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one, eternal, and unchanging because it is not subject to time or forgetfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What specific worry or regret will you consciously surrender to God's eternal knowledge today?

Honestly examine your heart: do you truly trust that His eternal knowledge of your entire life is better than your own changing plans? Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's Objection and the Response
Objection and response regarding divine knowledge of future vs past.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's objection that knowledge of future and past must differ is refuted by appealing to God's timeless knowledge.

Core Idea
An objection valid for temporal knowledge fails for God's timeless knowledge.

We now encounter an objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who argues that God's knowledge of a future event cannot be identical to His knowledge of its past occurrence because the objects themselves are different. The response that follows exposes a critical flaw: this reasoning only holds if knowledge is temporal. Since God's knowledge transcends time, the objection does not apply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together. Notice how the objection is presented and then the response begins with 'قوله'.
The response is essentially a marginal comment refuting the objection.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the premise that 'will occur' is different in reality from 'has occurred'. This is the basis of the objection.
3
Understand the response's argument
The response says that for a non-temporal knower, these distinctions do not exist. Therefore, the objection is invalid.
The key is that God's knowledge is not bound by time.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the objection might seem plausible and why the response is effective. This clarifies the nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أنكر أبو الحسين البصري ذلك الذي ذكره هؤلاء المشايخ من أن علمه بأنه وجد عين علمه بأنه سيوجد. واحتج عليه بوجوه : الأول : حقيقة أنه سيقع غير حقيقة أنه وقع بالضرورة فالعلم به غير العلم به لأن اختلاف المتعلقين أي المعلومين يستدعي اختلاف العلم بهما. قوله: (الأول: حقيقة أنه سيقع غير حقيقة أنه وقع إلخ لايخفى أنه إذا أرجع كلام من الفلاسفة لم يتم هذا الاحتجاج إذ حقيقة سيقع، وحقيقة وقع، وإن كانا متغايرين إلا أنه لا يتحقق سيقع ووقع بالنسبة إلى من ليس علمه زمانيا ولا يتصور بالنسبة إليه ماض ومستقبل.
And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri denied that which these shaykhs mentioned, that His knowledge that it exists is exactly His knowledge that it will exist. And he argued against it with several proofs: First: The reality that it will occur is necessarily different from the reality that it has occurred, so knowledge of it is different from knowledge of it because the difference of the two objects, i.e., the two knowns, necessitates difference of the knowledge of them. His saying: (First: 'The reality that it will occur differs from the reality that it has occurred', etc. It is not hidden that if the statement of the philosophers is referred to, this argument is not established, for 'will occur' and 'has occurred', although they are different, yet 'will occur' and 'has occurred' are not realized with respect to one whose knowledge is not temporal, and past and future are not conceivable with respect to him.)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri to the earlier argument that God's knowledge of future existence is identical to His knowledge of past existence. His first proof is that the fact of something's future occurrence is necessarily different from the fact of its having occurred, so the knowledge of each is different because different objects require different knowledge. A marginal comment responds by noting that if one accepts the philosophers' view that God's knowledge is not temporal, then the objection fails. For a being whose knowledge is not temporal, there is no meaningful distinction between 'will occur' and 'has occurred'—past and future do not apply. Therefore, the objection is invalid when applied to God.

Divine Knowledge Is Not Subject to Time
The author explains that divine knowledge is timeless and thus unchanging.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge is not temporal and that all existents are equally present to Him.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless; past, present, and future are all equal to Him.

The author begins by establishing that since God and His attributes are not temporal, time does not apply to Him. Therefore, His knowledge of all existents from pre-eternity to post-eternity is not divided into past, present, and future. This sets the foundation for understanding how divine knowledge can encompass changing particulars without changing itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the emphasis on time not applying to God.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that God's relation to all times is equal. Underline this sentence.
3
Understand the consequence
Because God is not temporal, His knowledge does not have 'was', 'is', and 'will be'; rather all things are present before Him.
4
Reflect on constancy
The author says such knowledge is constant and unchanging, like knowledge of universals. Think about why this analogy is used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قريب وبعيد ومتوسط كذلك لما لم يكن هو وصفاته الحقيقية زمانية لم يتصف الزمان مقيسا إليه بالمضي والاستقبال والحضور بل كان نسبته إلى جميع الأزمنة سواء، فالموجودات من الأزل إلى الأبد معلومة له كل في وقته وليس في علمه كان وكائن وسيكون بل هي حاضرة عنده في أوقاتها فهو عالم بخصوصيات الجزئيات وأحكامها، لكن لا من حيث دخول الزمان فيها بحسب أوصافها الثلاثة إذ لا تحقق لها بالنسبة إليه ومثل هذا العلم يكون ثابتاً مستمراً لا يتغير أصلاً كالعلم بالكليات قال بعض الفضلاء وهذا معنى قولهم : إنه يعلم الجزئيات على وجه كلي لا ما توهمه بعضهم من أن علمه محيط بطبائع الجزئيات وأحكامها دون خصوصياتها وما يتعلق بها من الأحوال كيف وما ذهبوا إليه من أن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول ينافي ما توهموه كما سبقت إليه الإشارة
Likewise, near and far and intermediate, since He and His real attributes are not temporal, time is not described as past, future, or present relative to Him. Rather, His relation to all times is equal. So existents from eternity past to eternity future are known to Him, each in its own time. There is not in His knowledge 'was', 'is', and 'will be'; rather they are present before Him in their times. Thus He knows the particularities of particulars and their judgments, but not insofar as time enters into them according to their three descriptions, since they have no actuality relative to Him. Such knowledge is constant and continuous, not changing at all, like knowledge of universals. Some virtuous person said: 'This is the meaning of their statement: He knows particulars in a universal manner, not what some have imagined, that His knowledge encompasses the natures of particulars and their judgments without their particularities and the states pertaining to them. How so? What they have held, that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, contradicts what they imagined, as has been previously indicated.'
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of divine knowledge with respect to time and particulars. The author begins by stating that since God and His attributes are not temporal, time does not have past, future, or present in relation to Him. Therefore, all existents from pre-eternity to post-eternity are known to Him equally, each at its own moment. His knowledge does not involve temporal categories like 'was', 'is', and 'will be'; rather, events are present before Him at their respective times. He knows the particularities of individual things and their judgments, but not in a way that time enters into them with its threefold division, because time has no reality relative to God. Such knowledge is constant and unchanging, similar to knowledge of universals. Then the author quotes a scholar ('some virtuous person') who explains that this is the meaning of the statement 'He knows particulars in a universal manner' – not that He only knows their universal natures without their particularities, but that His knowledge is not subject to temporal change. This is contrasted with a mistaken view that would imply otherwise. The passage thus establishes that divine knowledge of changing particulars is itself unchanging because it is not conditioned by time.

زمان

Correction: God's knowledge is not temporal; therefore, all times are equally present to Him, and His knowledge does not change.

A) Because God only knows universals, not particulars.

C) Because God's knowledge is created and thus changes.
D) Because particulars do not actually exist.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is constant and unchanging because it is not conditioned by time.

He Knows Particulars in a Universal Manner
The author clarifies the meaning of 'knows particulars in a universal manner' and refutes a misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the statement 'He knows particulars in a universal manner' and how it reconciles divine knowledge with changing particulars.

Core Idea
God knows every particular thing, but not in a temporal way.

After establishing the timelessness of divine knowledge, the author addresses a common saying: 'He knows particulars in a universal manner.' Some mistakenly think this means God only knows universal natures, not individual details. The author clarifies that it means His knowledge is not subject to temporal change, and it still encompasses all particularities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the phrase 'on a universal manner' and the clarification that follows.
2
Identify the misinterpretation
Some imagine that God's knowledge only encompasses the natures of particulars, not their individual specificities. Underline that part.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that this misinterpretation contradicts the principle that knowledge of the cause gives knowledge of the effect, which would require knowledge of particularities.
4
Synthesize
Now combine this with the previous lesson: God's knowledge is both comprehensive of all details and unchanging because it is not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قريب وبعيد ومتوسط كذلك لما لم يكن هو وصفاته الحقيقية زمانية لم يتصف الزمان مقيسا إليه بالمضي والاستقبال والحضور بل كان نسبته إلى جميع الأزمنة سواء، فالموجودات من الأزل إلى الأبد معلومة له كل في وقته وليس في علمه كان وكائن وسيكون بل هي حاضرة عنده في أوقاتها فهو عالم بخصوصيات الجزئيات وأحكامها، لكن لا من حيث دخول الزمان فيها بحسب أوصافها الثلاثة إذ لا تحقق لها بالنسبة إليه ومثل هذا العلم يكون ثابتاً مستمراً لا يتغير أصلاً كالعلم بالكليات قال بعض الفضلاء وهذا معنى قولهم : إنه يعلم الجزئيات على وجه كلي لا ما توهمه بعضهم من أن علمه محيط بطبائع الجزئيات وأحكامها دون خصوصياتها وما يتعلق بها من الأحوال كيف وما ذهبوا إليه من أن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول ينافي ما توهموه كما سبقت إليه الإشارة
Likewise, near and far and intermediate, since He and His real attributes are not temporal, time is not described as past, future, or present relative to Him. Rather, His relation to all times is equal. So existents from eternity past to eternity future are known to Him, each in its own time. There is not in His knowledge 'was', 'is', and 'will be'; rather they are present before Him in their times. Thus He knows the particularities of particulars and their judgments, but not insofar as time enters into them according to their three descriptions, since they have no actuality relative to Him. Such knowledge is constant and continuous, not changing at all, like knowledge of universals. Some virtuous person said: 'This is the meaning of their statement: He knows particulars in a universal manner, not what some have imagined, that His knowledge encompasses the natures of particulars and their judgments without their particularities and the states pertaining to them. How so? What they have held, that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, contradicts what they imagined, as has been previously indicated.'
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of divine knowledge with respect to time and particulars. The author begins by stating that since God and His attributes are not temporal, time does not have past, future, or present in relation to Him. Therefore, all existents from pre-eternity to post-eternity are known to Him equally, each at its own moment. His knowledge does not involve temporal categories like 'was', 'is', and 'will be'; rather, events are present before Him at their respective times. He knows the particularities of individual things and their judgments, but not in a way that time enters into them with its threefold division, because time has no reality relative to God. Such knowledge is constant and unchanging, similar to knowledge of universals. Then the author quotes a scholar ('some virtuous person') who explains that this is the meaning of the statement 'He knows particulars in a universal manner' – not that He only knows their universal natures without their particularities, but that His knowledge is not subject to temporal change. This is contrasted with a mistaken view that would imply otherwise. The passage thus establishes that divine knowledge of changing particulars is itself unchanging because it is not conditioned by time.

طبيعة
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'knows particulars in a universal manner' means God only knows their general natures, not their individual details.
Correction: It means His knowledge is not temporal, but He still knows every particular detail.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct understanding of 'He knows particulars in a universal manner'?
A) He knows only the universal natures of particulars.
B) He knows particulars without their specific details.
C) His knowledge of particulars is not subject to time.
D) He knows particulars through universals.

Key Takeaway
The divine knowledge of particulars is universal in the sense of being timeless, not in lacking specificity.

God's Concomitance with Time
Commentator critiques and refines the argument about God and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that God's concomitance with time allows time to be described relative to Him, and his proposed alternative explanation for divine knowledge of temporal events.

Core Idea
God's relation to time is solved by knowledge of time as container, not by denying temporality.

The commentator addresses a common argument that God and His attributes are not temporal, so time cannot be described relative to Him. He critiques this reasoning and offers a better way: God knows each moment as the container for events, not as past or future. This lesson walks through his refutation and solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the commentator's opening critique of the claim that God and His attributes are not temporal.
2
Understand the problem
The commentator says that while God is not in time, He is concomitant with time. This concomitance is sufficient for time to be described as past, present, or future relative to God. Identify this key point.
The 'three descriptions' likely refer to past, present, future.
3
Compare with space
Notice how the commentator contrasts time with space. Space has all parts present to God, but time's parts are sequential. This makes the simple non-temporality argument problematic.
4
Learn the alternative
The commentator proposes that instead of relying on God's non-temporality, we say that God knows a specific part of time as the container for an event, not in terms of past/future. This knowledge does not change with time.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كذلك لما لم يكن هو وصفاته الحقيقية زمانية فيه بحث لأن الله سبحانه ليس بواقع في الزمان وكذا صفاته لكن لا شك أنه مقارن له كما مر وهذه المقارنة تكفي في اتصاف الزمان مقيسا إليه تعالى بالأوصاف الثلاثة، وبالجملة المكان مجتمع الأجزاء حاضر عنده تعالى ولا يتصور فيه القرب والبعد بالنسبة إليه سبحانه لأنه ليس بمكاني وهذا ظاهر، وأما الزمان فأجزاؤه ممتنعة الاجتماع في الوجود وكذا الزمانيات قد لا تكون مجتمعة فيه. فالقول بأن نسبته تعالى إلى جميع الأزمنة سواء مع مقارنته لليوم مثلا بناء على أنه ليس بواقع في الزمان لا يخلو عن إشكال، فالأولى في بيان عدم لزوم التغير أصلا أن يترك القياس على المكان ولا يبنى الكلام على أنه تعالى ليس زمانياً، بل يقال : إن الله تعالى يعلم أن الجزء المعين من الزمان لا باعتبار المضي والاستقبال والحضور بل بحسب ذاته تعالى ظرف للحادث الفلاني، والعلم بهذا الوجه لا يتغير بتغير الأزمنة سواء كان العلم زمانياً أولا وهذا ظاهر عند التأمل.
قوله : ( قال بعض الفضلاء إلخ) قال الأستاذ المحقق ما ذهبوا إليه من أن العلم بالجزئيات المتشكلة يحتاج إلى الآلات الجسمانية ينافي ما حمل عليه هذا الفاضل مذهبهم في هذه المسألة فمنافاة مذهبهم على أي محمل حمل لأصل من أصولهم المقررة عندهم لازمة لله لا مجال لتخليصهم عنها .
His statement: 'Likewise, since He and His real attributes are not temporal' is debatable, because Allah is not located in time, and likewise His attributes, but there is no doubt that He is concomitant with it as has passed. This concomitance suffices for time to be described with the three descriptions relative to Him. In summary, space, with its parts gathered, is present before Him and nearness and distance relative to Him are inconceivable because He is not spatial, and this is apparent. As for time, its parts are impossible to gather in existence, and likewise temporal things may not be gathered in it. So the statement that His relation to all times is equal, despite His concomitance with today, for example, based on His not being in time, is not free from difficulty. The better way to explain that change does not occur at all is to abandon the analogy with space and not base the discussion on the fact that He is not temporal. Rather, it should be said: Allah knows that the specific part of time, not in terms of past, future, and present, but in terms of His essence, is the container for such-and-such an event. Knowledge in this manner does not change with the change of times, whether the knowledge is temporal or not. This is apparent upon reflection.
His statement: 'Some virtuous person said, etc.' The verifying teacher said: 'What they have held, that knowledge of changing particulars requires bodily instruments, contradicts what this virtuous person has interpreted their school to hold in this issue. So the contradiction of their school, on any interpretation, to one of their established principles is necessary for them, and there is no way to extricate them from it.'
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the previous main text. The commentator first takes issue with the claim that 'since He and His attributes are not temporal, time is not described as past, future, or present relative to Him.' He argues that while Allah is not in time, He is concomitant with time, and this concomitance suffices for time to have those descriptions relative to Him. He then draws an analogy with space: God is not spatial, so nearness and distance don't apply. But time is different because its parts cannot all be present together. Therefore, saying that God's relation to all times is equal, despite being concomitant with a particular day, is problematic. The commentator suggests a better approach: instead of relying on God's not being temporal, one should say that God knows that a specific part of time is the container for an event, and this knowledge does not change with time regardless of whether the knowledge itself is temporal. Then the commentator addresses a quote from 'some virtuous person', and he reports the 'verifying teacher's' critique: that the claim that knowledge of changing particulars requires bodily instruments contradicts the school's own principles, and there is no way out for them.

Key Terms
زمان
مقارنة
مكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's non-temporality alone solves the problem of time relation, making all times equal relative to Him.
Correction: The commentator shows that this leads to difficulty; instead, God's knowledge of time as a container for events is the better explanation.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main objection to the claim that God's relation to all times is equal?
A) It contradicts God's concomitance with a specific day.
B) God is in time, so relation varies.
C) Time does not exist for God.
D) The claim is not based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of time as container for events avoids the difficulty of claiming equal relation while being concomitant with specific moments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God knows every moment of your life—past, present, and future—without change. But do you live as if that knowledge is a static record, or as if God sees your current struggle as part of an unfolding plan? Which perspective changes how you act right now?
?
The commentator warns against simplistic analogies when thinking about God and time. Where in your own understanding have you made an easy parallel—like comparing God's relation to time with His relation to space—that actually oversimplifies the truth? Will you abandon that analogy today?
?
God knows the specific part of time as the container for your next sin, your next prayer, your next moment of doubt. Since His knowledge does not change, what does that mean for the excuses you make about 'not being ready yet' to change? What one step will you take before the day ends?

Critique of the Virtuous Person's View
Commentator reports a verifying teacher's critique of a scholar's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the verifying teacher's critique that the claim that knowledge of changing particulars requires bodily instruments contradicts the principles of their own school.

Core Idea
A school's own principles can undermine their claims about divine knowledge.

The commentator reports a critique by the 'verifying teacher' against a virtuous person's interpretation. The issue is whether knowledge of changing particulars requires bodily instruments. The teacher argues that this claim contradicts the school's own established principles, leaving no escape. This lesson examines that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this part. Identify who is being critiqued and by whom.
2
Understand the claim
The virtuous person's school (or interpretation) holds that knowledge of changing particulars requires bodily instruments. This is the target.
3
Grasp the contradiction
The verifying teacher says this claim contradicts the school's own established principles. No matter how you interpret it, the contradiction is inescapable.
The exact principles are not detailed here, but the point is the inconsistency.

Timeless Knowledge: Refuting Abu al-Husayn
Abu al-Husayn's objection and its refutation based on timelessness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu al-Husayn's objection that knowledge of future and past must be distinct does not apply to God's timeless knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless; past and future are irrelevant.

This lesson presents Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's first objection against the claim that God's knowledge of a thing's existence is identical to His knowledge that it will exist. He argues that because the realities of 'it will occur' and 'it occurred' are different, the knowledge must differ. The response shows that this reasoning only works for temporal beings, not for a timeless God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
Notice the structure: objection followed by response.
2
Understand Abu al-Husayn's argument
Identify his premise: different objects of knowledge (future vs. past) require different knowledge. This seems intuitive for temporal beings.
3
Examine the response
The response points out that for a knower whose knowledge is not temporal, the concepts 'will occur' and 'occurred' are not actualized. Therefore, the distinction collapses.
Think about what 'timeless' means: no before or after.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this defense protects the doctrine of divine omniscience without temporal limitations.
This is a classic move in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أنكر أبو الحسين البصري ذلك) الذي ذكره هؤلاء المشايخ من أن علمه بأنه وجد عين علمه بأنه سيوجد. واحتج عليه بوجوه : الأول : حقيقة أنه سيقع غير حقيقة أنه وقع بالضرورة ) فالعلم به غير العلم به لأن اختلاف المتعلقين أي المعلومين ( يستدعي اختلاف العلم بهما .
قوله: (الأول: حقيقة أنه سيقع إلخ لايخفى أنه إذا أرجع كلام من الفلاسفة لم يتم هذا الاحتجاج إذ حقيقة سيقع، وحقيقة وقع، وإن كانا متغايرين إلا أنه لا يتحقق سيقع ووقع بالنسبة إلى من ليس علمه زمانيا ولا يتصور بالنسبة إليه ماض ومستقبل.
And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri denied that which these shaykhs mentioned, that His knowledge of that it exists is the same as His knowledge that it will exist. He argued against it with proofs: The first: The reality that it will occur is necessarily different from the reality that it occurred. So knowledge of it is different from knowledge of it, because the difference of the two objects (i.e., the two known things) necessitates a difference in knowledge of them.
His statement: '(The first: The reality that it will occur, etc.)' It is not hidden that if one refers back to the speech of the philosophers, this argument is not complete, because 'the reality that it will occur' and 'the reality that it occurred', although they are different, yet 'will occur' and 'occurred' are not actualized for one whose knowledge is not temporal, and past and future are not conceivable relative to him.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Husayn's first objection and a response. Abu al-Husayn denies the claim that God's knowledge of a thing's existence is identical to His knowledge of its future existence. He argues: the reality of 'it will occur' is necessarily different from the reality of 'it occurred', so the knowledge of one is different from knowledge of the other, because different objects of knowledge require different knowledge. The commentator then responds: this argument fails when one considers the philosophers' perspective, because for a knower whose knowledge is not temporal, 'will occur' and 'occurred' are not actualized – past and future are not conceivable relative to such a knower. Thus, the objection assumes temporal categories that do not apply to God.

Key Terms
علم
تعلق
ماض
مستقبل
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of future events must be a different kind of knowledge than His knowledge of past events, because the events themselves are different.
Correction: The response shows that for a timeless God, past and future are not distinct categories; His knowledge is one and the same, not subject to temporal distinctions.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does Abu al-Husayn's first objection not apply to God?
A) Because God's knowledge is created and changes.
B) Because for a timeless knower, past and future are not distinct categories.
C) Because God does not know future events.
D) Because the objection is based on a flawed translation.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is not confined by time; therefore, distinctions based on past and future do not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes imagine God's knowledge as unfolding in time, like a movie you watch? How does this lesson challenge that mental image? What would it mean for your trust in Allah if you truly believed He sees your entire life as a single present moment?
?
When you struggle with a past sin, do you ever feel that God's view of you is 'stuck' in that moment? Based on this lesson, how should you reframe that thought? What action can you take today to align your belief with Allah's timeless forgiveness?
?
If you knew that Allah already knows every choice you will make today, would you live differently? How does the timelessness of His knowledge free you from anxiety about the future? What is one decision you can make right now that reflects that trust?

Abu al-Husayn's Second Objection: Conditions of Knowledge
Abu al-Husayn's second objection using conditions of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Husayn's second objection uses differing conditions of knowledge to argue that knowledge of future events is distinct from knowledge of past events.

Core Idea
Condition for knowledge of occurrence differs from condition for knowledge of future occurrence.

Abu al-Husayn presents a second argument against the identity of knowledge of past and future events. He points out that the conditions for knowing something has occurred versus knowing it will occur are not only different but actually conflicting. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the illustrative example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the logical structure: statement of conditions, example, and additional note.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the key conditions
Identify the two conditions: knowledge that something occurred requires its occurrence; knowledge that something will occur requires its non-occurrence. Ask yourself why these conditions conflict.
3
Analyze the example
Work through the example of Zayd entering the city tomorrow. Understand why continuous knowledge of future entry does not automatically yield knowledge of past entry without additional condition (knowledge of time's arrival).
The note explains why this example is not a third proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن شرط العلم بأنه وقع هو الوقوع وشرط العلم بأنه سيقع هو عدم الوقوع فلو كانا واحداً لم يختلف شرطهما أصلا فضلا عن التنافي بين شرطيهما. وقد يعبر عنه بأن من علم أن زيداً سيدخل البلد غداً وجلس إلى مجيء الغد في بيت مظلم مستديماً لذلك العلم فلم يعلم لأجل الظلمة دخول غد لم يعلم أنه دخل البلد بذلك العلم المستمر فكيف يكون أحدهما عين الآخر. نعم لو انضم إليه العلم بدخول غد علم من هذين العلمين ذلك أي أنه دخل فيكون حينئذ هذا العلم متفرعاً على العلمين السابقين لا عين وإنما لم يجعله وجهاً ثالثاً كما فعله الإمام الرازي في الأربعين لأن محصوله هو العلم بأنه سيدخل البلد غداً ليس مشروطاً بالعلم بمجيء الغد، والعلم بأنه دخل في مثالنا هذا مشروط به فيكون راجعاً إلى الوجه الثاني لا وجهاً على حدة.
The second: The condition of knowledge that it occurred is the occurrence, and the condition of knowledge that it will occur is non-occurrence. So if the two were one, their conditions would not differ at all, let alone the conflict between their conditions. It is sometimes expressed by: He who knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and sits until the coming of tomorrow in a dark house, maintaining that knowledge, but did not know due to darkness the advent of tomorrow, does not know that he entered the city by that continuous knowledge. So how can one be the other? Yes, if the knowledge of the advent of tomorrow is joined to it, then from these two knowledges he knows that (i.e., that he entered). In that case, this knowledge is derived from the two previous knowledges, not identical. And the reason he (the author) did not make it a third proof, as al-Imam al-Razi did in al-Arba'in, is that its content is: knowledge that he will enter the city tomorrow is not conditioned by knowledge of the advent of tomorrow, while knowledge that he entered in our example is conditioned by it, so it reduces to the second proof, not a separate proof.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Husayn's second objection. He argues that the condition for knowledge of an event having occurred is its actual occurrence, while the condition for knowledge that it will occur is its non-occurrence. If the two knowledges were identical, their conditions would not differ, but they do, indicating distinctness. An example illustrates: someone knows Zayd will enter tomorrow, sits in a dark house until the next day, but due to darkness does not perceive the advent of tomorrow, so that continuous knowledge does not tell him that Zayd has entered. Hence the knowledge of future entry is not the same as knowledge of past entry. If he later knows the advent of tomorrow, then he knows the entry, but this knowledge is derived from two previous knowledges, not identical. The commentator notes that this example was not made a separate proof by the author because it reduces to the second proof's reasoning about conditions.

Key Terms
شرط
علم
وقوع
عدم وقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that continuous knowledge of a future event automatically gives knowledge of its occurrence once the time arrives.
Correction: The example shows that additional condition (knowledge of time's arrival) is needed; continuous future knowledge alone does not suffice.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for knowledge that an event will occur according to the second proof?
A) The event must have occurred.
B) The event must not have occurred yet.
C) The knower must be present at the event.
D) The event must be universal.

Key Takeaway
The condition for knowledge of a future event is its non-occurrence, which conflicts with the condition for knowledge of a past event (occurrence), proving their distinctness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been so certain that something would happen that you began to act as if it had already occurred? Now that you understand that knowledge of the future requires its non-occurrence, how does this expose the hidden presumption in your certainty? Will you confess to Allah that your 'knowledge' was mere speculation and submit to His unseen decree?
?
If the condition for knowing that something will happen is that it has not yet happened, then how can you be truly sure of any future outcome, including your own salvation or the success of your efforts? Does this lesson unsettle your confidence in your own plans? What would it look like to hold your future hopes with humility, acknowledging that you do not know until it occurs?
?
The passage shows that knowledge of a past event depends on the event having occurred. Think of a time you were absolutely certain about something that happened, only later to discover you were mistaken. How does the lesson's stress on proper conditions for knowledge challenge you to examine the foundations of your beliefs—both about the past and the future? Will you now pause before claiming certainty, and ask Allah to guide you to truth?

The Third Proof: Temporal Separation of Knowledge
Abu al-Husayn's third proof and its immediate reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that knowledge of an event at the time of occurrence can be separate from knowledge of it beforehand, indicating distinct knowledges.

Core Idea
Knowing now does not require knowing before.

This lesson introduces Abu al-Husayn's third argument for the multiplicity of divine attributes. It uses the simple observation that one can know an event at the moment it happens without having known it beforehand, and vice versa. The proof suggests that such knowledge must be distinct, and then immediately notes that this might reduce to the first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'الثالث : يمكن العلم أنه وقع مع الجهل بأنه سيقع' and its translation. Notice the two examples: knowing after occurrence without foreknowledge, and knowing beforehand without simultaneous knowledge.
The proof is based on temporal distinction.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The proof gives two cases: (1) knowing an event at the moment it occurs without prior knowledge, and (2) knowing it beforehand without knowing it at the moment. Observe how these imply that the two knowledges (before and after) are different.
The term 'غير المعلوم' (other than the known) is used to argue that if the objects differ, the knowledges differ.
3
Understand the reduction claim
The passage ends by stating that on this basis, the third proof reduces to the first. Think about why that might be argued—perhaps because both rely on the distinction of objects.
This reduction will be challenged in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : يمكن العلم بأنه وقع مع الجهل بأنه سيقع كما إذا علم الحادث حال حدوثه ولم يشعر به قبله أصلاً وبالعكس كما إذا علم حاله قبل حدوثه ولم يشعر به في أوانه وغير المعلوم أي ما ليس معلوماً في زمان غير المعلوم أي مغاير لما هو معلوم في ذلك الزمان وإذا تغاير المعلومان تغاير العلمان، وعلى هذا فقد رجع الثالث إلى الأول والصواب كما هو في الأربعين أنه يمكن العلم بأنه سيقع مع الجهل بأنه عالم بأنه وقع بالعكس وغير المعلوم غير المعلوم فيثبت حينئذ تمايز العلمين ابتداء ويشهد لما قلناه قوله : ( وقد يعبر عن هذا الثالث : بأن قبل الوقوع اعتقاد أنه سيقع علم و اعتقاد أنه وقع جهل وبعد الوقوع وبالعكس فتغايرا لتنافي وصفيهما أعني العلمية والجهلية كتنافي وصف المعلومية والمجهولية المعتبرتين في الوجه الثالث، وقد الإمام الرازي وجها برأسه ثم إن أبا الحسين بعد إبطاله جواب مشايخه التزم وقوع التغير في علم الباري سبحانه وتعالى بالمتغيرات. وزعم أن ذاته تعالى تقتضي كونه
قوله : (فقد رجع الثالث إلى الأول قد يجاب عنه بأن المقصود من الوجه الثالث أن كلاً من العلمين ينفك عن الآخر وانفكاك الشيء عن نفسه محال فثبت تغايرهما وهو ظاهر، إلا أنه ضم إليه تغاير المتعلقين لزيادة الاستظهار ويشهد به شهادة بينة قوله وقد يغير إلخ فإنه صريح في بيان انفكاك كل منهما عن الآخر فعلى هذا لم يرجع الثالث إلى الأول .
قوله: (والصواب كما هو في (الأربعين رد عليه بأنه يدل على تغاير العلمين بالاعتبار لا بالذات وهو المراد، وذلك لأن الشيء الواحد يجوز أن يكون معلوماً باعتبار ومجهولاً بآخر. وأجيب : بأن المراد أنه يمكن العلم بأنه سيقع في الجملة مع الجهل بأنه عالم بأنه وقع من كل الوجوه فعلى هذا يثبت تغاير العلمين بالذات .
The third: It is possible to know that it occurred while being ignorant that it will occur, as when one knows an event at the moment of its occurrence and was not aware of it before at all, and vice versa, as when one knows its state before occurrence and was not aware of it at its time. And the unknown, i.e., what is not known in a time, is different from the known. So if the two known things differ, the two knowledges differ. On this basis, the third reduces to the first. But the correct view, as in al-Arba'in, is that it is possible to know that it will occur while being ignorant that one knows that it occurred, and vice versa, and the unknown is different from the known. Thus the distinctness of the two knowledges is established ab initio. And it supports what we say: His statement: 'And this third is sometimes expressed by: before occurrence, the belief that it will occur is knowledge and the belief that it occurred is ignorance; after occurrence, the reverse. So they differ due to the opposition of their two attributes, namely knowledge and ignorance, like the opposition of the attributes of knownness and unknownness considered in the third proof. And al-Imam al-Razi made it a separate proof. Then Abu al-Husayn, after refuting the response of his shaykhs, committed to the occurrence of change in the knowledge of the Exalted Creator with respect to changing things, and claimed that His essence necessitates that He be... (text breaks off).
His statement: 'Thus the third reduces to the first.' It may be answered that the intent of the third proof is that each of the two knowledges is separable from the other, and the separation of a thing from itself is impossible, so their difference is established. This is apparent. But he added to it the difference of the objects for further clarification. And it is clearly witnessed by his statement 'and it is sometimes expressed...' which explicitly shows the separation of each from the other. On this basis, the third does not reduce to the first.
His statement: 'And the correct view as in al-Arba'in.' He responded to it that it indicates the difference of the two knowledges by consideration (bi-l-i'tibar) not by essence (bi-l-dhat), and that is what is meant. That is because a single thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another. And it was answered: What is meant is that it is possible to have knowledge that it will occur in general while being ignorant that one knows that it occurred from all aspects. On this basis, the difference of the two knowledges by essence is established.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and extensive commentary. The third proof states that knowledge of occurrence can exist without knowledge of future occurrence, and vice versa, indicating they are distinct. The commentary first notes that this reduces to the first proof, but then argues against that reduction. The correct view, according to al-Arba'in, is that the distinctness is established initially. Another expression is given: before an event, belief in future occurrence is knowledge, belief in past occurrence is ignorance; after, the reverse. This shows separation. Then there is discussion about whether the difference is by consideration or by essence. The commentator defends that the difference is by essence, not just by consideration, based on the possibility of complete ignorance from all aspects.

Key Terms
علم
جهل
انفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the third proof aim to show?
A) That knowledge of an event is always the same regardless of time.
B) That knowledge of an event can exist without knowledge of the same event at another time, suggesting distinct knowledges.
C) That the first proof is invalid.
D) That God's knowledge does not change.

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses temporal examples to argue that knowledge of an event at one time can be separate from knowledge of it at another time, implying multiple distinct knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The third proof presents two scenarios: knowing an event only at the moment of its occurrence, and knowing it only before it occurs. Which of these patterns have you experienced in your own life? How does recognizing your temporal limitations in knowledge deepen your trust in Allah's complete and timeless knowledge?
?
If knowledge can be present at one time and absent at another, what does this reveal about your need for constant guidance from Allah? In what area of your life today are you assuming you have full knowledge when you might actually be ignorant? How will you seek Allah's light in that area?

Correcting the Reduction: Distinctness Established
Correcting the reduction and presenting an alternative formulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct view that the third proof establishes distinctness of knowledges ab initio, and the alternative expression that clarifies the separation.

Core Idea
The third proof stands on its own, showing separation directly.

After the initial claim that the third proof reduces to the first, the text presents the correct view as found in al-Arba'in. It argues that the distinctness of the two knowledges is established at the outset, without relying on the first proof. An alternative expression is then given, contrasting beliefs before and after an event to further illustrate the separation.

Guided Path
1
Read the correct view
Start from 'والصواب كما هو في الأربعين' and read until 'وقد الإمام الرازي وجها برأسه'. Notice how the correct view rejects the reduction and asserts that distinctness is established ab initio.
The phrase 'ابتداء' means from the beginning, i.e., not derived from the first proof.
2
Examine the alternative expression
The passage gives another way to express the third proof: before an event, belief that it will occur is knowledge, and belief that it has occurred is ignorance; after the event, the opposite. This shows that the two knowledges have opposite attributes (knowledge vs. ignorance), so they are distinct.
This expression highlights the conflict of attributes.
3
Note al-Razi's position
Imam al-Razi considered this third proof as a separate, independent argument. This underscores its significance.
This shows the proof was taken seriously by later scholars.

By Essence or By Consideration? The Final Resolution
Addressing the objection of difference by consideration and affirming essence difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the difference between the two knowledges is by essence (bi-l-dhat) or by consideration (bi-l-i'tibar), and how the final position defends the essential distinction.

Core Idea
The same object can be known in one respect and unknown in another, but the knowledges themselves differ in essence.

This lesson addresses the nuanced objection that the third proof only shows a difference by consideration (one thing being known in one respect and unknown in another), not a real difference in the knowledges themselves. The commentary defends that the intended meaning is a complete ignorance from all aspects, which entails an essential distinction. Abu al-Husayn's commitment to change in divine knowledge is also mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Start from 'ثم إن أبا الحسين بعد إبطاله جواب مشايخه' and read to the end. First, note Abu al-Husayn's position that change occurs in divine knowledge. Then follow the commentary's objection and response.
The commentary is introduced by 'قوله'.
2
Understand the 'consideration' objection
The objection says that the third proof only shows that the same thing is known in one respect and unknown in another, which is a difference by consideration, not by essence. This would not prove multiple knowledges.
This is a subtle distinction in kalam.
3
Grasp the essentialist response
The response argues that the intended meaning is complete ignorance from all aspects (من كل الوجوه), which leads to a difference in the knowledges themselves, not just in the mode of consideration.
This defends the proof's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : يمكن العلم بأنه وقع مع الجهل بأنه سيقع كما إذا علم الحادث حال حدوثه ولم يشعر به قبله أصلاً وبالعكس كما إذا علم حاله قبل حدوثه ولم يشعر به في أوانه وغير المعلوم أي ما ليس معلوماً في زمان غير المعلوم أي مغاير لما هو معلوم في ذلك الزمان وإذا تغاير المعلومان تغاير العلمان، وعلى هذا فقد رجع الثالث إلى الأول والصواب كما هو في الأربعين أنه يمكن العلم بأنه سيقع مع الجهل بأنه عالم بأنه وقع بالعكس وغير المعلوم غير المعلوم فيثبت حينئذ تمايز العلمين ابتداء ويشهد لما قلناه قوله : ( وقد يعبر عن هذا الثالث : بأن قبل الوقوع اعتقاد أنه سيقع علم و اعتقاد أنه وقع جهل وبعد الوقوع وبالعكس فتغايرا لتنافي وصفيهما أعني العلمية والجهلية كتنافي وصف المعلومية والمجهولية المعتبرتين في الوجه الثالث، وقد الإمام الرازي وجها برأسه ثم إن أبا الحسين بعد إبطاله جواب مشايخه التزم وقوع التغير في علم الباري سبحانه وتعالى بالمتغيرات. وزعم أن ذاته تعالى تقتضي كونه
قوله : (فقد رجع الثالث إلى الأول قد يجاب عنه بأن المقصود من الوجه الثالث أن كلاً من العلمين ينفك عن الآخر وانفكاك الشيء عن نفسه محال فثبت تغايرهما وهو ظاهر، إلا أنه ضم إليه تغاير المتعلقين لزيادة الاستظهار ويشهد به شهادة بينة قوله وقد يغير إلخ فإنه صريح في بيان انفكاك كل منهما عن الآخر فعلى هذا لم يرجع الثالث إلى الأول .
قوله: (والصواب كما هو في (الأربعين رد عليه بأنه يدل على تغاير العلمين بالاعتبار لا بالذات وهو المراد، وذلك لأن الشيء الواحد يجوز أن يكون معلوماً باعتبار ومجهولاً بآخر. وأجيب : بأن المراد أنه يمكن العلم بأنه سيقع في الجملة مع الجهل بأنه عالم بأنه وقع من كل الوجوه فعلى هذا يثبت تغاير العلمين بالذات .
The third: It is possible to know that it occurred while being ignorant that it will occur, as when one knows an event at the moment of its occurrence and was not aware of it before at all, and vice versa, as when one knows its state before occurrence and was not aware of it at its time. And the unknown, i.e., what is not known in a time, is different from the known. So if the two known things differ, the two knowledges differ. On this basis, the third reduces to the first. But the correct view, as in al-Arba'in, is that it is possible to know that it will occur while being ignorant that one knows that it occurred, and vice versa, and the unknown is different from the known. Thus the distinctness of the two knowledges is established ab initio. And it supports what we say: His statement: 'And this third is sometimes expressed by: before occurrence, the belief that it will occur is knowledge and the belief that it occurred is ignorance; after occurrence, the reverse. So they differ due to the opposition of their two attributes, namely knowledge and ignorance, like the opposition of the attributes of knownness and unknownness considered in the third proof. And al-Imam al-Razi made it a separate proof. Then Abu al-Husayn, after refuting the response of his shaykhs, committed to the occurrence of change in the knowledge of the Exalted Creator with respect to changing things, and claimed that His essence necessitates that He be... (text breaks off).
His statement: 'Thus the third reduces to the first.' It may be answered that the intent of the third proof is that each of the two knowledges is separable from the other, and the separation of a thing from itself is impossible, so their difference is established. This is apparent. But he added to it the difference of the objects for further clarification. And it is clearly witnessed by his statement 'and it is sometimes expressed...' which explicitly shows the separation of each from the other. On this basis, the third does not reduce to the first.
His statement: 'And the correct view as in al-Arba'in.' He responded to it that it indicates the difference of the two knowledges by consideration (bi-l-i'tibar) not by essence (bi-l-dhat), and that is what is meant. That is because a single thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another. And it was answered: What is meant is that it is possible to have knowledge that it will occur in general while being ignorant that one knows that it occurred from all aspects. On this basis, the difference of the two knowledges by essence is established.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and extensive commentary. The third proof states that knowledge of occurrence can exist without knowledge of future occurrence, and vice versa, indicating they are distinct. The commentary first notes that this reduces to the first proof, but then argues against that reduction. The correct view, according to al-Arba'in, is that the distinctness is established initially. Another expression is given: before an event, belief in future occurrence is knowledge, belief in past occurrence is ignorance; after, the reverse. This shows separation. Then there is discussion about whether the difference is by consideration or by essence. The commentator defends that the difference is by essence, not just by consideration, based on the possibility of complete ignorance from all aspects.

Key Terms
اعتبار
ذات
علم
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third proof only shows a difference in how we consider the same knowledge, not a real distinction.
Correction: The proof intends complete ignorance from all aspects, which requires the knowledges themselves to be distinct in essence.

Checkpoint
How does the commentary defend that the third proof shows difference by essence?
A) By accepting that the difference is only by consideration.
B) By arguing that the ignorance is from all aspects, so the knowledges cannot be the same.
C) By claiming that the first proof already established essential difference.
D) By quoting al-Razi.

Key Takeaway
The third proof ultimately establishes an essential difference between the two knowledges, not merely a difference in consideration, because the ignorance is complete from all aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim ignorance about a sin or shortcoming, do you admit to a complete lack of awareness from all aspects, or is it a convenient partial excuse? How would your accountability change if you acknowledged that God's knowledge of that sin is essential and complete?
?
The lesson forces a distinction between a conceptual label and a real attribute. In your own relationship with God, do you treat His attributes—like knowledge, mercy, or justice—as mere concepts to affirm, or as realities that demand a response from your very core? What is one attribute you have reduced to a consideration rather than an essence?
?
If you truly believed that God's knowledge of your hidden thoughts is not just a theoretical category but an essential aspect of His being that never changes, what secret would you feel compelled to confess today? How does that thought make you feel—dread or relief?

The Second Argument: Conflicting Conditions for Future and Past Knowledge
Al-Jurjani presents the second argument distinguishing future and past knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the distinction between knowledge of a future event and knowledge of its occurrence, based on the contradiction of their conditions.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a future event and its occurrence have opposite conditions.

The second argument builds on a simple insight: the condition for knowing something will happen is that it has not happened yet, while knowing it has happened requires that it has occurred. If the two knowledges were identical, their conditions would not differ. Al-Jurjani illustrates this with a vivid example involving Zayd and a dark house, then clarifies why one might mistakenly think the example suggests a third type of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then follow the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: statement, example, clarification.
This passage is dense, so read slowly.
2
Understand the core conditional logic
Identify the two opposing conditions: for knowledge that something will occur, the condition is that it has not occurred yet; for knowledge that it has occurred, the condition is that it occurred. If they were the same knowledge, how could their conditions conflict?
This is the heart of the argument.
3
Work through the Zayd example
Consider the scenario: someone knows Zayd will enter tomorrow, stays in a dark house, and maintains that knowledge. When tomorrow arrives, he doesn't know it due to darkness, so he cannot know that Zayd entered. This shows the future knowledge alone does not become past knowledge.
The example demonstrates the practical difference.
4
Understand why it is not a third aspect
Al-Jurjani notes that if you add knowledge of the arrival of tomorrow, you can deduce that Zayd entered. But this derived knowledge is not identical to the prior knowledges; it depends on them. He explains why this is still the second aspect, not a third independent one as al-Razi considered.
This clarifies the argument's boundaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما. أن العلم بأنه وقع هو الوقوع وشرط العلم بأنه سيقع هو عدم الوقوع فلو كانا واحداً لم يختلف شرطهما أصلا فضلا عن التنافي بين شرطيهما. وقد يعبر عنه أي عن الوجه الثاني بأن من علم أن زيداً سيدخل البلد غداً وجلس إلى مجيء الغد في بيت مظلم مستديماً لذلك العلم فلم يعلم لأجل الظلمة دخول غد لم يعلم أنه دخل البلد بذلك العلم المستمر فكيف يكون أحدهما عين الآخر. نعم لو انضم إليه أي إلى العلم بأنه سيدخل العلم بدخول غد علم من هذين العلمين ذلك أي أنه دخل فيكون حينئذ هذا العلم متفرعاً على العلمين السابقين لا عين وإنما لم يجعله وجهاً ثالثاً كما فعله الإمام الرازي في الأربعين لأن محصوله هو العلم بأنه سيدخل البلد غداً ليس مشروطاً بالعلم بمجيء الغد، والعلم بأنه دخل في مثالنا هذا مشروط به فيكون راجعاً إلى الوجه الثاني لا وجهاً على حدة.
One of them: that knowledge that it occurred is the occurrence, and the condition of knowledge that it will occur is non-occurrence. For if they were one, their conditions would not differ at all, let alone conflict between their conditions. And it is expressed regarding it, i.e., the second aspect, that whoever knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and sits until the arrival of tomorrow in a dark house, continuing that knowledge, then did not know due to darkness the arrival of tomorrow, he did not know that he entered the city by that continuing knowledge. So how can one of them be the very other? Yes, if there is joined to it, i.e., to the knowledge that he will enter, the knowledge of the arrival of tomorrow, then from these two knowledges he knows that, i.e., that he entered. So then this knowledge is derived from the two preceding knowledges, not identical [to them]. And the reason he did not make it a third aspect, as Imam al-Razi did in al-Arba'in, is because its product is the knowledge that he will enter the city tomorrow is not conditioned by knowledge of the arrival of tomorrow, while the knowledge that he entered in this example is conditioned by it. So it reverts to the second aspect, not as an independent aspect.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the distinction between knowledge of an event before it occurs and knowledge of it after it occurs. The argument is that the conditions for these two knowledges differ: knowledge that something will occur requires that it has not occurred yet, while knowledge that it has occurred requires that it has occurred. If the two knowledges were identical, their conditions would not differ, nor would there be a conflict of conditions. Then the author provides an example: a person who knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, and sits in a dark house until the next day, maintaining that knowledge of the future event, but due to darkness, does not know when 'tomorrow' arrives, and thus does not know that Zayd actually entered. The knowledge of the future event alone does not become knowledge of the past event. If one adds to the first knowledge the knowledge of the arrival of 'tomorrow', then from both he knows that Zayd entered. This second knowledge is derived from the first two, not identical to them. The author explains why this derived knowledge should not be considered a third aspect, as Imam al-Razi treated it. The product (the knowledge that he will enter tomorrow) is not conditioned by knowledge of the arrival of tomorrow, but the knowledge that he entered is so conditioned. Therefore, the derived knowledge is ultimately an instance of the second aspect, not an independent category.

Key Terms
العلم بالوقوع
العلم بالاستقبال
شرط العلم
الوجه الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that future knowledge and past knowledge are identical because they refer to the same event.
Correction: The argument shows they are distinct because their conditions conflict: one requires non-occurrence, the other occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what is the condition for knowledge that something will occur?
A) Its occurrence
B) Its non-occurrence
C) Knowledge of the present moment
D) The existence of the thing

Key Takeaway
The second argument proves the distinction between future and past knowledge by showing that their conditions (non-occurrence vs. occurrence) are mutually exclusive, and no amount of persistence of one knowledge transforms it into the other without new information.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you were certain that something would happen and then it did, did your knowledge feel like the same knowledge or something new? Why?
?
If your knowledge of the future depends on non-occurrence, what does that reveal about the limits of your certainty? Will you humbly admit that your grasping of future events is fragile?
?
This argument shows that your knowledge is conditioned by time. How does that affect your trust in Allah's timeless knowledge? Are you willing to surrender your temporal certainty to His eternal knowledge?

The Third Argument: Establishing Distinctness of Knowledges
Al-Jurjani presents the third argument and asserts the correct view that the two knowledges are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the distinction between knowledge before and after an event, and why it is considered independently correct.

Core Idea
Two knowledges differ because their objects differ, and this alone establishes their distinctness.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a third argument for the distinction between knowledge of an event before and after it occurs. This argument, based on the difference in the objects known, is particularly powerful because it does not rely on the first argument. He then clarifies that the correct view, as stated in al-Arba'in, is that the two knowledges are distinct from the start, not merely reducible to the first. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the third argument to the correct view.
Pay attention to the conditional 'إذا تغاير المعلومان تغاير العلمان'.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The passage presents a claim (third argument), an objection that it reverts to first, and then the correct view with supporting evidence. Identify each part.
The supporting evidence is the expression about before/after occurrence.
3
Understand the correct view
The correct view, as in al-Arba'in, is that the two knowledges are distinct from the start, not reducible. The difference in attributes (knowledge vs. ignorance) shows their conflict.
Imam al-Razi considered this an independent aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : يمكن العلم بأنه وقع مع الجهل بأنه سيقع كما إذا علم الحادث حال حدوثه ولم يشعر به قبله أصلاً وبالعكس كما إذا علم حاله قبل حدوثه ولم يشعر به في أوانه وغير المعلوم أي ما ليس معلوماً في زمان غير المعلوم أي مغاير لما هو معلوم في ذلك الزمان وإذا تغاير المعلومان تغاير العلمان وعلى هذا فقد رجع الثالث إلى الأول والصواب كما هو في الأربعين أنه يمكن العلم بأنه سيقع مع الجهل بأنه عالم بأنه وقع بالعكس وغير المعلوم غير المعلوم فيثبت حينئذ تمايز العلمين ابتداء ويشهد لما قلناه قوله : وقد يعبر عن هذا الثالث : بأن قبل الوقوع اعتقاد أنه سيقع علم واعتقاد أنه وقع جهل وبعد الوقوع وبالعكس فتغايرا لتنافي وصفيهما أعني العلمية والجهلية كتنافي وصف المعلومية والمجهولية المعتبرتين في الوجه الثالث وقد جعله الإمام الرازي وجهاً برأسه.
The third: It is possible to know that it occurred while being ignorant that it will occur, as when one knows an event at the moment of its occurrence and did not perceive it before at all, and the opposite, as when one knows its state before its occurrence and did not perceive it at its time. And the unknown, i.e., what is not known in a time, is other than the known, i.e., different from what is known in that time. And if the objects known are different, the knowledges are different. On this basis, the third has reverted to the first. But the correct view, as in al-Arba'in, is that it is possible to know that it will occur while being ignorant that one knows that it occurred, and vice versa, and the unknown is other than the unknown. So then the distinctness of the two knowledges is established initially. And it is evidence for what we have said his statement: And it is expressed regarding this third: that before occurrence, the belief that it will occur is knowledge, and the belief that it has occurred is ignorance; and after occurrence, the opposite. So they differ due to the conflict of their two attributes, namely knowledge and ignorance, like the conflict of the attribute of being known and being unknown considered in the third aspect. And Imam al-Razi made it an independent aspect.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the distinction between knowledge of an event before and after it occurs. The argument is that one can know that something has occurred while being ignorant that it would occur, and vice versa. If the objects known are different (the future event vs. the past event), then the knowledges are different. Although some claim that this third argument reduces to the first, the correct view (according to al-Arba'in) is that the two knowledges are distinct from the start. The author supports this by citing an expression: before occurrence, believing it will occur is knowledge, while believing it has occurred is ignorance; after occurrence, the opposite is true. This shows the two knowledges differ because their attributes—knowledge and ignorance—conflict. Imam al-Razi considered this an independent aspect, not reducible.

Key Terms
الوجه الثالث
تمايز العلمين
المعلوم
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument is just a variant of the first argument and does not provide independent proof.
Correction: The correct view is that the third argument establishes distinctness of the two knowledges from the start, independent of the first.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument use to show the distinctness of the two knowledges?
A) Difference in their objects
B) Difference in their conditions
C) Difference in their subjects
D) Difference in their times

Key Takeaway
The third argument independently establishes the distinctness of two knowledges by showing that their objects differ, and this does not reduce to the first argument.

?
