Defending the Third Argument's Independence
Al-Jurjani gives two responses to the reduction claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's two responses defending the independence of the third argument from the first.

Core Idea
The third argument stands on its own by showing real separation of knowledges.

Al-Jurjani addresses a critical objection: that his third argument is merely a repetition of the first. He offers two distinct responses that clarify the logical independence of the argument. This passage is essential for understanding how kalam arguments maintain their distinctiveness and force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the structure: first response then second response.
2
Identify the two responses
Note the first response (intent of showing separation) and the second (addressing the 'by consideration' objection).
3
Understand key concepts
Focus on terms like الانفكاك (separation) and تغاير الذات (distinctness in essence).
These are central to the argument.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why a real (ذاتي) distinction matters for the argument's validity.
It prevents reduction to a mere conceptual distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فقد رجع الثالث إلى الأول قد يجاب عنه بأن المقصود من الوجه الثالث أن كلاً من العلمين ينفك عن الآخر وانفكاك الشيء عن نفسه محال فثبت تغايرهما وهو ظاهر، إلا أنه ضم إليه تغاير المتعلقين لزيادة الاستظهار ويشهد به شهادة بينة قوله وقد يغير إلخ فإنه صريح في بيان انفكاك كل منهما عن الآخر فعلى هذا لم يرجع الثالث إلى الأول . قوله: (والصواب كما هو في (الأربعين رد عليه بأنه يدل على تغاير العلمين بالاعتبار لا بالذات وهو المراد، وذلك لأن الشيء الواحد يجوز أن يكون معلوماً باعتبار ومجهولاً بآخر. وأجيب : بأن المراد أنه يمكن العلم بأنه سيقع في الجملة مع الجهل بأنه عالم بأنه وقع من كل الوجوه فعلى هذا يثبت تغاير العلمين بالذات .
His statement: 'The third has reverted to the first' may be answered by saying that the intent of the third aspect is that each of the two knowledges can be separated from the other, and the separation of a thing from itself is impossible, so their distinctness is established, and that is apparent. However, he appended to it the difference of the two objects for greater illustration, and to that testifies his statement 'and it may change etc.,' for it is explicit in explaining the separation of each from the other. On this basis, the third has not reverted to the first. His statement: 'The correct view as in al-Arba'in' is responded to by saying that it indicates the distinctness of the two knowledges by consideration, not by essence, and that is intended, because one thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another. And it was answered: that the meaning is that it is possible to know that it will occur in general while being ignorant that one knows that it occurred from all aspects; on this basis, the distinctness of the two knowledges in essence is established.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage contains two responses to the claim that the third argument reduces to the first. The first response explains that the purpose of the third aspect is to show that each knowledge can exist independently of the other; self-separation is impossible, so they must be distinct. The reference to differing objects is just for added clarity. The second response addresses the claim that the correct view in al-Arba'in indicates distinctness by consideration, not by essence. But the author argues that the intended meaning is that one can know that something will occur while being completely ignorant that one knows it has occurred, establishing a real difference in essence.

Key Terms
الرجوع
الانفكاك
الاعتبار
الذات
تغاير العلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a single thing can be known and unknown in different respects, but the argument insists on a real (ذاتي) distinction between the two knowledges.
Correction: The argument shows that the two knowledges are distinct in essence, not just in consideration.

Checkpoint
What does the first response say the purpose of the third aspect is?
A) To show the two knowledges are identical
B) To show each knowledge can be separated from the other
C) To show the objects are the same
D) To show the conditions are the same

Key Takeaway
The third argument is defended by showing that the two knowledges can be separated in reality, not just conceptually, thus it does not reduce to the first argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Jurjani insists on a real separation between knowing and being ignorant. In your own life, are there areas where you confuse having information about Islam with genuine knowledge that produces certainty? How can you bridge that gap today?
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of a thing being separated from itself. Reflect: Are there contradictions in your own character that you have not yet confronted? What would it mean to honestly acknowledge those inner separations before Allah?

Refuting Change in Divine Knowledge
Al-Jurjani refutes Abu al-Husayn's claim of change in divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that divine knowledge changes with events is impossible and leads to attributing ignorance to Allah.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge does not change; change would mean prior ignorance.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents and refutes an objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who argued that divine knowledge of temporal events must change as they occur. The refutation exposes the logical consequence: if knowledge arises with events, then before those events, Allah would not know them, implying ignorance—which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full English translation. Notice how the author first presents the opponent's view, then gives the refutation.
The passage is concise but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Identify Abu al-Husayn's claim
Abu al-Husayn claims that divine knowledge arises when an event occurs and disappears when it ends, because knowledge is conditioned on the event's occurrence.
He argues that God's essence necessitates this conditional knowledge.
3
Understand the refutation's logic
The refutation points out that if knowledge only occurs at the time of the event, then in pre-eternity God did not know that event, which is tantamount to charging God with ignorance—an impossibility.
This shows that change in knowledge implies a deficiency in the Divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أبا الحسين بعد إبطاله جواب مشايخه التزم وقوع التغير في علم الباري سبحانه وتعالى بالمتغيرات. وزعم أن ذاته تعالى تقتضي كونه عالماً بالمعلومات بشرط وقوعها فيحدث العلم بها عند وجودها ويزول عند زوالها ويحصل علم آخر، ورد عليه بأنه يلزم منه أن لا يكون الباري سبحانه وتعالى في الأزل عالماً بأحوال وجودات الحوادث وهو تجهيله له تعالى عنه.
Then Abu al-Husayn, after refuting the answer of his masters, committed to the occurrence of change in the knowledge of God—glorified and exalted is He—regarding changing things. And he claimed that His essence—exalted—necessitates that He is knowing of known things conditioned upon their occurrence, so knowledge of them occurs upon their existence and disappears upon their disappearance, and another knowledge comes to be. And it was refuted against him that it necessitates that God—glorified and exalted—is not in pre-eternity knowing the states of the existences of temporal events, which is rendering Him ignorant—exalted is He above that.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage refutes Abu al-Husayn's view that divine knowledge changes with changing events. He argued that God's essence requires knowing things only when they occur, so knowledge arises and ceases with the events. The refutation is that this would imply God was not knowing from pre-eternity the states of temporal events, which would be attributing ignorance to God—something impossible.

Key Terms
التغير في علم الله
أبو الحسين
شرط الوقوع
التجهيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for God to know temporal events as they happen, His knowledge must change to reflect their changing states.
Correction: In reality, God's eternal knowledge encompasses all events timelessly; change would imply He did not know them before they occurred, which is ignorance.

Checkpoint
What does the refutation claim is a consequence of Abu al-Husayn's view?
A) That God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging
B) That God is not in pre-eternity knowing the states of temporal events
C) That God's essence is simple
D) That knowledge is identical with the knower

Key Takeaway
Attributing change to divine knowledge entails that God did not know temporal events before they occurred, which is an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe God knows everything, but when you sin in secret, do you act as if you are hiding from Him? If you truly knew that His knowledge of that sin existed eternally before you committed it, would you still commit it? What will you do the next time you are tempted to sin alone?
?
When you make dua, do you sometimes feel you are informing God of your need rather than asking the One who already ordained it before time? This lesson says change in knowledge implies ignorance. Does your dua reflect a belief in God's eternal, unchanging knowledge? What one phrase will you add to your next dua to remind yourself of this truth?
?
Consider a painful event from your past that still bothers you. Do you believe God knew it would happen from pre-eternity and still allowed it? If yes, can you accept that His knowledge never changed—He never learned of it later? What would it mean for you to forgive someone if you fully trust that God's eternal knowledge encompasses that event?

The Group Denying Comprehensive Divine Knowledge and the Reply
Al-Jurjani discusses a group who deny God knows all things and gives the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of a group that denies God knows all things and how the reply resolves the infinite regress objection.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in relations is not impossible, saving God's comprehensive knowledge.

This passage tackles a subtle theological objection: if God knows everything, then He must know His knowledge, leading to an infinite chain. Some groups used this to deny that God knows all things. Al-Jurjani presents their argument and then gives the decisive reply that such a regress is in relations, not in existent things, and is therefore permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sect's claim
Read the Arabic text and find the statement of the sixth opposing sect. Note their interpretation of 'not knowing all'.
The sect says 'لا يعلم الجميع' meaning 'He does not know all', but they mean it in a specific sense.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
The author presents the consequence: if God knows everything, then knowing something implies knowing that knowledge, ad infinitum. See how this leads to a chain of knowledges.
This is a classic argument used to deny comprehensive divine knowledge.
3
Analyze the reply
Al-Jurjani responds that the regress is in relations (إضافات), not in actual existent things. Since knowledge is relational, such a regress is not impossible.
This reply avoids the conclusion that God does not know all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة من الفرق المخالفين : من قال : لا يعلم الجميع بمعنى سلب الكل يرفع الإيجاب الكلي لا بمعنى السلب الكلي كما زعمته الفرقة الثانية إذ لو علم كل شيء فإذا علم شيئاً علم أيضاً علمه به لأن هذا العلم شيء من الأشياء ومفهوم من المفهومات وكذا علم علمه بعلمه لأنه شيء آخر ويلزم التسلسل في العلوم والجواب : إنه تسلسل في الإضافات لا في أمور موجودة لأن العلم من قبيل الإضافة والتعلق عندنا وإنه أي تسلسل الإضافات غير ممتنع كما مر غير مرة.
The sixth among the opposing sects: one who said He does not know all, meaning the negation of the whole, which negates universal affirmation, not in the sense of universal negation as the second sect claimed. For if He knows everything, then if He knows something, He also knows His knowledge of it, because this knowledge is something among things and a concept among concepts; likewise, His knowledge of His knowledge of His knowledge, because it is another thing, and an infinite regress in knowledges would follow. And the answer: it is a regress in relations, not in existent things, because knowledge in our view is of the category of relation and attachment, and that—i.e., the regress of relations—is not impossible, as has been passed many times.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a deviant sect that denies God knows all things, interpreting 'not knowing all' as the negation of the whole (سلب الكل) rather than universal negation (السلب الكلي). The argument against this is that if God knows everything, then He would know each thing, including His knowledge of that thing, leading to an infinite regress of knowledges. The reply is that this regress is in relations (إضافات), not in existent entities, and such a regress is not impossible, as previously established. Thus, the argument does not force the conclusion that God does not know all.

Key Terms
سلب الكل
السلب الكلي
التسلسل
الإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any infinite regress is logically impossible.
Correction: A regress in relations (like knowledge relations) is not impossible; only regress in existent substances is impossible.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the problem of infinite regress in knowledges?
A) It is impossible, so God does not know all
B) It is a regress in relations, which is not impossible
C) Knowledge is not a thing
D) God knows without knowledge

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress argument against God's comprehensive knowledge is resolved by recognizing it as a regress in relations, which is permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's knowledge, do you ever limit it by applying human logic that leads to endless regress? How does this lesson challenge you to trust that God's attributes are not bound by creaturely limitations?
?
The opponent used an infinite regress to deny God's comprehensive knowledge. Have you ever rejected a truth about God because you couldn't resolve a philosophical puzzle? What does your willingness to submit to revelation say about your faith?
?
This lesson distinguishes between two types of negation. In your own life, are you tempted to take a lazy 'all-or-nothing' approach to difficult theological matters? What would it cost you to stop avoiding the nuance and wrestle with it?

Knowledge as an Added Attribute: The Mu'tazila Objection and Reply
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the Mu'tazila first objection to knowledge as an added attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that God's knowledge would be similar to human knowledge and how the reply turns the objection back on them using the concept of 'Alamiyya.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila objection fails because it applies equally to any attribute, including their own.

This passage from al-Jurjani addresses a key controversy: whether God's knowledge is an added attribute. The Mu'tazila argue that if God's knowledge relates to the same objects as ours, they would be similar, forcing temporality. Al-Jurjani presents a powerful reply, showing the objection backfires on the Mu'tazila themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice the text cuts off; the implied answer is that 'Alamiyya is a relation of the essence.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection and its reasoning
Find the objection: if God's knowledge and our knowledge relate to the same object from the same aspect, they are similar, and similars share in eternity or temporality.
The objection aims to force that God's knowledge would be temporal, which is impossible.
3
See how the reply turns the objection
The responder says the same reasoning applies to 'Alamiyya (knowledgeability). If the Mu'tazila have an answer for 'Alamiyya, they can use it for knowledge.
This shows the objection is symmetrical and not specific to knowledge.
4
Reflect on the incomplete conclusion
The text ends with 'His knowledgeability is the relation of the essence.' This suggests the Mu'tazila answer is that 'Alamiyya is not an added attribute but a relation, which would likewise apply to knowledge.
The actual answer would be that knowledge is also a relation, but that contradicts the Mu'tazila position, proving their objection invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : العلم صفة زائدة على ذاته تعالى قائمة به لما مر من بيان زيادة الصفات على الإجمال وأنكره المعتزلة لوجوه الأول : لو كان له تعالى علم فإذا تعلق بشيء وتعلق علمنا به فقد تعلقا به من وجه واحد وهو تعلق العلوم بمعلوماتها إما إجمالا أو تفصيلا فيلزم حينئذ تماثلهما لأن كل علمين تعلقا بمعلوم واحد من جهة واحدة فهما متماثلان ويلزم إما قدمهما معا أو حدوثهما معا لأن التماثلات يجب اشتراكها في اللوازم فإن قيل في جوابهم : هذا لازم عليكم في العالمية فإنه إذا تعلق عالميته تعالى بشيء وتعلق به عالميتنا من وجه واحد لزم تماثلهما واشتراكهما في القدم والحدوث فما هو جوابكم في العالمية فهو جوابنا في العلم . قلنا : لهم أن يقولوا في دفع هذا النقض: عالميته تعلق الذات
A notification: Knowledge is an attribute additional to His essence—exalted—subsisting in Him, due to what has passed of the statement of the addition of attributes in summary. And the Mu'tazila denied it for reasons. The first: If He—exalted—has knowledge, and it is related to a thing and our knowledge is related to it, then they have related to it from one aspect—which is the relation of knowledges to their objects, either in summary or in detail—then their similarity would follow, because any two knowledges related to one object from one aspect are similar. And then it follows either both are eternal or both are temporal, because similars must share in their concomitants. If it is said in their answer: This is necessary for you regarding 'Alamiyya (knowledgeability), for if His knowledgeability—exalted—is related to a thing and our knowledgeability is related to it from one aspect, then their similarity and sharing in eternity and temporality would follow. So whatever your answer is in 'Alamiyya, it is our answer in knowledge. We said: They may say in repelling this objection: His knowledgeability is the relation of the essence...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage asserts that knowledge is an attribute added to God's essence, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Their first objection is that if God's knowledge and our knowledge both relate to the same thing from the same aspect, they would be similar, and if similar, they would share in being either both eternal or both temporal—which is absurd. The response to this objection is that the same reasoning would apply to the attribute of 'Alamiyya (being Knowing). If God's knowing and our knowing both relate to the same thing, they would be similar and thus share in eternity/temporality. The Mu'tazila must have an answer to this in the case of 'Alamiyya that does not lead to absurdity, and that same answer can be used for 'ilm (knowledge). The author suggests that the answer is that 'Alamiyya is a relation of the essence, not an additional attribute, but the text is cut off. The point is that the objection fails because it applies to any attribute, and the Mu'tazila themselves hold that 'Alamiyya is not a separate attribute.

Key Terms
صفة زائدة
تماثل
العالمية
تعلق الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two knowledges relate to the same object, they must be similar and share properties like eternity/temporality.
Correction: Similarity only follows if the relation is from the same aspect, but even then, the objection applies symmetrically to any attribute, showing the conclusion is not forced.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's first objection to knowledge being an added attribute?
A) It leads to an infinite regress
B) It would make God's knowledge similar to human knowledge, implying temporality
C) It contradicts divine simplicity
D) It is not mentioned in revelation

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection that God's knowledge would be similar to human knowledge is refuted by showing it applies to any attribute, leaving the Mu'tazila no consistent escape.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazila objected to God's knowledge being like human knowledge, did you feel a sense of relief that God's knowledge is infinitely superior? But honestly, in what areas of your life do you secretly assume that God's knowledge is limited like yours, causing you to worry or doubt His ability to handle your affairs? Will you today repent of that assumption and trust His unlimited knowledge?
?
The argument shows that the Mu'tazila were forced into inconsistency. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that, upon honest reflection, lead to contradictions? What would it cost you to abandon those beliefs for a consistent understanding based on revelation?
?
If you had been in that debate, would you have been able to see the flaw in the Mu'tazila argument immediately? What does that reveal about your own humility in learning theological intricacies? How can you cultivate a deeper study of 'ilm al-kalam so that you are not swayed by shallow arguments?

The Objection of Infinite Regress from Divine Knowledge
The sixth sect's objection that divine knowledge necessitates infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth sect's argument that divine omniscience entails an infinite regress of knowledges.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge of everything seems to require an infinite chain of knowing.

The sixth opposing sect presents a logical challenge: if Allah knows everything, then His knowledge must include knowledge of His own knowledge, leading to an infinite regress. This lesson unpacks their objection and the conceptual problem it raises.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by a conclusion.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: if Allah knows everything, then He knows His own knowledge, and that knowledge is also a thing to be known, leading to an infinite regress.
The key term is 'تسلسل' (infinite regress).
3
Reflect on the problem
Consider why this regress is problematic for the claim of omniscience. The objection challenges the coherence of divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة من الفرق المخالفين : ( من قال : لا يعلم الجميع بمعنى سلب الكل يرفع الإيجاب الكلي ( لا ) بمعنى ( السلب الكلي ( كما زعمته الفرقة الثانية ( إذ لو علم كل شيء فإذا علم شيئاً علم أيضاً (علمه به لأن هذا العلم شيء من الأشياء ومفهوم من المفهومات ) وكذا علم علمه بعلمه ( لأنه شيء آخر ( ويلزم التسلسل في العلوم ( والجواب : إنه تسلسل في الإضافات لا في أمور موجودة لأن العلم من قبيل الإضافة والتعلق عندنا وإنه ) أي تسلسل الإضافات ( غير ممتنع كما مر غير مرة قبل نقول : ( كيف) ) يلزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة على تقدير كون العلم صفة حقيقية (و) الحال ( أنه قد يكون علمه بعلمه نفس علمه كما ذهب إليه الإمام القاضي ) فإنهما قالا : كل شيئين لا انفكاك العلم بهما كالعلم بالشيء والعلم بالعلم به وكالعلم بالتضاد والاختلاف فقد يتعلق بهما علم واحد كما سلف في مباحث العلم من الموقف الثالث .
The sixth of the opposing sects: (Those who said: He does not know all things in the sense of negating the universal affirmative, not in the sense of universal negation, as the second sect claimed. For if He knows everything, then if He knows something, He also knows His knowledge of it because this knowledge is one of the things and a concept among concepts. Likewise, His knowledge of His knowledge by His knowledge, because it is another thing, and an infinite regress in knowledges follows. The answer: It is a regress in relations, not in existent things, because knowledge is of the type of relation and attachment with us, and it (i.e., regress of relations) is not impossible as has been mentioned numerous times before. We say: How does the regress follow in existent things on the assumption that knowledge is a real attribute, while it may be that His knowledge of His knowledge is the same as His knowledge, as al-Imam al-Qadi held? For they said: Two things that are inseparable in knowledge, like knowledge of something and knowledge of the knowledge of it, and like knowledge of opposition and difference, may be related to by a single knowledge as previously mentioned in the discussions of knowledge in the third observatory.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a group (the sixth sect) who claim that if Allah is omniscient, then His knowledge leads to an infinite regress because knowing something requires knowing that He knows it, and so on. The response distinguishes between regress in existent entities and regress in relations (iḍāfāt). Since knowledge is considered an attribute of relation, the regress in relations is not impossible. Additionally, they cite the view of al-Qadi that knowledge of knowledge could be the same knowledge when the two things are inseparable, thus avoiding an actual infinite chain.

Key Terms
تسلسل
علوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sixth sect, why does divine knowledge lead to infinite regress?
A) Because knowledge is an attribute.
B) Because knowing something requires knowing that you know it.
C) Because Allah's knowledge is limited.
D) Because knowledge is a relation.

Key Takeaway
The sixth sect uses infinite regress to challenge the coherence of divine omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if your thoughts are hidden from Allah? What would change if you truly believed that His knowledge of your inner state is not a separate 'thing' but immediate and complete?
?
The objection assumes that knowledge is a 'thing' to be known. In your own life, do you treat Allah's knowledge as a distant abstraction or as something present and personal? How would thinking differently affect your next sinful thought?
?
Infinite regress is used here to challenge divine omniscience. Have you ever harbored any doubt about Allah's all-encompassing knowledge? What step can you take today to strengthen your certainty in this attribute?

The Response: Regress in Relations, Not in Entities
The answer that the regress is in relations and is not impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī response that the regress is in relations (iḍāfāt), not in existent things, and that this type of regress is permissible.

Core Idea
Regress in relations is not impossible; only regress in existent entities is problematic.

The response to the sixth sect's objection clarifies that the regress occurs in relations, not in actual existent things. Since knowledge is relational, this regress is acceptable. Additionally, the view of al-Qadi that knowledge of knowledge may be identical to knowledge further defuses the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic passage. Note the key phrase 'تسلسل في الإضافات' (regress in relations).
2
Understand the distinction
Identify the difference between regress in existent things and regress in relations. The text says the latter is not impossible.
The term 'إضافات' (relations) is central.
3
Consider the alternative view
Note the view of al-Qadi: knowledge of knowledge may be the same knowledge if the two objects are inseparable, thus avoiding regress entirely.
This is a second line of response.

Source Text

This passage presents an objection from a group (the sixth sect) who claim that if Allah is omniscient, then His knowledge leads to an infinite regress because knowing something requires knowing that He knows it, and so on. Correction: Only regress in existent things is impossible; regress in relations is acceptable.

A) Regress in existent things.
B) Regress in relations.
C) Regress in attributes.
D) Regress in actions.

Key Takeaway
The objection's regress is only in relations, which is rationally acceptable, and may even be avoided if the knowledges are identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's knowledge, do you naturally picture it as a 'thing' or as an infinite relation? How does reducing it to a 'thing' lead to impossibility, and what does that reveal about your own assumptions about God?
?
If knowledge can be of itself without a new act, what does that tell you about the simplicity and perfection of Allah's knowledge? How should that humble your attempts to fully comprehend Him?
?
This lesson shows that not all infinite chains are problematic. Where in your own faith or life have you assumed something is impossible (like infinite mercy or forgiveness) when it is actually possible? What would it mean to trust in the infinite relational attributes of Allah starting today?

The Mu'tazila Objection and Counter-Argument
Mu'tazila objection and Ash'ari refutation via ʿālimiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazila objection against knowledge as an additional attribute and how the Ash'ari turn it back using the attribute of being-knowing (al-ʿālimiyya).

Core Idea
If the Mu'tazila objection works for knowledge, it also works for being-knowing.

The Mu'tazila argue that if divine knowledge is an additional attribute, then when it relates to an object it would be similar to human knowledge, forcing both to be eternal or originated. The Ash'ari immediately counter that the same argument applies to the attribute of being-knowing (ʿālimiyya), which the Mu'tazila accept. This lesson presents the initial exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation. Note the structure: the objection, the counter-objection, and the Mu'tazila response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two arguments
First, identify the Mu'tazila's objection: that two knowledges attached to the same thing from one aspect would be similar and thus share in eternity or origination. Second, identify the Ash'ari counter: that the same reasoning applies to 'ālimiyya, which the Mu'tazila accept.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila response
The Mu'tazila try to avoid the counter by distinguishing 'ālimiyya as the attachment of the essence to the known, while human 'ālimiyya is attachment of knowledge to the known, so they are not from one aspect.
This sets up the next lesson's refutation.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Ask yourself: Why is this exchange important? It shows that the Mu'tazila objection is not decisive, as it can be turned on itself.
This lays the groundwork for the Ash'ari deeper refutation.

تنبيه : العلم صفة زائدة ) على ذاته تعالى قائمة به ( لما مر من بيان زيادة الصفات على الإجمال ) وأنكره المعتزلة لوجوه الأول : لو كان له تعالى علم فإذا تعلق : بشيء وتعلق علمنا به فقد تعلقا به من وجه واحد ) وهو تعلق العلوم بمعلوماتها إما إجمالا أو تفصيلا فيلزم حينئذ (تماثلهما ) لأن كل علمين تعلقا بمعلوم واحد من جهة واحدة فهما متماثلان ( ويلزم ) إما ( قدمهما ) معا ( أو حدوثهما معا، لأن التماثلات يجب اشتراكها في اللوازم ( فإن قيل) في جوابهم : ( هذا لازم عليكم في العالمية ) فإنه إذا تعلق عالميته تعالى بشيء وتعلق به عالميتنا من وجه واحد لزم تماثلهما واشتراكهما في القدم والحدوث فما هو (جوابكم) في العالمية فهو جوابنا ) في العلم . (قلنا : ( لهم أن يقولوا في دفع هذا النقض: (عالميته تعلق الذات) بالمعلوم ) وعالميتنا تعلق العلم بالمعلوم فليسا ) أي هذان التعلقان ( من وجه واحد ) فلا يكونان متماثلين ( والجواب أنه لا يلزم من الاشتراك من وجه التعلق) وطريقه ( التماثل إذ المختلفات ) بل المتضادات ) تشترك في لازم واحد فإن قيل : ( إذا لم يدل ما ذكرناه على تماثل العلمين (فيم يعرف تماثل العلوم ؟ قلنا: إن كان ) هناك ( طريق آخر إلى معرفة ) تماثلها ( فذلك ) يتوصل به إليها ( وإلا توقف ) كما في سائر الأشياء التي لا سبيل لنا إلى معرفتها (سلمنا التماثل لكن لا يجب الاشتراك في القدم والحدوث ) لأن المتماثلات قد تختلف فيهما (كما في (الوجود) فإن وجوده تعالى قديم ووجود الممكنات حادث مع تماثلهما، وسره أن المتماثلين لا بد أن يتمايزا بشيء فربما كان ذلك الشيء مبدأ لحكم مختص . قوله : (سلمنا التماثل إلخ) لا شك أن تسليم التماثل تنزلي لإبطال رأي المعتزلة وإلا فليس كمثله شيء في ذاته وصفاته . قوله : ( كما في الوجود) قال في حواشي التجريد : المتكلمون القائلون بأن الوجود مشترك متواطئ حكموا بأن الوجودات متماثلة .
Note: Knowledge is an additional attribute (ṣifa zā'ida) upon His essence, exalted, subsisting in Him, due to what has passed of the explanation of the addition of attributes in summary. And the Mu'tazila denied it with reasons. The first: If He, exalted, had knowledge, then when it is attached to something and our knowledge is attached to it, the two attachments are from one aspect, namely the attachment of knowledges to their objects either in detail or in summary. Then similarity (tamāthul) necessarily follows because any two knowledges attached to one object from one aspect are similar. And it necessitates either both being eternal or both being originated, because similars must share in concomitants. If it is said in their answer: This is necessary for you concerning being-knowing (al-'ālimiyya), for if His being-knowing, exalted, is attached to something and our being-knowing is attached to it from one aspect, their similarity and sharing in eternity and origination would follow. So what is your answer concerning being-knowing is our answer concerning knowledge. We say: They may say in repelling this refutation: His being-knowing is the attachment of the essence to the known, and our being-knowing is the attachment of knowledge to the known, so these two attachments are not from one aspect, so they are not similar. The answer: That sharing in the mode of attachment does not necessitate similarity, for different things, even opposites, share in one common property. If it is said: If what we have mentioned does not indicate the similarity of the two knowledges, then by what is the similarity of knowledges known? We say: If there is another way to know their similarity, then that is the means to it; otherwise, we stop, as in other things for which we have no means of knowledge. We grant similarity, but sharing in eternity and origination is not necessary, because similars may differ in them, as in existence; for His existence, exalted, is eternal and the existence of possibles is originated while they are similar. The secret is that two similars must be distinguished by something, and perhaps that thing is a basis for a specific ruling. His saying: 'We grant similarity etc.' There is no doubt that granting similarity is a concession to refute the opinion of the Mu'tazila; otherwise, there is nothing like Him in His essence and attributes. His saying: 'As in existence' – he said in the glosses of al-Tajrīd: The theologians who hold that existence is common and univocal ruled that existences are similar.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the first Mu'tazila objection against knowledge being an additional attribute. The Mu'tazila argue that if divine knowledge is additional, then when it relates to an object and human knowledge also relates to the same object from the same aspect, the two knowledges would be similar, and thus both must be either eternal or originated, which is impossible for divine knowledge. The Ash'ari first turn the objection back by pointing out that the same argument applies to the concept of 'being-knowing' (al-ʿālimiyya): if God's being-knowing and our being-knowing relate to the same object, they would also be similar. The Mu'tizala respond that God's being-knowing is the attachment of the essence to the known, while our being-knowing is the attachment of knowledge to the known, so they are from different aspects. The Ash'ari then argue that even if the mode of attachment is shared, that does not entail similarity, as different things can share common properties. They further argue that even if similarity is granted, it does not force shared eternity or origination, giving the example of existence: God's existence is eternal while contingent existences are originated, yet they are considered similar in the sense of existence being a univocal concept. The marginal notes emphasize that the concession of similarity is for argument's sake only, and that the theologians hold existences to be similar.

Key Terms
تماثل
عالمية
تعلق الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's first objection against knowledge being an additional attribute?
A) It leads to infinite regress
B) It leads to similarity between divine and human knowledge, forcing both to be eternal or originated
C) It contradicts the Quran
D) It implies multiple gods

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection that divine and human knowledge would be similar and share in eternality is refuted by showing the same argument applies to their own attribute of being-knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study theological disputes like this, do you find yourself automatically siding with a particular school without examining the logical consistency of your own position? In what area of your faith might you be holding a view that, if applied consistently, would also apply to another belief you reject?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to distinguish between God's ʿālimiyya and human ʿālimiyya to avoid contradiction. Have you ever made a similar distinction in your own reasoning—perhaps about Allah's attributes versus human attributes—without fully thinking it through? Are you willing to accept the full implications of your beliefs even when they challenge your comfort?
?
This passage shows how a logical argument can be turned back on its proponent. When someone challenges your understanding of Islam, do you seek to understand their reasoning thoroughly or simply dismiss it? How can you practice intellectual honesty like the Ash'ari did here, by testing arguments against your own views?

The Ash'ari Refutation: Sharing Mode and the Example of Existence
Ash'ari arguments against similarity entailment and the existence example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sharing a mode of attachment does not entail similarity, and how the example of existence shows that similars can differ in temporal origin.

Core Idea
Similars can differ in eternity and origination, as seen in existence.

The Ash'ari deepen the refutation by arguing that even if the attachments share a mode, that does not force similarity; different things can share common properties. Moreover, even if similarity is granted, it does not necessitate shared temporality. The example of existence—God's existence eternal, contingent existence originated, yet both are called 'existence'—shows that similars can differ in temporal properties. This lesson unpacks these final arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the shift from the previous exchange to a new argument. The Ash'ari now give two independent points.
2
Understand the first argument: sharing mode does not imply similarity
The Ash'ari argue that sharing a mode of attachment does not necessitate similarity, because even different or opposite things can share a common property. For example, opposites can share the property of being a quality.
This refutes the Mu'tazila's inference.
3
Analyze the second argument: similars can differ in temporality
Even if we grant similarity, it does not force shared eternity or origination. The example of existence: God's existence is eternal, contingent existence is originated, yet they are considered similar in being 'existence.' The secret is that similars must be distinguished by something, which can be a basis for a different ruling.
4
Reflect on the concession
The marginal note clarifies that granting similarity is a dialectical concession; in reality, God is unlike anything in essence and attributes.
This shows the Ash'ari are careful not to compromise divine uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Ash'ari conceded similarity for the sake of argument, not from belief. Are there aspects of your faith where you have conceded too much to doubts or to others' arguments, downplaying the absolute uniqueness of Allah? What is one specific truth about Allah's essence or attributes that you have avoided fully embracing?
?
If two similar things are distinguished by a feature that determines their ruling, what distinguishing feature of your faith should set you apart in Allah's sight? Are you actively cultivating that feature today, or are you letting it fade into mere similarity with those around you?

Second Mu'tazila Objection: Infinite Knowledges and the Response
Mu'tazila objection and Ash'ari response distinguishing essence from attachments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians respond to the Mu'tazila objection that Allah's additional knowledge would entail infinite distinct knowledges, by distinguishing between the essence of knowledge and its relational attachments.

Core Idea
One knowledge can relate to infinite objects because multiplicity lies in attachments, not essence.

The Mu'tazila argued that if Allah's knowledge is an additional attribute, then since He knows infinite things, He must have infinite distinct knowledges, which is absurd. The Ash'ari response is a clever distinction: the multiplicity is not in the knowledge itself but in its relational attachments (ta'alluqat), which can be infinite without multiplying the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two-part structure: first the objection, then the answer.
The objection is straightforward: different objects require different knowledges.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the text distinguishes between 'essence of knowledge' and 'relational attachments'. Understand why the attachments can be infinite.
The term 'iḍāfiyya' indicates they are relational, not substantial.
3
Connect to broader theological principle
The response relies on the permissibility of regress in conceptual matters, which is not subject to the proof of correspondence.
This is a key principle in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني ) من الوجوه : (إنه تعالى عالم بما لا نهاية له ( فإذا فرض أن علمه زائد على ذاته ( فيلزم ) أن يكون له ( علوم ) موجودة (غير متناهية ضرورة أن العلم بشيء غير العلم بشيء آخر. (والجواب أن التعدد في التعلقات العلمية (وهي إضافية ) فيجوز لا تناهيها وأما ذات العلم فواحدة . قوله : ( والجواب أن التعدد في التعلقات العلمية) قد سبق البحث في جواز تعلق علم واحد بالمعلومات المتعددة وأنه مبني على أن العلم صفة حقيقة ذات إضافة وأن قيام علم مقام علوم مختلفة لا يستلزم جواز قيام صفة واحدة مقام صفات مختلفة الأجناس حتى يلزم الاستغناء عن تعدد الصفات فمن أراد التفصيل فلينظر فيما سبق . قوله : ( وهي إضافية فيجوز لا تناهيها صريح فيما مر منا مراراً من أن التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية وإن لم تكن اعتبارية محضة ليس بمستحيل، إذ لا يجري فيه برهان التطبيق باتفاق الفريقين . اللهم إلا في بعض المواد لا لبرهان التطبيق بل لدليل آخر .
The second of the objections: (He, exalted, knows infinite things. So if it is assumed that His knowledge is additional to His essence, then it necessitates that He has existing knowledges that are infinite, necessarily because knowledge of something is not the same as knowledge of another thing. The answer: Multiplicity is in the scientific attachments (al-taʿalluqāt al-ʿilmiyya), which are relational (iḍāfiyya), so it is permissible for them to be infinite, but the essence of knowledge is one. His saying: (And the answer that multiplicity is in the scientific attachments) – the investigation has already preceded on the permissibility of one knowledge attaching to multiple objects, and that it is based on knowledge being a real attribute with relation, and that one knowledge taking the place of different knowledges does not necessitate the permissibility of one attribute taking the place of different kinds of attributes such that independence from multiplicity of attributes would follow. So whoever wants detail should look at what preceded. His saying: (And they are relational, so it is permissible for them to be infinite) is clear in what we have repeatedly mentioned that regress in conceptual matters, even if not purely conceptual, is not impossible, because the proof of correspondence (burhān al-taṭbīq) does not apply to it by agreement of the two parties. Except in some matters not due to the proof of correspondence but due to another proof.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This is the second Mu'tazila objection. They argue that since Allah knows infinite things, if His knowledge were an additional attribute, He would have infinite distinct knowledges, which is impossible. The Ash'ari response is that the multiplicity is not in the essence of knowledge but in its relational attachments (taʿalluqāt), which are relational (iḍāfiyya) and can be infinite without multiplicity in the attribute itself. The essence of knowledge remains one. The marginal notes elaborate that this view is based on the permissibility of one knowledge relating to multiple objects, and that such a regress in conceptual matters is not impossible.

Key Terms
تعلقات علمية
إضافية
ذات العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infinite objects of knowledge require infinite attributes in Allah.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that one attribute (knowledge) with multiple relational attachments suffices for infinite objects.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, how can one knowledge cover infinite objects?
A) The essence of knowledge multiplies
B) The attachments are relational and can be infinite
C) Knowledge is not real but mental
D) Allah does not know particulars

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response preserves divine simplicity by allowing one knowledge to relate to infinite objects through relational attachments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's knowledge encompassing every detail of your life, does the fact that His knowledge is one in essence but relationally multiple make you feel more intimately known or more distant? How does that shape your daily awareness of being before Him?
?
If Allah's knowledge of your sins is just a relational attachment from His single essence, does that lessen the weight of accountability or make it more profound? What change in your repentance is needed to align with this truth?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that infinite objects require infinite attributes. Have you ever assumed that God's greatness must be complex in a human sense? How can you train your heart to accept a simplicity that transcends multiplicity?

Third Mu'tazila Objection: Regress of Knowledge of Knowledge and the Response that It is in Relations
The Mu'tazila object that if knowledge is additional, knowledge of that knowledge leads to infinite regress; the answer is it is regress in relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that knowledge of knowledge leads to an infinite regress and the Ash'ari response that the regress is only in relations, not in existent attributes.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is avoided because the regress applies only to relations, not to real existents.

The Mu'tazila argued that if Allah's knowledge is an additional attribute, then His knowledge of that knowledge would require yet another attribute, leading to an infinite chain of attributes. The Ash'ari response is that the regress occurs only in the relations (iḍāfāt) of a single knowledge to its objects, not in the essence of knowledge itself. This subtle distinction preserves the simplicity of the divine attribute while accounting for self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: if knowledge is additional, then knowledge of it must also be additional, leading to infinite regress.
The Mu'tazila are pressing a logical consequence of taking attributes as additional.
2
Analyze the answer
Focus on the response: 'It is in relations.' Understand that the regress is dismissed as involving only relations (iḍāfāt) rather than real, existent attributes. The single knowledge has multiple relations.
Relations can be infinite without implying an infinite number of existing things.
3
Understand 'bi al-iʿtibār'
The answer says the knowledge of knowledge differs 'by consideration not by essence' (bi al-iʿtibār dūna al-dhāt). This is a key Ash'ari move: conceptual distinctions do not multiply realities.
This concept is used elsewhere to handle similar problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث ) منها : ( يلزم ) على تقدير كونه عالماً بعلم أن يكون ) علمه بعلمه ) أيضاً زائداً على علمه ) وتتسلسل ( العلوم الموجودة إلى مالا نهاية له . ( والجواب : إنه في في الإضافات ) لأن علمه واحد وله تعلقات بمعلومات لا تتناهى من جملتها علمه الذي يخالفه بالاعتبار دون الذات .
The third of them: (It necessitates, on the assumption that He is knowing by knowledge, that His knowledge of His knowledge is also additional to His knowledge, and the existing knowledges regress infinitely. The answer: It is in relations, because His knowledge is one and it has attachments to infinite objects, among which is His knowledge that differs by consideration not by essence.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
The third objection is that if knowledge is an additional attribute, then Allah's knowledge of His own knowledge would be yet another attribute, leading to an infinite regress of existing attributes. The response is that the regress is only in the relations (iḍāfāt), not in the essence of knowledge. His knowledge is one essence, but it has attachments to infinite things, including His knowledge itself, but that attachment is a conceptual distinction (bi al-iʿtibār), not a real distinction in the attribute. Thus, no actual infinite series of existent attributes arises.

Key Terms
علمه بعلمه
إضافات
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if Allah knows His own knowledge, that requires a second, separate knowledge attribute, leading to an infinite regress.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that the 'knowledge of knowledge' is just a relation (iḍāfa) of the same knowledge to itself, not a new existent attribute. Infinite relations are permissible because they don't multiply realities.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari response avoid an infinite regress of knowledges?
A) By denying that Allah knows His knowledge
B) By stating that the regress is only in relations, not in existent things
C) By limiting Allah's knowledge
D) By claiming knowledge is not an attribute

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress objection is resolved by recognizing that knowledge of knowledge involves a relation (iḍāfa) of a single attribute, not a distinct attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own knowledge of your own thoughts, do you experience a chain of 'knowledge of knowledge'? How does this lesson change your understanding of that experience?
?
The Ash'ari response distinguishes real attributes from mental relations. Is there an area of your faith where you have conflated a conceptual distinction with a real separation? What would change if you applied this distinction?
?
If Allah's knowledge of His own knowledge is a single relation, not a separate attribute, what does that imply about your own attempts to know God? Are you content with a God who is beyond your full comprehension, or do you seek to reduce Him to what your mind can fully grasp?

Fourth Mu'tazila Objection: The Quranic Argument and the Response
The Mu'tazila cite the Quran to argue that if Allah has knowledge, then there is a higher knower; the response denies the implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's fourth objection based on the Quranic verse 'Fawqa kulli dhi ʿilmin ʿalīm' and the Ash'ari response that denies the implication.

Core Idea
A Quranic verse does not prove a higher knower above Allah; the argument fails by parity of reasoning.

The Mu'tazila often used scriptural arguments to challenge Ash'ari theology. Here they cite a verse from Surah Yusuf to argue that if Allah has knowledge, then there must be a greater knower above Him. The Ash'ari response exposes a flaw in their analogy, showing that equality in attachment does not imply similarity. This lesson unpacks the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the objection and response. Notice the compact style: the objection is stated, then the proof from the Quran is given, followed by the response.
The response is a bit dense; focus on the logic of equality in attachment versus similarity.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The Mu'tazila claim: if Allah has knowledge (i.e., is a possessor of knowledge), then there must be someone more knowledgeable above Him, which is impossible. They base this on the Quranic verse 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' (12:76).
This is a literal interpretation of the verse, implying a hierarchy.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The response argues by analogy: Allah's essence is equal to our knowledge in terms of attachment (i.e., both are attached to objects of knowledge), but that does not make His essence similar to our knowledge. Similarly, equality in attachment between His knowledge and our knowledge does not imply similarity. Thus, the Mu'tazila's inference is invalid.
The commentator adds 'wa fihi ma fihi' suggesting the response is not fully accepted but shows inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the deeper lesson
This exchange illustrates that scriptural arguments in theology must be carefully interpreted. A verse that seems to imply a hierarchy can be understood differently. The Ash'ari approach avoids attributing imperfection to Allah by denying the implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان ( تعالى ( ذا علم لكان فوقه عليم واللازم باطل اتفاقاً بيان الملازمة قوله تعالى وفوق كل ذي علم عليم الله . تساوى ذات الله تعالى علمنا في التعلق فيلزم أن تكون ذاته تعالى مثلاً لعلومنا وأنه محال فإذا لم تثبت المماثلة بين ذاته تعالى وعلمنا مع المساواة في التعلق فكذا بین علمه وعلمنا وفيه ما فيه .
The fourth: If He, exalted, were possessor of knowledge, then above Him would be a knower, and the consequent is false by consensus. The proof of the implication is His statement, may He be exalted, 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' (12:76). The essence of Allah, exalted, is equal to our knowledge in attachment, so it would necessitate that His essence, exalted, be similar to our knowledges, which is impossible. So if similarity between His essence and our knowledge is not established despite equality in attachment, likewise between His knowledge and our knowledge. And there is something in it.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
The fourth objection uses the Quranic verse 'Fawqa kulli dhi ʿilmin ʿalīm' from Surah Yusuf. The Mu'tazila argue that if Allah has knowledge, then there must be someone more knowledgeable above Him, which is absurd. The response turns the argument back on the Mu'tazila by comparing the relation: just as the equality in attachment between Allah's essence and our knowledge does not prove similarity, so too equality in attachment between His knowledge and our knowledge does not prove similarity. The argument is not fully accepted, but it shows the inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's position.

Key Terms
ذو علم
عليم
مساواة
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Quranic verse 'Fawqa kulli dhi ʿilmin ʿalīm' implies a literal hierarchy of knowledge, thus Allah cannot be omniscient if He has knowledge.
Correction: The verse is understood by Ahl al-Sunnah as a general statement that there is always someone more knowledgeable except Allah, who is the ultimate All-Knowing. It does not imply a chain above Allah. The response shows that equality in attachment does not prove similarity, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's basis for the fourth objection?
A) Logical reasoning
B) A Quranic verse
C) Consensus
D) Prophetic tradition

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's scriptural argument is refuted by showing that equality in attachment does not necessitate similarity, preserving Allah's uniqueness while affirming His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazila objection, did you feel a momentary doubt about Allah's uniqueness? How does the response here expose your own tendency to measure Allah by human standards? Will you actively train your mind to reject such analogies from today?
?
The Ash'ari response uses logical reasoning to protect Allah's transcendence. But is your heart truly convinced by this argument, or do you rely on it as a mental escape from deeper questions? Take a moment to ask Allah to increase your certainty and remove any hidden doubts.
?
The commentator's 'wa fihi ma fihi' shows that even great scholars acknowledge the limits of their responses. In what area of your faith have you been overly confident in your own understanding, without the humility to say 'there is something in it'? Commit to studying more deeply with a teacher or book instead of settling for surface answers.

The Objection from Relation of Knowledge
Presents the first objection and initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the additionalness of divine knowledge and the counter-argument that similarity in relation does not entail similarity in essence.

Core Idea
Sharing a relation does not make two things similar.

This lesson introduces the first major objection to the view that God's knowledge is additional to His essence. The objection argues that if God's knowledge is additional, it must relate to objects just as human knowledge does, implying similarity. The response challenges the inference from shared relation to shared essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Carefully read the passage from 'And our statement' to 'as in other things we have no way to know'. Note the structure: objection, answer, further question, final response.
The objection is based on the relation of knowledge to its object.
2
Understand the logical flaw
The reply argues that sharing one aspect (relation) does not imply overall similarity; even opposites share necessary concomitants. Recognize this as a key distinction.
3
Consider the epistemic challenge
The objection asks how similarity is known if not through relation. The answer is either another method exists or we suspend judgment. Notice the dialectical humility.
This shows the limits of human reasoning about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعالميتنا تعلق العلم بالمعلوم فليسا أي هذان التعلقان من وجه واحد فلا يكونان متماثلين والجواب أنه لا يلزم من الاشتراك من وجه التعلق وطريقه التماثل إذ المختلفات بل المتضادات تشترك في لازم واحد فإن قيل إذا لم يدل ما ذكرناه على تماثل العلمين فيم يعرف تماثل العلوم قلنا إن كان هناك طريق آخر إلى معرفة تماثلها فذلك يتوصل به إليها وإلا توقف كما في سائر الأشياء التي لا سبيل لنا إلى معرفتها سلمنا التماثل لكن لا يجب الاشتراك في القدم والحدوث لأن المتماثلات قد تختلف فيهما كما في الوجود فإن وجوده تعالى قديم ووجود الممكنات حادث مع تماثلهما وسره أن المتماثلين لا بد أن يتمايزا بشيء فربما كان ذلك الشيء مبدأ لحكم مختص. قوله سلمنا التماثل إلخ لا شك أن تسليم التماثل تنزلي لإبطال رأي المعتزلة وإلا فليس كمثله تعالى شيء في ذاته وصفاته. قوله كما في الوجود قال في حواشي التجريد المتكلمون القائلون بأن الوجود مشترك متواطئ حكموا بأن الوجودات متماثلة.
And our statement: the relation of knowledge to the known is not the same, i.e., these two relations are not from one aspect, so they are not similar. The answer: it does not follow from the commonality in one aspect of relation and its path that they are similar, because different things, even opposites, share in a single necessary concomitant. If it is said: if what we mentioned does not indicate the similarity of the two knowledges, then how is the similarity of knowledges known? We say: if there is another way to know their similarity, then it is reached through it; otherwise it is suspended, as in other things we have no way to know. We concede similarity, but it is not necessary to share in pre-eternity and temporality, because similars may differ in them, as in existence: His existence, exalted, is pre-eternal and the existence of contingent things is temporal despite their similarity. And the secret of it is that two similars must be distinguished by something, and perhaps that thing is the source of a specific ruling. His saying: 'We concede similarity etc.' No doubt that the concession of similarity is a dialectical step to refute the Mu'tazila's opinion; otherwise, there is nothing like Him, exalted, in His essence and attributes. His saying: 'as in existence': He said in the glosses of al-Tajrid: The theologians who say that existence is univocal (mushtarak mutawāṭiʼ) ruled that existences are similar.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the view that God's knowledge is additional to His essence. The objection argues that if God's knowledge is additional, then it must be related to its objects in a specific way, and since the relation of human knowledge to its objects is similar, the two knowledges would be similar. The answer begins by rejecting the inference: sharing in one aspect of relation does not imply similarity in essence, as even opposites share in some necessary concomitants. The objection then asks how similarity is established if not this way. The response is that if there is another method, then use it; otherwise we suspend judgment. Then a concession is made: even if we grant similarity (dialectically for refutation), it does not necessitate sharing in pre-eternity and temporality. The example of existence is given: God's existence is pre-eternal and contingent existence is temporal, yet they are similar in concept. This shows that similars can differ in attributes. The commentary clarifies that the concession is only dialectical to argue against the Mu'tazila, and that in reality God is incomparable. The second commentary notes that theologians who hold existence to be univocal consider existences similar.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
تماثل
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share the same type of relation (e.g., both are knowledge related to objects), then they must be similar in essence.
Correction: The passage argues that sharing in one aspect of relation does not entail similarity in essence; even opposites share necessary concomitants.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the inference from shared relation to similarity?
A) Because relations are always unique to each being.
B) Because even opposites can share a necessary concomitant.
C) Because human knowledge is not really related to objects.
D) Because the objection is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
Similarity in one aspect, such as relation, does not prove overall similarity; the inference is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself assuming that two people or situations are the same because they share one similarity, what does this passage teach you about the danger of such hasty generalizations? How will you guard against this in your own judgments?
?
This passage shows that even opposites can share a common necessary concomitant. Name one area in your life where you have judged something as 'similar to sin' or 'like a good deed' based on a superficial commonality. Repent from that assumption and commit to a deeper analysis according to revelation.
?
The response ends with tawaqquf—suspending judgment when no method is available. Is there a theological question you have been struggling with that you are now called to simply stop trying to resolve and entrust to Allah? Write it down and commit to leaving it suspended.

Dialectical Concession and the Example of Existence
Dialectical concession with example of existence and clarifying commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dialectical concession of similarity is used to refute the Mu'tazila and why similarity does not require sharing in temporal attributes, illustrated by the example of existence.

Core Idea
Similar things can differ in pre-eternity and temporality.

Having conceded similarity for the sake of argument, the author now argues that even if divine and human knowledge were similar, they need not share in being pre-eternal or temporal. The example of existence shows that similars can differ in these attributes. The commentary clarifies that this concession is dialectical, not literal.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical concession
Read from 'We concede similarity' to the end. Note that 'we concede' is a dialectical step, as explained in the commentary.
The concession is for refutation, not acceptance.
2
Understand the example of existence
Examine how God's existence (pre-eternal) and contingent existence (temporal) are similar in concept but differ in temporality. This shows similars can differ in attributes.
3
Note the secret (sirr)
The passage explains that two similars must be distinguished by something, and that something can be the source of a specific ruling (like pre-eternity vs. temporality).
This explains how similars can differ without contradiction.
4
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary emphasizes that the concession is only dialectical (tanazzulī) and that in reality God is incomparable. Also note the gloss on univocal existence.
The gloss from al-Tajrid mentions that theologians who hold existence to be univocal (mushtarak mutawāṭiʼ) consider existences similar.

Source Text

The Second Objection: Does God's Knowledge of the Infinite Require Infinite Sciences?
Objection and answer regarding God's knowledge of the infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God's knowledge of infinite objects would require infinite distinct sciences is answered by distinguishing between the unity of knowledge's essence and the multiplicity of its relations.

Core Idea
Knowledge is one in essence; relations can be infinite.

A common objection against the attribute of knowledge is that if God knows infinite objects, and knowledge is additional to His essence, then He must have infinite distinct sciences. This would imply a problematic multiplicity. In this passage, the author presents this objection and gives a crisp answer: the multiplicity is only in the relations, not in the essence of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: if God's knowledge is additional and He knows infinite things, then there must be infinite distinct sciences because knowledge of one thing is not knowledge of another.
Notice that the objection assumes that each known object requires a separate act of knowledge.
2
Identify the answer
The answer states that multiplicity is in the cognitive relations (iḍāfāt), which are relational and can be infinite. The essence of knowledge remains one.
This distinction is the key to resolving the objection.
3
Connect to earlier discussion
The passage references earlier discussion that a single knowledge can relate to multiple known things because knowledge is a real attribute having relation (iḍāfa). Reflect on how that supports the answer.
The earlier discussion is mentioned in the commentary.
4
Test your understanding
Explain in your own words why the objection fails. Then answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp.
Use the key terms: essence of knowledge (ذات العلم) and relations (تعلقات علمية).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والجواب أن التعدد في التعلقات العلمية قد سبق البحث في جواز تعلق علم واحد بالمعلومات المتعددة وأنه مبني على أن العلم صفة حقيقة ذات إضافة وأن قيام علم مقام علوم مختلفة لا يستلزم جواز قيام صفة واحدة مقام صفات مختلفة الأجناس حتى يلزم الاستغناء عن تعدد الصفات فمن أراد التفصيل فلينظر فيما سبق. قوله وهي إضافية فيجوز لا تناهيها صريح فيما مر منا مراراً من أن التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية وإن لم تكن اعتبارية محضة ليس بمستحيل إذ لا يجري فيه برهان التطبيق باتفاق الفريقين اللهم إلا في بعض المواد لا لبرهان التطبيق بل لدليل آخر. الثاني من الوجوه إنه تعالى عالم بما لا نهاية له فإذا فرض أن علمه زائد على ذاته يلزم أن يكون له علوم موجودة غير متناهية ضرورة أن العلم بشيء غير العلم بشيء آخر. والجواب أن التعدد في التعلقات العلمية وهي إضافية فيجوز لا تناهيها وأما ذات العلم فواحدة.
His saying: 'And the answer is that multiplicity is in the cognitive relations' — the inquiry has preceded on the permissibility of a single knowledge being related to multiple known things, and that it is based on knowledge being a real attribute having relation (iḍāfa), and that a single knowledge taking the place of different knowledges does not entail the permissibility of a single attribute taking the place of attributes of different kinds, such that independence from the multiplicity of attributes would be necessary. Whoever wants detail should look at what preceded. His saying: 'And they are relational (iḍāfiyya), so their infinitude is permissible' — this is explicit in what we have repeatedly stated: that infinite regress (tasalsul) in matters of consideration (umūr iʻtibāriyya), even if not purely conventional, is not impossible, because the proof of correspondence (burhān al-taṭbīq) does not apply to it by agreement of both parties, except in some materials not due to the proof of correspondence but due to another proof. The second of the aspects: He, exalted, knows what is infinite. If it is assumed that His knowledge is additional to His essence, then it follows that He has infinite existent sciences, necessarily because the knowledge of one thing is not the knowledge of another. And the answer: multiplicity is in the cognitive relations, which are relational (iḍāfiyya), so their infinitude is permissible; as for the essence of knowledge, it is one.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins with two commentaries that explain the answer to the second objection. The first commentary recalls that earlier discussion established that a single knowledge can relate to multiple known things because knowledge is a real attribute with a relation (iḍāfa). The second commentary asserts that infinite regress in relational or conventional matters is not impossible, as the proof of correspondence does not apply. Then the main objection is presented: God knows infinite objects; if His knowledge is additional, it would require infinite sciences, since each known thing requires a distinct knowledge. The answer is that multiplicity is only in the relations (iḍāfāt), not in the essence of knowledge. Since relations can be infinite, a single knowledge essence can have infinite relational aspects without multiplying the substance of knowledge. Thus the objection fails.

Misconception: Thinking that infinite known objects necessarily require infinite distinct sciences in the essence of knowledge.
Correction: The multiplicity is only in the relations; the essence of divine knowledge remains one and simple.

Key Takeaway
The essence of divine knowledge is one; multiplicity lies only in its relations, which can be infinite without problem.

The Third Objection: Infinite Regress of Knowledge
The third objection: infinite regress of knowledges and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection of infinite regress against additional divine knowledge fails, and how the answer distinguishes between essential knowledge and relational aspects.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is avoided because the multiplicity is in relations, not in the essence of knowledge.

This passage tackles a powerful objection: if God knows through an additional knowledge, wouldn't knowing that knowledge require yet another knowledge, leading to an endless chain? The answer offers a subtle distinction between knowledge in its essence and its relations to objects. Let's read how the author resolves this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is formulated: it assumes a separate knowledge attribute and deduces an infinite regress.
2
Understand the force of the infinite regress argument
The objection argues that if God's knowledge of an object requires a distinct additional knowledge, then His knowledge of that knowledge would itself be an additional knowledge, leading to an endless chain.
This would be a problem because infinite regress of distinct realities is deemed impossible in classical theology.
3
See how the answer uses the concept of relations
The answer states that the regress occurs only in the relations (iḍāfāt), not in the essence of knowledge. God's knowledge is one, and it relates to infinite objects, including itself, through conceptual distinctions.
This distinction resolves the regress without needing multiple distinct knowledges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث منها يلزم على تقدير كونه عالماً بعلم أن يكون علمه بعلمه أيضاً زائداً على علمه وتتسلسل العلوم الموجودة إلى مالا نهاية له. والجواب إنه في الإضافات لأن علمه واحد وله تعلقات بمعلومات لا تتناهى من جملتها علمه الذي يخالفه بالاعتبار دون الذات.
The third of them: It follows, on the assumption that He is knowing by a knowledge, that His knowledge of His knowledge also is additional to His knowledge, and the existent sciences would regress infinitely. And the answer: It is in the relations (iḍāfāt), because His knowledge is one, and it has relations (taʻalluqāt) to infinite known things, among which is His knowledge that differs from it by consideration, not by essence.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against divine knowledge being additional. The objection argues that if God knows through an additional knowledge, then His knowledge of that knowledge would also require another additional knowledge, leading to an infinite regress of existent sciences. The answer is that the regress is only in the relations (iḍāfāt), not in the substance of knowledge. God's knowledge is one in essence, and it has multiple relations to infinite known objects. Among these objects is His own knowledge, but that relation is a conceptual distinction (bi-l-iʻtibār), not a real distinction in essence. Therefore, no infinite regress of essential knowledges occurs; rather, it is a regress of relational aspects that is permissible.

Misconception: The infinite regress applies to the essence of knowledge.
Correction: The regress only applies to relations, not to the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint

A) In the essence of knowledge.
B) In the relations (iḍāfāt).
C) In the known objects.
D) In the attribute of life.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress objection is resolved by distinguishing between the one essence of divine knowledge and its multiple relational aspects.
