The Fourth Objection and Its Answer
Refutes the objection and presents two-part answer with counterargument and specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to divine knowledge from Quran 12:76 and the twofold answer: counterarguments from other verses and specification of the verse to exclude God.

Core Idea
The Quranic proof for a superior knower is a general statement that must be specified.

The fourth objection argues that if God had knowledge, there would be a knower above Him, citing Quran 12:76. The response involves two strategies: citing other verses that show God's all-encompassing knowledge, and arguing that the verse is a general verbal proof that can be specified to exclude the Creator. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection, noting how Quran 12:76 is used to imply a superior knower.
The implication is that God cannot have knowledge because it would entail a knower above Him.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The answer begins with counterarguments: other verses affirm God's knowledge, such as Quran 35:11 and 2:255.
These verses show knowledge is attributed to God, contradicting the objection's implication.
3
Grasp the second part: specification
The objection's verse is a general verbal proof (دليل لفظي عام) that admits specification (تخصيص). It must be specified to exclude God to align with decisive proofs of His knowledge.
Specification is a standard method in Islamic legal theory to reconcile general texts with certainties.
4
Reflect on the debate about one verse vs many
The passage notes disagreement: some say one general verse contradicts many others, while others say no, because the general can be specified.
This shows the nuance in handling scriptural evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع لو كان تعالى ذا علم لكان فوقه عليم واللازم باطل اتفاقاً بيان الملازمة قوله تعالى وفوق كل ذي علم عليم الله [يوسف: ٧٦]، والجواب المعارضة بقوله وما تحمل من أنثى ولا تضع إلا بعلمه الله [فاطر: ١١]، ولا يحيطون بشيء من علمه [البقرة: ٢٥٥] وتأويله بالمعلوم خلاف الظاهر إن الله عنده علم الساعة كيف وأنه أي قوله وفوق كل ذي علم عليم دليل لفظي عام يقبل التخصيص فيجب تخصيصه بما عدا الباري سبحانه وتعالى ليوافق ما ذكرناه من الدليل القطعي على ثبوت علمه تعالى. قوله والجواب المعارضة قيل عليه التمسك بالدليل السمعي على العلم يستلزم الدور لأن التصديق بإرسال الرسل يتوقف على التصديق بعلم المرسل وأجيب بأن التمسك به على زيادة العلم لا على نفسه. واعلم أن الآية الواحدة هل تعارض الآيات التي أوردها أم لا اختلفت الأئمة فيه فقال بعضهم تعارض إذ لا فرق بين القلة والكثرة وقال بعضهم لا تعارض لأن تأويل المتعددة أصعب من تأويل الواحدة خصوصاً أن هذه الواحدة عامة وما من عام إلا وقد خص منه البعض إلا نادراً. قوله وتأويله بالمعلوم خلاف الظاهر هذا ليس كما ينبغي لأن أئمة التفسير من المعتزلة وأهل السنة مجتمعون على أن المراد من العلم المعلوم. وقد استدل عليه الإمام في التفسير الكبير بوجوه، وكيف يكون ذلك التأويل خلاف الظاهر وإبقاء العلم على ظاهره بحذف المضاف أي لا يحيطون بشيء من متعلق علمه ليس بأرجح من تأويل العلم بالمعلوم. فكلامه هاهنا مما لا ينبغي أن يلتفت إليه.
The fourth: If the Exalted possessed knowledge, there would be above Him a knower, and the consequent is false by consensus. The explanation of the implication is His saying: 'And above every knower is the All-Knowing' (Quran 12:76). The answer is counterargument by His saying: 'And no female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' (Quran 35:11), and 'They encompass nothing of His knowledge' (Quran 2:255). And its interpretation as 'the known' is contrary to apparent meaning. Notwithstanding that God has knowledge of the Hour. And how is it [significant]? Also, His saying 'above every knower is the All-Knowing' is a verbal universal proof that admits specification. So it must be specified to exclude the Creator, to agree with the decisive proof we mentioned of His knowledge, exalted. His saying: 'And the answer is counterargument' — it was said: holding to the scriptural proof for knowledge entails circularity because belief in the sending of messengers depends on belief in the knowledge of the Sender. And it was answered: holding to it is for the addition of knowledge, not for knowledge itself. Know that whether a single verse contradicts the verses he cited or not, the imams disagreed. Some said they contradict, as there is no difference between few and many; some said no contradiction, because the interpretation of many is harder than the interpretation of one, especially since this one is general, and every general has been particularized except rarely. His saying: 'And its interpretation as the known is contrary to apparent meaning' — this is not as it should be, because the imams of tafsir from the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah are unanimous that what is meant by 'knowledge' is 'the known'. And the Imam [al-Razi] argued for it in the Great Tafsir with proofs. How can that interpretation be contrary to apparent meaning, while keeping knowledge on its apparent meaning by deleting the genitive — i.e., 'they encompass nothing of that which pertains to His knowledge' — is not more preponderant than interpreting 'knowledge' as 'the known'. So his statement here is not to be paid attention to.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: if God had knowledge, there would be a knower above Him, based on Quran 12:76. The answer is twofold. First, counterargument by citing other verses that show God's knowledge is all-encompassing. Second, the verse is a general verbal proof that can be specified to exclude God. The commentary then discusses the possibility of circularity: using scriptural proofs for knowledge would be circular because belief in the messengers depends on belief in God's knowledge; but the response is that the proof is for the addition of knowledge, not for knowledge itself. There is also a debate on whether one verse can contradict many verses; some say yes, some say no. Finally, a commentator criticizes the claim that interpreting 'knowledge' as 'the known' is contrary to the apparent meaning, pointing out that authoritative interpreters unanimously support that interpretation.

Key Terms
فوق كل ذي علم عليم
دليل لفظي
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Quran 12:76 proves God is not all-knowing because there is a superior knower.
Correction: The verse is a general statement that does not apply to God; it refers to created knowers.

Checkpoint
What is the main response to the fourth objection based on Quran 12:76?
A) The verse is metaphorical.
B) The verse is a general proof that must be specified to exclude God.
C) The verse is abrogated.
D) The verse applies only to angels.

Key Takeaway
A general Quranic verse can be specified to exclude God when confronted with decisive proofs, preserving divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to challenge what you believe about Allah's attributes, do you trust the scholars who have resolved such apparent conflicts, or do you let doubt fester? This lesson shows that the Quran itself provides the tools to answer objections. Will you commit to seeking knowledge before letting your heart waver?
?

?

Circularity and Interpretation in the Quranic Proof
Addresses circularity objection and discusses interpretation of 'knowledge' as 'the known'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of circularity when using scriptural proofs for God's knowledge and the debate over interpreting 'knowledge' as 'the known'.

Core Idea
Using scripture to prove God's knowledge is not circular when applied to additional attributes.

This lesson tackles two deeper issues arising from the previous refutation. First, the charge that using a scriptural proof for God's knowledge is circular, since belief in the scripture itself depends on God's knowledge. Second, the interpretation of the word 'knowledge' in certain verses—whether it means 'the known'—and why this interpretation is not forced. Both issues sharpen our understanding of how rational and scriptural proofs interact.

Guided Path
1
Examine the circularity objection
Read the part where an opponent argues that using the Quran to prove God's knowledge is circular because belief in the Quran requires belief in God's knowledge.
This is a standard objection in kalam against scriptural arguments for divine attributes.
2
Understand the answer to circularity
The response is that the proof is not for knowledge itself but for the addition of knowledge—that God's knowledge is all-encompassing.
This distinguishes between proving the existence of knowledge and proving its extent.
3
Analyze the interpretation debate
The passage criticizes the claim that interpreting 'knowledge' as 'the known' is contrary to the apparent meaning, citing consensus of tafsir scholars.
The author argues that the alternative interpretation (deleting the genitive) is not stronger.
4
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how these debates illustrate the careful methodology of kalam in handling scriptural proofs.
The goal is to preserve divine attributes while respecting the Quranic text.

The Fourth Purpose: God is Alive
Affirms God is alive via consensus and rational proof, then discusses meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attribute of life is established through consensus and rational necessity, and why it differs from human life.

Core Idea
God is alive by necessity, but His life is not like ours.

This lesson introduces the fourth purpose of the author: affirming that God is alive (hayy). The passage begins by noting universal agreement on this attribute, then argues that because God is knowing and powerful, He must be alive. However, the meaning of life for God is not the same as for humans, which involves physical temperament or sensation. The author highlights the disagreement on this point to prepare for a deeper understanding of divine life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author starts with consensus, then gives the rational argument, and finally discusses the meaning of life.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp each part.
2
Identify the two key arguments
First, note the consensus (ijmā‘) among all religious groups. Second, the rational argument: every knowing and powerful being is necessarily alive. Understand the logical connection.
3
Understand the difference in meaning
The passage explains that for humans, life is either temperamental balance or the power of sensation and movement. For God, these are not applicable. Reflect on why this distinction matters for theology.
This is a common point of confusion.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the MCQ. This will help solidify the main point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع في أنه تعالى حي. في أنه تعالى حي هذا مما اتفق عليه الكل من أهل الملل وغيرهم لأنه عالم قادر لما مر من الدلائل وقد أطبقوا أيضاً عليه أي على أنه عالم إلا شرذمة لا يعبأ بهم كما عرفته وكل عالم قادر فهو حي بالضرورة لكن اختلفوا في معنى حياته لأنها في حقنا إما اعتدال المزاج النوعي كما يشعر به كلام المحصل حيث قال المراد من الحياة إن كان اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة فهو معقول وإن كان أمراً ثالثاً. قوله لأنها في حقنا إما اعتدال المزاج النوعي لفظه إما من عبارة الشارح أوردها لينضم إليها قوله وإما قوة تتبع ذلك الاعتدال وإنما لم يذكر المصنف هذا المعنى الأخير لأنه أورده وحده في أول بحث الكيفيات النفسانية فأورد هاهنا معنى آخر تنبيهاً على الخلاف أو على تحقيق الحق كما أشرنا إليه هناك.
The fourth purpose: On that He, exalted, is alive. That He, exalted, is alive —this is agreed upon by all, from people of religions and others, because He is knowing, willing, as the proofs have preceded. And they also agreed upon it, i.e., upon that He is knowing, except a handful who are not regarded, as you know. And every knowing, willing one is necessarily alive. But they differed on the meaning of His life, because it in our case is either the specific temperamental balance (iʻtidāl al-mizāj al-nawʻī), as suggested by the speech of al-Muḥaṣṣal where he said: 'If the intended meaning of life is temperamental balance or the power of sensation and movement, then it is intelligible; but if it is a third thing, then...' His saying: 'because it in our case is either the specific temperamental balance' — its wording is from the expression of the commentator, he brought it to attach to his saying 'and either power consequent upon that balance'. And the author only did not mention this latter meaning because he mentioned it alone at the beginning of the discussion on psychical qualities, so he brought here another meaning to call attention to the disagreement or to establish the truth, as we indicated there.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage establishes that God is alive (hayy). It begins by noting the consensus (ijmāʻ) among all religious people and others that God is alive, based on the fact that He is knowing (ʿālim) and powerful (qādir), as previously proven. Since every knowing and powerful being is alive by necessity, the attribute of life is derived. However, there is disagreement on the meaning of life when attributed to God. For humans, life is defined as either a specific temperamental balance (iʻtidāl al-mizāj al-nawʻī) or the power of sensation and movement. But these are corporeal definitions not applicable to God. The commentator explains that the author (al-Ījī) deliberately only mentioned the first meaning (temperamental balance) to highlight the disagreement and the need for a different understanding of divine life, noting that the second meaning was already covered earlier. Thus, divine life is affirmed as a real attribute beyond physical definitions.

Key Terms
حي
عالم
قادر
اعتدال المزاج
قوة الحس والحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that God's life is similar to human life, involving a physical temperament or bodily sensations.
Correction: God's life is not like human life; it is a perfect, non-corporeal attribute that is necessarily implied by His knowledge and power, and it does not involve any physical balance or sensation.

Checkpoint
Why is God necessarily alive according to the passage?
A) Because He is powerful.
B) Because He is knowing and powerful.
C) Because He created life.
D) Because consensus says so.

Key Takeaway
God is alive by necessity as a consequence of His knowledge and power, but His life is not defined by physical properties like balance or sensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you unconsciously imagine God's life as similar to your own, with limitations and physicality? What would it mean to truly internalize that His life is beyond any physical description?
?
If God's life is not defined by sensation or movement, then He is not affected by our actions in the way humans are. How does this change the way you approach worship — as a purely obedient act rather than trying to 'move' God emotionally?
?
The passage argues that every knowing and powerful being is necessarily alive. Do you live as if God is alive and aware of your every thought? What would you change about your internal life if you truly believed that?

The Circularity Objection and the Principle of Specification
Objection and answer on circularity and specification of a general verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity against using scriptural proof for God's knowledge is answered, and why a general verse must be specified to agree with definitive rational proof.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs for divine attributes must be harmonized with definitive rational proofs through specification.

The commentator anticipates an objection: using a verse to prove God's knowledge seems circular because belief in the messenger requires believing God knows him. He provides a nuanced answer and also addresses how to handle a general verse that appears to contradict rational arguments. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and counterargument
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the commentator first presents a counterargument using other verses, then answers the circularity objection.
The counterargument uses two verses: one about female conception, another about encompassing His knowledge.
2
Understand the circularity issue and its resolution
The objection states that using a scriptural proof for God's knowledge leads to circularity because belief in messengers depends on God's knowledge. The answer: the proof is used for increase of knowledge, not for God's essence.
3
Grasp the principle of specification
The commentator asserts that the verse 'above every possessor of knowledge' is a general verbal proof that admits specification. It must be specified to exclude the Creator to align with the definitive rational proof.
This shows how scriptural and rational proofs are harmonized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب المعارضة بقوله : وما تحمل من أنثى ولا تضع إلا بعلمه الله [فاطر : ١١]، ولا يحيطون بشيء من علمه ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٥٥] وتأويله بالمعلوم خلاف الظاهر (إن الله عنده علم الساعة كيف وأنه ( أي قوله : وفوق كل ذي علم عليم [ يوسف : ٧٦]، دليل ( لفظي ( عام ) يقبل التخصيص) فيجب تخصيصه بما عدا الباري سبحانه وتعالى ليوافق ما ذكرناه من الدليل القطعي على ثبوت علمه تعالى. قوله : ( والجواب المعارضة) قيل : عليه التمسك بالدليل السمعي على العلم يستلزم الدور لأن التصديق بإرسال الرسل يتوقف على التصديق بعلم المرسل وأجيب : بأن التمسك به على زيادة العلم لا على نفسه. واعلم أن الآية الواحدة هل تعارض الآيات التي أوردها أم لا اختلفت الأئمة فيه فقال بعضهم : تعارض إذ لا فرق بين القلة والكثرة وقال بعضهم : لا تعارض لأن تأويل المتعددة أصعب من تأويل الواحدة خصوصاً أن هذه الواحدة عامة وما من عام إلا وقد خص منه البعض إلا نادراً . قوله : ( وتأويله بالمعلوم خلاف الظاهر هذا ليس كما ينبغي لأن أئمة التفسير من المعتزلة وأهل السنة مجتمعون على أن المراد من العلم المعلوم. وقد استدل عليه الإمام في التفسير الكبير بوجوه، وكيف يكون ذلك التأويل خلاف الظاهر وإبقاء العلم على ظاهره بحذف المضاف أي لا يحيطون بشيء من متعلق علمه ليس بأرجح من تأويل العلم بالمعلوم. فكلامه هاهنا مما لا ينبغي أن يلتفت إليه
And the answer is the counterargument by His saying: 'And no female conceives nor does she give birth except with His knowledge' [Fatir 35:11], and 'They encompass not anything of His knowledge' [Al-Baqarah 2:255], and its interpretation as known (al-ma'lum) is contrary to the apparent. 'Indeed, Allah [alone] has knowledge of the Hour' ... and how is it that His saying: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' [Yusuf 12:76] is a verbal, general proof that admits specification? So it must be specified for other than the Creator, exalted, to agree with what we have mentioned of the definitive proof for the affirmation of His knowledge, exalted. His saying: 'And the answer is the counterargument' — it was said: adherence to the scriptural proof for knowledge entails circularity because belief in the sending of messengers depends on belief in the knowledge of the sender. And it was answered: adherence to it is for the increase of knowledge, not for [its] essence. And know that whether one verse can contradict the verses that were mentioned or not, the imams differed. Some said: it does contradict, for there is no difference between small and large. Some said: it does not contradict because interpretation of multiple verses is more difficult than interpretation of a single one, especially since this single verse is general, and no general is specified except rarely. His saying: 'and its interpretation as known is contrary to the apparent' — this is not appropriate, because the imams of interpretation from the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna agree that by 'knowledge' what is meant is 'the known'. The Imam [al-Razi] argued for it in the Great Tafsir with proofs. And how can that interpretation be contrary to the apparent while keeping 'knowledge' in its apparent sense by deleting the genitive, i.e., 'they encompass not anything of what pertains to His knowledge', is not more preponderant than interpreting 'knowledge' as 'the known'. So his speech here is not to be paid attention to.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding a scriptural proof for God's knowledge. The commentator discusses an objection: someone might argue that using a verse like 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' to prove God's knowledge leads to circularity because believing in the messenger's knowledge depends on knowing that God knows the messenger. The answer: this verse is used to prove the increase of God's knowledge, not its essence (i.e., the fact that God knows). Additionally, the commentator notes that some imams argue that a single verse cannot contradict multiple verses, while others say it can. The discussion then moves to the interpretation of 'science' (ilm) in other verses as 'the known' (ma'lum). The commentator criticizes the statement that this interpretation is contrary to the apparent, noting that major exegetes from both Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna accept this interpretation. He argues that leaving the word 'science' in its apparent sense by implying an omitted genitive (i.e., 'they encompass not anything of that which pertains to His knowledge') is not more preponderant than interpreting 'science' as 'the known'.

Key Terms
دليل سمعي (scriptural proof)
تخصيص (specification)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think scriptural proofs for divine attributes are irrelevant or always override rational proofs.
Correction: The passage shows that scriptural proofs must be specified and harmonized with definitive rational proofs; they are not overridden but interpreted.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the verse 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' must be specified?
A) Because it is a metaphorical verse
B) Because it is a general verbal proof that admits specification to agree with rational proof
C) Because it is abrogated
D) Because it is not authentic

Key Takeaway
A general scriptural verse must be specified to align with definitive rational proofs, and the circularity objection is resolved by noting that the proof is for increase of knowledge, not essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran about Allah's knowledge, do you ever stop to question whether you already know that truth by reason, or do you rely solely on the verse? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundation of your belief in Allah's attributes?
?
The circularity objection forces you to realize that proving Allah's knowledge from scripture assumes you already know that Allah knows. Is your faith built on such an assumption? What would you say if someone asked you why you trust the Quran when it speaks about Allah? Write a personal response.
?
The passage distinguishes between proving the essence of knowledge and its increase. In your own life, do you seek only to confirm what you already know about Allah, or are you actively seeking to deepen and expand that knowledge? What is one step you can take today to increase your knowledge of Allah's attributes?

Contradiction of a Single Verse and the Interpretation of 'Ilm as Ma'lum
Disagreement on contradiction and defense of interpreting 'ilm as ma'lum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether a single general verse can contradict multiple verses, and you will grasp the interpretation of 'ilm (knowledge) as 'the known' (ma'lum) in certain contexts.

Core Idea
Interpreting a single verse is easier than interpreting many; thus a single general verse does not necessarily contradict multiple verses.

The commentator moves to a new issue: can one verse override many? He presents two positions. Then he addresses a claim that interpreting ‘ilm as ma‘lum is contrary to the apparent, showing that major exegetes accept it. This lesson explores both debates.

Guided Path
1
Learn the two positions on contradiction
Read the passage where the commentator mentions that imams differed: some said a single verse can contradict multiple verses, others said it cannot because interpretation of multiple verses is harder.
The second position uses the principle that no general is left unspecific except rarely.
2
Analyze the argument about difficulty of interpretation
Understand the reasoning: a single general verse is easier to interpret than multiple verses. Thus, if multiple verses suggest a certain meaning, a single apparently contrary verse is not strong enough to contradict them.
3
Examine the interpretation of 'ilm as ma'lum
The commentator criticizes the claim that interpreting 'ilm as 'the known' is contrary to the apparent. He notes that major exegetes from both Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna agree on this interpretation, and gives an argument from al-Razi.
He argues that keeping 'ilm in its apparent sense by deleting the implied genitive is not more preponderant than this interpretation.

The Meaning of Life: Human Definitions and the Philosophical View
Al-Iji enumerates human definitions of life and presents the philosophical view for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers define God's life simply as His capacity to know and will, rejecting anthropomorphic definitions.

Core Idea
For God, 'life' means nothing more than being able to know and will.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the fundamental disagreement over the meaning of divine life. He first explains how life is defined for created beings—either as a temperamental balance or as a power that follows it—and then shows that none of these apply to God. This leads to the philosophers' minimalist definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on human definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the definitions of life for humans: temperamental balance or a power following it.
Note that these definitions are based on physical properties.
2
See why they don't apply to God
Notice the statement 'life is not conceivable by any of these meanings in His right' – this is the key move.
God is not physical, so these definitions are irrelevant.
3
Understand the philosophers' alternative
The philosophers conclude that God's life is simply that He can know and will. Identify that as the end of the passage.
This is a negative or minimal definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن اختلفوا في معنى حياته لأنها في حقنا إما اعتدال المزاج النوعي ( كما يشعر به كلام المحصل حيث قال : المراد من الحياة إن كان اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة فهو معقول . وإن كان أمراً ثالثاً فلا بد من تصويره وإقامة الدليل عليه ) وإما قوة تتبع ذلك الاعتدال) سواء كانت نفس قوة الحس والحركة أو مغايرة لها على ما اختاره ابن سينا كما مر . ( ولا تتصور ) الحياة بشيء من هذه المعاني ( في حقه تعالى فقالوا : إنما كونه يصح أن يعلم ويقدر وهو مذهب الحكماء وأبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة. وقال الجمهور : ) من أصحابنا ومن المعتزلة ( إنها صفة توجب صحة العلم ) والقدرة ( إذ لولا اختصاصه بصفة توجب صحة العلم الكامل والقدرة الشاملة ( لكان) (اختصاصه بصحة العلم والقدرة المذكورين ( ترجيحاً بلا مرجح وأجابوا عنه : بأنه منقوض باختصاصه بتلك الصفة ) الموجبة للصحة ) فإنه لو كان بصفة أخرى لزم التسلسل ) في الصفات الوجودية هذا خلف ( فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى ما يكون ( اختصاصه به ( بصفة أخرى ) فيكون ترجيحاً بلا مرجح ولما كان استدلالهم هذا مبنياً على تماثل الذوات أشار إلى بطلانه بقوله : ( والحق أنه ذاته تعالى مخالفة بالحقيقة لسائر الذوات فقد يقتضي ) لذاته ( الاختصاص بآخر فلا يلزم ترجيح من غير مرجح (و) من المعلوم أن ( ليس جعل ذلك ) الأمر الذي يقتضيه ذاته لذاته ( علة صحة العلم أولى من جعلها ) أي جعل ذلك الأمر أشبه نظر إلى قوله : ( نفس صحة العلم فمن أراد إثبات زيادة ) على نفس الصحة فعليه بالدليل. قوله : ( ومن المعتزلة أنها صفة القائلون به قدماء المعتزلة المثبتون للأحوال لا متأخروهم النافون لها القائلون بأن صفاته تعالى عين ذاته وتعليلهم صحة العالمية والقادرية بها لا ينافي نفس العالمية والقادرية بالألوهية كما سبق . قوله: (فعليه بالدليل أي فليتمسك بالدليل أو فالدليل واجب عليه على أن الباء زائدة وعليه خبر مقدم على المبتدأ إفادة للحصر.
But they differed regarding the meaning of His life. Because in our case [life] is either a specific temperamental balance — as indicated by the speech of al-Muhassal where he said: 'If by life is meant temperamental balance or the power of sense and movement, it is intelligible. If it is a third thing, then it must be conceptualized and a proof for it established.' — or it is a power that follows that balance, whether it is the same as the power of sense and movement or different from it according to Ibn Sina's choice as has passed. And life is not conceivable by any of these meanings in His right, exalted. So they said: rather, His being such that it is correct for Him to know and will — that is the [meaning of life]. That is the doctrine of the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila. And the majority—from our companions and from the Mu'tazila—said: It is an attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and power. For if He were not distinguished by an attribute that necessitates the soundness of perfect knowledge and comprehensive power, then His being distinguished by the soundness of the aforementioned knowledge and power would be a preponderance without a preponderator. And they answered it: that it is refuted by His being distinguished by that attribute (which necessitates soundness), because if it were by another attribute, it would lead to regress in existential attributes — this is a contradiction. So it must end with that which is distinguished by it being by another attribute, thus it would be a preponderance without a preponderator. And since their argument is based on the similarity of essences, he indicated its invalidity by saying: 'And the truth is that His essence, exalted, is different in reality from other essences. So it may necessitate by itself distinction by another, so no preponderance without a preponderator is required.' And it is known that making that thing which His essence necessitates for itself a cause for the soundness of knowledge is not more preponderant than making it (i.e., making that thing) — resembling consideration to his saying — 'the soundness of knowledge itself.' So whoever wants to prove an increase over the soundness itself, let him provide the proof. His saying: 'and from the Mu'tazila it is an attribute' — those who say it are the early Mu'tazila who affirm states (ahwal), not the later ones who deny them and say that His attributes are identical to His essence. Their explanation of the soundness of knowledge and power by it does not contradict the soundness of knowledge and power by divinity as has preceded. His saying: 'so let him provide the proof' means let him adhere to proof, or proof is obligatory upon him, with 'al-ba' being extra and 'alayhi' predicate preceding subject to indicate restriction.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and dispute over the meaning of God's life. First, the author explains that for humans, life is either a specific temperamental balance or a power that follows that balance, which may be identical to the power of sense and movement or different (as per Ibn Sina). None of these meanings apply to God. So the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri say that life for God is simply that He is capable of knowing and willing (i.e., the ability itself). The majority (Ash'aris and earlier Mu'tazila) say life is a separate attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and power. They argue: without such an attribute, God's distinction in being capable of knowing and power would be arbitrary (preponderance without a preponderator). The response to this argument is that this leads to a regress: if that attribute itself requires another attribute to be distinguished, then it would be an infinite regress. The author (al-Iji) counters that this argument assumes that all essences are similar, but God's essence is unique and may by itself necessitate distinction by another attribute, so no arbitrary preponderance follows. Also, it's not necessary that what the essence necessitates be a cause for soundness; it could be the same as soundness itself. Therefore, the burden of proof is on those who claim an increase over soundness itself. The commentary then clarifies that the early Mu'tazila who affirm states are the ones who say life is an attribute, and that their explanation does not contradict the fact that the soundness of knowledge and power is by divinity. Finally, a grammatical note on the phrase 'alayhi bi-al-dalil' is given.

Key Terms
حياة
اعتدال المزاج
قوة الحس
قوة الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's life is similar to human life, involving a physical balance or power.
Correction: The philosophers argue that none of the human meanings apply to God; His life is simply the ability to know and will.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the meaning of God's life?
A) Temperamental balance
B) Power of sense and movement
C) That He can know and will
D) A separate attribute

Key Takeaway
For God, life is not a physical property but the pure capacity to know and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's life is simply His capacity to know and will everything you do, does that change how you feel about being constantly under His awareness? Will you adjust any habit today knowing that His 'life' means He perfectly sees and responds to your choices?
?
You have likely thought of God's life as similar to your own—a pulse, a breath. Al-Iji strips that away. Are you ready to let go of that comfortable image and worship a God whose life is pure, unmediated knowledge and will? What cost does that demand from your heart?
?
The philosophers defined God's life as 'the fact that He can know and will.' If that is the only meaning, then your life is a gift that mirrors His only in the ability to know and choose. How will you use your knowledge and will today to honor the One whose life is the very source of yours?

The Majority View: Life as a Separate Attribute and al-Iji's Critique
Al-Iji states the majority position, their argument, the regress counter, and his critique based on the uniqueness of God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that life is a separate attribute and how al-Iji critiques their argument.

Core Idea
The majority's argument for a separate life attribute collapses if God's essence is unique.

The majority of theologians held that life is an additional attribute that makes knowledge and power possible. Al-Iji presents their reasoning and then exposes its weakness, showing that it rests on an assumption about the similarity of essences. This lesson walks through that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the majority's claim and argument
Read the passage where the majority says life is an attribute necessitating soundness of knowledge and power, and their argument against arbitrariness.
They argue: without an attribute, God's distinction in being able to know and will would be arbitrary.
2
See the regress objection
Note the response: if the attribute itself needs another attribute to be distinguished, we get an infinite regress.
This is a typical kalam argument against infinite chains.
3
Understand al-Iji's critique
Al-Iji points out that the majority's argument assumes all essences are similar. But God's essence is unique and may necessitate distinction without a separate attribute.
This undermines the preponderance argument.
4
Recognize the burden of proof
Al-Iji concludes that making the essence's property a cause for soundness is no more valid than identifying it with soundness itself. So the one who claims an increase must bring proof.
The burden is on the majority.

But they differed regarding the meaning of His life. Because in our case [life] is either a specific temperamental balance — as indicated by the speech of al-Muhassal where he said: 'If by life is meant temperamental balance or the power of sense and movement, it is intelligible. If it is a third thing, then it must be conceptualized and a proof for it established.' — or it is a power that follows that balance, whether it is the same as the power of sense and movement or different from it according to Ibn Sina's choice as has passed. And life is not conceivable by any of these meanings in His right, exalted. So they said: rather, His being such that it is correct for Him to know and will — that is the [meaning of life]. That is the doctrine of the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila. And the majority—from our companions and from the Mu'tazila—said: It is an attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and power. For if He were not distinguished by an attribute that necessitates the soundness of perfect knowledge and comprehensive power, then His being distinguished by the soundness of the aforementioned knowledge and power would be a preponderance without a preponderator. And they answered it: that it is refuted by His being distinguished by that attribute (which necessitates soundness), because if it were by another attribute, it would lead to regress in existential attributes — this is a contradiction. So it must end with that which is distinguished by it being by another attribute, thus it would be a preponderance without a preponderator. And since their argument is based on the similarity of essences, he indicated its invalidity by saying: 'And the truth is that His essence, exalted, is different in reality from other essences. So it may necessitate by itself distinction by another, so no preponderance without a preponderator is required.' And it is known that making that thing which His essence necessitates for itself a cause for the soundness of knowledge is not more preponderant than making it (i.e., making that thing) — resembling consideration to his saying — 'the soundness of knowledge itself.' So whoever wants to prove an increase over the soundness itself, let him provide the proof. His saying: 'and from the Mu'tazila it is an attribute' — those who say it are the early Mu'tazila who affirm states (ahwal), not the later ones who deny them and say that His attributes are identical to His essence. Their explanation of the soundness of knowledge and power by it does not contradict the soundness of knowledge and power by divinity as has preceded. His saying: 'so let him provide the proof' means let him adhere to proof, or proof is obligatory upon him, with 'al-ba' being extra and 'alayhi' predicate preceding subject to indicate restriction.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and dispute over the meaning of God's life. First, the author explains that for humans, life is either a specific temperamental balance or a power that follows that balance, which may be identical to the power of sense and movement or different (as per Ibn Sina). None of these meanings apply to God. So the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri say that life for God is simply that He is capable of knowing and willing (i.e., the ability itself). The majority (Ash'aris and earlier Mu'tazila) say life is a separate attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and power. They argue: without such an attribute, God's distinction in being capable of knowing and power would be arbitrary (preponderance without a preponderator). The response to this argument is that this leads to a regress: if that attribute itself requires another attribute to be distinguished, then it would be an infinite regress. The author (al-Iji) counters that this argument assumes that all essences are similar, but God's essence is unique and may by itself necessitate distinction by another attribute, so no arbitrary preponderance follows. Also, it's not necessary that what the essence necessitates be a cause for soundness; it could be the same as soundness itself. Therefore, the burden of proof is on those who claim an increase over soundness itself. The commentary then clarifies that the early Mu'tazila who affirm states are the ones who say life is an attribute, and that their explanation does not contradict the fact that the soundness of knowledge and power is by divinity. Finally, a grammatical note on the phrase 'alayhi bi-al-dalil' is given.

Divine Will as Care and Its Distinction from Choice
Al-Iji defines divine will as care and distinguishes it from choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical definition of divine will as the essence's regard for the most complete order (inayah) and how it differs from choice.

Core Idea
Divine will is care for the best order, not a choice between alternatives.

This passage introduces the fifth objective of the section: proving that God is Willing. The author first presents the philosophers' conceptualization of divine will as caring regard for the most complete order, then distinguishes this from human choice which involves weighing alternatives. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the necessary nature of God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: it begins with the fifth objective, then lays out two investigations: conceptualization and proof.
The passage is slightly cut off at the end, but the main ideas are clear.
2
Understand the definition of will as 'inayah'
Identify the philosophers' definition: God's will is the essence's regard for the most complete order, called care. Consider Ibn Sina's explanation: care is the encompassment of God's perfect knowledge.
This defines will as a necessary concomitant of knowledge, not arbitrary choice.
3
Distinguish will from choice
The passage explains that choice (ikhtiyar) involves looking at both sides and inclining to one, while will (irada) is directed solely to the side willed. This distinction clarifies that God's will does not involve hesitation or comparison.
This guards against anthropomorphizing God's will as indecision.

The fifth objective: regarding that He, exalted, is Willing. And there are two investigations: the first: in proving will. And here it is necessary to first conceptualize it, then to establish and verify it by proof. So the philosophers said: His will, exalted, is the essence's regard for the most complete order, and they call it care (inayah). Ibn Sina said: Care is the encompassment of the knowledge of the First [i.e., God] — that is, His encompassing knowledge, patterned after their saying: 'Knowledge is the acquisition of the form together with...' intending thereby the acquired form. His saying: 'regarding that He, exalted, is Willing': They said the difference between will and choice is that choice is will with observation of the other side. Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth objective: that God is Willing (Murid). The subject has two investigations: the first is the proof of will. The author states that one must first conceptualize it, then prove it. The philosophers define God's will as the essence's regard for the most complete order (nazm al-akmal), which they call 'inayah' (care). Ibn Sina explains that care is the encompassment of God's knowledge — that is, His knowledge encompasses everything in the best possible way. The commentary then distinguishes between will (irada) and choice (ikhtiyar). Choice is will that takes into account the other side; the chooser looks at both alternatives and inclines to one, whereas the willer simply looks at the side he wills. This distinction is important for understanding that God's will is not a choice between alternatives in the human sense, but rather a necessary emanation from His knowledge of what is best. The passage ends abruptly.

Correction: The passage clarifies that divine will is not a choice between options; it is the necessary consequence of His perfect knowledge of the best order, called care.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty in your life, do you truly believe that God's will for you is part of the most complete order, even when it feels chaotic? Or do you secretly wish God had chosen differently, as if He deliberated like a human?
?
If God's will is pure care without change or deliberation, then every trial you face is exactly what His perfect knowledge knew was best. Can you accept that your current hardship is not a mistake or an afterthought but the very best possible arrangement for you right now? If not, what does that reveal about your trust in His care?
?
Reflect on a recent decision you made where you weighed two options. Now compare that to how God wills: without hesitation, without regret, without looking back. How can you cultivate a heart that submits to His will without questioning His choice as though He were a finite chooser like you?

The Proof That Life Is an Additional Attribute and Refutation of the Infinite Regress Objection
The commentator presents the proof that life is an additional attribute and refutes the objection based on infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument for life as an additional divine attribute and how the objection of infinite regress is refuted by appealing to the uniqueness of God's essence.

Core Idea
Life is a real attribute that makes knowledge and power possible; without it, they'd be arbitrary.

The commentators now turn to the attribute of life (hayat). They need to show that life is not just another name for God's being knowing and powerful, but a distinct attribute that grounds those abilities. The argument involves the principle of preponderance without a preponderator, and an objection that threatens an infinite regress of attributes is answered by the uniqueness of God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Get a sense of the flow of the argument from definition to proof to objection to answer.
Notice the parentheses in Arabic indicate the original matn being quoted by the sharh.
2
Identify the two views on life
The passage mentions two interpretations: the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri who say life is just the capability of knowing and being powerful, and the majority (including Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) who say life is a separate attribute that necessitates knowledge and power. Understand the difference.
3
Understand the proof from preponderance
The majority's proof: if God were not characterized by a distinct attribute that necessitates perfect knowledge and power, then His being qualified with those would be a preponderance without a preponderator (tarjih bi-la murajjih). This is the key argument.
This is a standard kalam principle: any contingent, free choice of a cause must have a specifying cause, otherwise it is arbitrary.
4
See the objection and its answer
An objector argues that the same logic applies to the life attribute itself, leading to an infinite regress of attributes. The answer is that this regress only occurs if essences are similar; but God's essence is unique, so it can by itself necessitate an attribute without further cause. Thus the proof for life as additional stands.
The word 'khulf' in the text likely means 'impossible' (contradiction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا بد من تصويره وإقامة الدليل عليه ) وإما قوة تتبع ذلك الاعتدال) سواء كانت نفس قوة الحس والحركة أو مغايرة لها على ما اختاره ابن سينا كما مر . ( ولا تتصور ) الحياة بشيء من هذه المعاني ( في حقه تعالى فقالوا : إنما كونه يصح أن يعلم ويقدر وهو مذهب الحكماء وأبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة. وقال الجمهور : ) من أصحابنا ومن المعتزلة ( إنها صفة توجب صحة العلم ) والقدرة ( إذ لولا اختصاصه بصفة توجب صحة العلم الكامل والقدرة الشاملة ( لكان) (اختصاصه بصحة العلم والقدرة المذكورين ( ترجيحاً بلا مرجح وأجابوا عنه : بأنه منقوض باختصاصه بتلك الصفة ) الموجبة للصحة ) فإنه لو كان بصفة أخرى لزم التسلسل ) في الصفات الوجودية هذا خلف ( فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى ما يكون ( اختصاصه به ( بصفة أخرى ) فيكون ترجيحاً بلا مرجح ولما كان استدلالهم هذا مبنياً على تماثل الذوات أشار إلى بطلانه بقوله : ( والحق أنه ذاته تعالى مخالفة بالحقيقة لسائر الذوات فقد يقتضي ) لذاته ( الاختصاص بآخر فلا يلزم ترجيح من غير مرجح (و) من المعلوم أن ( ليس جعل ذلك ) الأمر الذي يقتضيه ذاته لذاته ( علة صحة العلم أولى من جعلها ) أي جعل ذلك الأمر أشبه نظر إلى قوله : ( نفس صحة العلم فمن أراد إثبات زيادة ) على نفس الصحة فعليه بالدليل.
It is necessary to conceptualize it and to establish the proof for it. [There is also a power that follows that moderation] whether it is the same as the power of sensation and motion or different from it, according to what Ibn Sina chose, as has passed. And life is not conceived by any of these meanings in His right, Exalted. So they said: Rather, His being such that He can know and be powerful is the doctrine of the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila. And the majority—from our companions and from the Mu'tazila—said: It is an attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and power. For if He were not characterized by an attribute that necessitates the validity of perfect knowledge and comprehensive power, then His characterization by the validity of the mentioned knowledge and power would be a preponderance without a preponderator. And they answered about it: That it is refuted by His characterization by that attribute which necessitates validity, for if it were by another attribute, then there would be an infinite regress in the existential attributes—this is impossible (khulf). So it is necessary to end at what is such that its characterization by another attribute would be a preponderance without a preponderator. And since their inference was based on the similarity of essences, he indicated its invalidity by saying: And the truth is that His essence, Exalted, is essentially different from all other essences, so it may require by itself the characterization with another, thus no preponderance without a preponderator follows. And it is known that making that thing which His essence requires for itself the cause of the validity of knowledge is not more appropriate than making it—that is, making that thing, as it resembles consideration of His saying—the very validity of knowledge. So whoever wants to prove an addition beyond the validity itself, the proof is incumbent upon him.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof for the attribute of life (hayat) and a refutation of an objection. It begins by stating that life is necessary to conceptualize and prove. It discusses the view of Ibn Sina that life is either a power of sensation and motion or something else, but then rejects that life can be understood by any of these meanings in God. The first position presented is that of the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who say that life is simply that God is capable of knowing and being powerful. The majority (including Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) say life is a distinct attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and power. Their proof is: if God were not characterized by such an attribute, then His being qualified with full knowledge and power would be an arbitrary preponderance (tarjih bi-la murajjih). An objector replies that this same argument applies to the life attribute itself, leading to an infinite regress of attributes. The answer is that this regress would only occur if essences were similar; but since God's essence is unique, it can by itself necessitate an attribute without need for a further attribute. Thus the proof for life as additional is valid, and the burden of proof is on anyone who claims an added attribute beyond life itself.

ترجيح بلا مرجح (preponderance without a preponderator)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
تماثل الذوات (similarity of essences)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress objection proves that life cannot be an additional attribute.
Correction: The objection fails because God's essence is unique and can necessitate an attribute without further cause, thus no regress.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Life is an additional attribute because without it, God's knowledge and power would be arbitrary, and the objection of infinite regress is answered by the uniqueness of His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that without an additional attribute of life, God's knowledge and power would be arbitrary. Is there any area of your life where you assume God's attributes are arbitrary rather than rooted in His perfect essence? How does this affect your trust in Him?
?
The passage says God's essence is unique and can by itself necessitate attributes. Do you sometimes treat God as if He were like His creation, subject to the same limitations? How can you cultivate a deeper awe of His uniqueness in your daily worship?
?
The burden of proof is on those who deny the addition. In your own faith, are you content to accept what the majority of scholars have concluded, or do you feel the need to investigate the proofs yourself? What step can you take this week to strengthen your understanding of one of God's attributes?

The Philosophers' Definition of Divine Will as Care (Inaya)
Ibn Sina defines divine will as care (inaya): knowledge of the best order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina defines divine will as God's knowledge of the best order, which flows forth as care (inaya) without additional intent.

Core Idea
Divine will is not a separate act; it is God's knowledge of the best system.

Ibn Sina's concept of divine will differs sharply from the classical Ash'ari understanding. For Ibn Sina, God's will is not a distinct attribute but rather His all-encompassing knowledge of the most perfect order. This passage presents that definition under the term 'care' (al-'inaya). Let's read his words directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the author's own phrasing come through. Then read the translation.
Notice the term 'العناية' (al-inaya) at the start.
2
Identify the key components
The definition has three parts: 1) encompassing knowledge of the First, 2) of what the whole must be for the best order, 3) this knowledge is the source of good flow, without intent. Identify each.
3
Understand the logical chain
See how knowledge leads to best order and then to overflowing good. There is no added volition — that is the crux of the philosophers' position.
This chain is a single causal process, not separable steps.
4
Contrast with Ash'ari view
Compare with the standard Ash'ari definition where will is a distinct attribute separate from knowledge. Notice the key difference: presence or absence of intent (qasd).
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the correct definition from the passage.
The answer is found directly in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا: العناية هي إحاطة علم الأول علمه تعالى بالكل وبما يجب أن يكون عليه الكل حتى يكون على أحسن النظام، فعلم الأول بكيفية الصواب في ترتيب وجود الكل منبع لفضيان الخير في الكل من غير انبعاث قصد وطلب من الأول الحق.
Ibn Sina said: Care (al-'inaya) is the encompassing of the knowledge of the First—His knowledge, Exalted—of the whole and of what the whole must be upon such that it is upon the best order. Thus the First's knowledge of the manner of correctness in the arrangement of the existence of the whole is the source for the overflowing of good in the whole, without the emanation of intent and request from the First, the Truth.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines divine will (irada) as understood by the philosophers (al-hukama'), specifically Ibn Sina. It is a definition of the term 'care' (inaya). The passage states that God's will is His encompassing knowledge of the entire universe and of what the universe must be in order to be in the best possible order. This knowledge itself is the source from which goodness flows into the whole of creation, without any additional act of will or desire on God's part. Thus, for the philosophers, God's will is not a distinct attribute but identical to His knowledge of the optimal system.

Key Terms
العناية (care, inaya)
إحاطة علم الأول (encompassing knowledge of the First)
أحسن النظام (the best order)
فيضان الخير (overflowing of good)
قصد وطلب (intent and request)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny God's will entirely.
Correction: They affirm will but identify it with God's knowledge of the best order; will is not a separate attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's definition in this passage, what is divine care (al-inaya)?
A) God's will as a separate attribute that creates the world.
B) God's knowledge of the best possible order, from which good flows automatically.
C) God's command to angels to arrange the universe.
D) God's love for His creation.

Key Takeaway
For Ibn Sina, divine care is God's knowledge of the best order, from which goodness flows necessarily without any additional act of will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you believe that God's will is a separate act from His knowledge, or is it identical to it? How does this passage challenge your own understanding of God's care for the world?
?
If goodness flows necessarily from God's knowledge without a deliberate choice, does that make you feel less personally valued by God, or does it assure you that everything is already arranged in the best possible way? Be honest with yourself.
?
Ibn Sina says that no 'intent or request' emanates from God in bringing about the best order. How does this affect your reliance on God in your daily struggles? Do you pray expecting a change in God's will, or do you seek to align yourself with what is already best?

The Mu'tazilite Definition of Divine Will as Knowledge of Benefit
Abu al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila define divine will as knowledge of benefit (da'iya).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that God's will is His knowledge of benefit, and the concept of the motivating factor (da'iya).

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, God's will is not a separate attribute but His knowledge of benefit.

The Mu'tazilites held a distinctive view on divine will, arguing that it is not an additional attribute but rather God's knowledge of the benefit in an action. This passage presents the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and other leading Mu'tazilites, explaining how they ground this definition in human psychology while safeguarding God's transcendence. Let's examine the text closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the author's wording settle before analyzing.
2
Identify the definition and the human analogy
The passage defines divine will as knowledge of benefit. It then uses human psychology as an analogy: in humans, opinion or belief that something is beneficial motivates action. Identify how this analogy supports the definition.
Notice that opinion and belief are impossible for God, so His motivating factor is only knowledge.
3
Understand the transmitted nuance
The passage ends with a report from Abu al-Husayn alone that in humans, will is additional to the motivating factor. Reflect on why this nuance matters for the overall position.
This shows that the human case is not identical to the divine case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو الحسين وجماعة من رؤساء المعتزلة كالنظام والجاحظ والعلاف وأبي القاسم البلخي ومحمود الخوارزمي : إرادته تعالى ( هو علمه ينفع في الفعل وذلك كما يجده كل عاقل من نفسه إن ظنه أو اعتقاده ينفع في الفعل) أو علمه به ( يوجب الفعل ويسميه ) أبو الحسين ( بالداعية) ولما استحال الظن والاعتقاد في حقه تعالى انحصر داعيته في العلم بالنفع ونقل عن أبي الحسين وحده أنه قال : الإرادة في الشاهد زائدة على الداعي.
And Abu al-Husayn and a group of the leaders of the Mu'tazila, such as al-Nazzam, al-Jahiz, al-'Allaf, Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi, said: His will, Exalted, is His knowledge of benefit in the action, just as every rational person finds in himself that his opinion or belief benefits in the action, or his knowledge of it necessitates the action. And Abu al-Husayn calls it the motivating factor (al-da'iya). And since opinion and belief are impossible with respect to Him, Exalted, His motivating factor is confined to knowledge of benefit. And it is transmitted from Abu al-Husayn alone that he said: Will in the witnessed (i.e., humans) is additional to the motivating factor.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines divine will (irada) according to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and other leading Mu'tazilites. They say that God's will is His knowledge that an action is beneficial (nafi'), and this knowledge itself motivates the action without any additional volition. In humans, the motivating factor (da'iya) is either an opinion or belief that something is beneficial, and this leads to will. Since opinion and belief are impossible in God, His da'iya is simply knowledge of benefit. The passage also notes a nuance: Abu al-Husayn alone holds that in human beings, will is a distinct thing over and above the da'iya (the motivation).

Key Terms
الإرادة (will)
العلم بالنفع (knowledge of benefit)
الداعية (motivating factor)
الشاهد (the witnessed, i.e. human realm)
الظن والاعتقاد (opinion and belief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites deny that God has any will at all.
Correction: The passage shows they affirm divine will but identify it with knowledge of benefit, not as a separate attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Husayn, what is God's will (irada)?
A) A separate attribute that moves God to act.
B) His knowledge of benefit in an action.
C) His command to angels.
D) His absence of coercion.

Key Takeaway
For Abu al-Husayn, divine will is identical to God's knowledge of the benefit in an action, preserving divine transcendence and simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment in your life when you were absolutely certain that an action would benefit you, yet you did not do it. Why did your will fail to follow your knowledge? How does this human experience differ from God's perfect will as described here? What does that say about your need to align your will with the truth?
?
The Mu'tazilites say God's will is identical to His knowledge of benefit. When you face hardship or unanswered prayers, do you truly believe that God's will for you is still based on a benefit you cannot see? If not, what would it take for you to fully trust that God's knowledge of benefit is always your good? Reflect on a specific difficulty and commit to pray for that trust.
?
In this view, human will can be swayed by opinion or incomplete knowledge. When making decisions, do you often act on mere assumptions (zann) rather than certain knowledge of what is truly beneficial? How can you move toward acting on knowledge that leads to benefit, especially in matters of faith? What step will you take today to replace an opinion with certainty?

Al-Najjar and Al-Ka'bi on Divine Will
Definitions of divine will by al-Najjar and al-Ka'bi with explanation of metaphorical commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the privative and action-based definitions of divine will proposed by al-Najjar and al-Ka'bi.

Core Idea
Divine will is defined either as absence of coercion or as creation/command.

Not all classical theologians define divine will as an eternal attribute. Some, like al-Najjar, see it as a privative concept – the absence of being compelled. Others, like al-Ka'bi, link it strictly to God's actions. This passage presents both views and explains how commands and prohibitions are reinterpreted metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage where al-Najjar and al-Ka'bi define divine will. Notice the contrast: one defines it as a privation, the other as an action.
Al-Najjar's definition is an 'amr 'adami (privative matter).
2
Understand the metaphor of commands
The commentator explains that according to these theologians, commands and prohibitions are metaphorical for a 'scientific requirement' that good flows and evil is repelled.
This shows how they avoid attributing real intent or request to God.
3
Compare with the standard Ash'ari view
Reflect on how these definitions differ from the mainstream view that divine will is an eternal attribute of will. Why might these alternative definitions have been proposed?
This lesson does not include the Ash'ari view, but it helps to recall it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحسين النجار : إنه ) أي كونه مريداً (أمر عدمي وهو عدم كونه مكرهاً ومغلوباً ، ( وقال الكعبي : هي في فعله قوله: (من غير انبعاث قصد وطلب فالأوامر والنواهي الدالة على القصد والطلب تكون عندهم مجازات عن الاقتضاء العلمي لفيضان الخير في الكل والإبعاد عن الشر. قوله : ( وأبي القاسم البلخي هذا مع قوله : فيما سيأتي قال الكعبي إلخ يدل على أن أبا القاسم البلخي غير الكعبي. وقال في بحث القدرة في شرح المقاصد: ومنهم يعني من المخالفين في عموم قدرته تعالى أبو القاسم البلخي المعروف بالكعبي وهذا يدل على عدم تغايرهما لكن ما ذكر هاهنا يوافق ما في أبكار الأفكار حيث قال في بحث الإرادة: ومنهم النظام والبلخي والكعبي ولو ثبت تعدد البلخي والكعبي لتأتى التوفيق بين الكل. واعلم أن نقل الكتاب هاهنا مخالف لما في الأربعين حيث قال فيه : معنى كونه تعالى مريداً في أفعال نفسه عند أبي القاسم البلخي : أنه موجد لها وفي أفعال غيره أنه آمر بها ولما في أبكار الأفكار حيث قال فيه : وأما النظام والكعبي والبلخي فإنهم قالوا إن وصف الله تعالى بالإرادة شرعاً فليس معناه إن أضيف : ذلك إلى أفعاله إلا أنه خالقها وإن أضيف إلى أفعال العباد فالمراد أنه تعالى آمر بها، ولعل هناك نقلاً آخر منهم اختاره المصنف والشارح وإن لم نطلع عليه .
And al-Husayn al-Najjar said: It—i.e., His being willing—is a privative matter, and it is the absence of His being compelled and overcome. And al-Ka'bi said: It is in His action. His saying: 'without emanation of intent and request, so the commands and prohibitions indicating intent and request are, according to them, metaphors for the scientific requirement for the overflowing of good in the whole and the repulsion of evil.' His saying: 'And Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi'—this, along with his saying later 'al-Ka'bi said' etc., indicates that Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi is other than al-Ka'bi. And it was said in the discussion of power in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'Among them—i.e., among those who oppose the generality of His power, Exalted—is Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, known as al-Ka'bi.' This indicates that they are not distinct. But what is mentioned here agrees with what is in Abkar al-Afkar, where it was said in the discussion of will: 'Among them are al-Nazzam, al-Balkhi, and al-Ka'bi.' If the multiplicity of al-Balkhi and al-Ka'bi were established, then reconciliation between all would be possible. Know that the transmission of the book here contradicts what is in al-Arba'in, where it said: 'The meaning of His being willing, Exalted, in His own actions according to Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi is that He is their creator, and in the actions of others that He is the commander of them.' And likewise in Abkar al-Afkar where it said: 'As for al-Nazzam, al-Ka'bi, and al-Balkhi, they said that the description of God, Exalted, with will is a revealed matter, so if it is attributed to His acts, it does not mean anything except that He is their creator; and if attributed to the acts of servants, then it means that He, Exalted, is the commander of them.' Perhaps there is another transmission from them chosen by the author and the commentator, even if we have not come across it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents two minority views on divine will and then discusses a textual identity issue. First, al-Husayn al-Najjar defines will privatively as the absence of being compelled (ikrah) or overcome (ghalaba). Second, al-Ka'bi defines will in terms of God's action: for His own acts, will means He creates them; for human acts, it means He commands them. The passage then turns to a critical textual note: the commentator points out that the phrase in the matn mentioning 'Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi' alongside later references to 'al-Ka'bi' suggests they are different persons. However, in Sharh al-Maqasid, Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi is identified as al-Ka'bi. The commentator resolves this by noting that the source in Abkar al-Afkar mentions all three separately (al-Nazzam, al-Balkhi, al-Ka'bi), so if they are distinct, the apparent contradiction is resolved. The commentator also notes discrepancies in what al-Balkhi said according to different works (al-Arba'in vs. Abkar al-Afkar) and suggests there might be another transmission that the author and commentator have used.

Key Terms
أمر عدمي (privative matter)
عدم كونه مكرهاً ومغلوباً (absence of being compelled and overcome)
في فعله (in His action)
مجازات (metaphors)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ka'bi denies divine will entirely.
Correction: Al-Ka'bi redefines will as creation and command, not as an attribute distinct from action.

Checkpoint
What is al-Najjar's definition of divine will?
A) It is the creation of actions.
B) It is the absence of being compelled or overcome.
C) It is the intent to create.
D) It is the command for good.

Key Takeaway
Al-Najjar and al-Ka'bi offer alternative definitions of divine will that avoid positing an eternal attribute, viewing it either as a privation or as identical to action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that al-Najjar defined God's will as merely the absence of being compelled, do you feel that your own understanding of God's sovereignty has been shaped more by abstract definitions or by the living sense that He is absolutely free in His actions? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that God is not under any compulsion whatsoever – not even by your expectations or prayers?
?
Al-Ka'bi says that God's commands are metaphors for a necessary ordering for good. If you truly believed that every command in the Qur'an is rooted in a reality that necessarily brings good and repels evil for the whole universe, how would that change the way you approach obedience to those commands in your daily life? Would you still pick and choose based on your own convenience?
?
Both definitions in this lesson avoid attributing a psychological 'intent' to God. Does the idea that God's will might not involve a process of deliberation or desire disturb you because it makes Him seem less relatable? Or does it deepen your awe that His will is direct, effortless, and absolutely effective? Which of these reactions is more present in your heart right now, and why?

The Identity of al-Balkhi and al-Ka'bi
Textual note on whether al-Balkhi is distinct from al-Ka'bi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the textual debate over whether Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi is the same person as al-Ka'bi, and how the commentator reconciles conflicting sources.

Core Idea
Textual evidence suggests al-Balkhi and al-Ka'bi may be distinct, but later sources equate them.

In discussing al-Ka'bi's view, the text mentions 'Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi', raising the question: is this a different theologian? The commentator examines evidence from multiple works, showing that some sources treat them as one and others as distinct. This lesson explores how scholars resolve such textual discrepancies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The passage notes that the matn refers to 'Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi' separately from 'al-Ka'bi'. The commentator flags this as indicating they might be different people.
Look for the phrase 'أبي القاسم البلخي' and 'الكعبي'.
2
Examine the conflicting evidence
Read the quotations from Sharh al-Maqasid and Abkar al-Afkar. Sharh al-Maqasid identifies al-Balkhi as al-Ka'bi, while Abkar al-Afkar lists them separately.
The commentator notes that if they are distinct, the contradiction disappears.
3
Consider the possibility of another transmission
The commentator suggests that perhaps there is another version of their views that the author and commentator have chosen, even if we haven't found it.
This shows a cautious approach to textual criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And al-Husayn al-Najjar said: It—i.e., His being willing—is a privative matter, and it is the absence of His being compelled and overcome. And al-Ka'bi said: It is in His action. His saying: 'without emanation of intent and request, so the commands and prohibitions indicating intent and request are, according to them, metaphors for the scientific requirement for the overflowing of good in the whole and the repulsion of evil.' And it was said in the discussion of power in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'Among them—i.e., among those who oppose the generality of His power, Exalted—is Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, known as al-Ka'bi.' First, al-Husayn al-Najjar defines will privatively as the absence of being compelled (ikrah) or overcome (ghalaba). Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The evidence is conflicting; some sources identify them, others treat them separately.

What reason does the commentator give for thinking al-Balkhi and al-Ka'bi might be distinct?
A) Sharh al-Maqasid calls al-Balkhi 'al-Ka'bi'.
B) The matn mentions both names separately in a way that implies they are different.
C) Al-Arba'in gives a different definition of will for al-Balkhi.

The identity of al-Balkhi vs. al-Ka'bi is a textual puzzle that the commentator attempts to solve by comparing multiple sources, showing careful scholarly methodology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage shows a scholar acknowledging his own limited access to transmissions: 'perhaps there is another transmission... we have not come across it.' In your own spiritual life, where are you pretending to have certainty while secretly knowing you have not gathered all the evidence? Will you today take one step toward intellectual honesty by admitting a gap in your knowledge?
