Three Mu'tazilite Definitions of God's Will
Presents three Mu'tazilite definitions of God's will: knowledge, privation, command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main Mu'tazilite views on the nature of God's will and how they differ from each other.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites reduced God's will to knowledge, privation, or command.

The Mu'tazilite school, while affirming that God is willing, proposed several reductionist interpretations of what His will means. This passage from al-Ījī's Mawāqif surveys three major definitions: will as knowledge of benefit, as a privative attribute, or as command. Understanding these views is essential for grasping later Ash'arite critiques.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Get a sense of the three distinct views presented.
Note that the passage starts with the view of Abu al-Husayn and others, then moves to al-Najjār, then al-Ka'bī.
2
Identify the first definition
Focus on the view attributed to Abu al-Husayn and several Mu'tazilite leaders. What do they say God's will is? How do they argue by analogy to human experience?
Key term: 'dā'īyah' (motive).
3
Identify the second definition
Examine al-Husayn al-Najjār's definition. He says God's will is a 'privative matter' (amr 'adamī). What does he mean by that?
Think about what it means to say God's will is simply not being compelled or overcome.
4
Identify the third definition
Look at al-Ka'bī's view. He distinguishes between God's will regarding His own acts and regarding others' acts. What is each?
For His own acts: knowledge of benefit; for others' acts: command.
5
Compare and contrast the three views
Reflect on how these definitions differ. Which one reduces will to an attribute? Which one treats it as a negation? Which one splits it into two concepts?
This helps you see the diversity within the Mu'tazilite school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أنه تعالى مريد وقال أبو الحسين وجماعة من رؤساء المعتزلة كالنظام والجاحظ والعلاف وأبي القاسم البلخي ومحمود الخوارزمي : إرادته تعالى ( هو علمه ينفع في الفعل وذلك كما يجده كل عاقل من نفسه إن ظنه أو اعتقاده ينفع في الفعل) أو علمه به ( يوجب الفعل ويسميه ) أبو الحسين ( بالداعية) ولما استحال الظن والاعتGameقاد في حقه تعالى انحصر داعيته في العلم بالنفع ونقل عن أبي الحسين وحده أنه قال : الإرادة في الشاهد زائدة على الداعي ) وقال ( الحسين ( النجار : إنه ) أي كونه مريداً (أمر عدمي وهو عدم كونه مكرهاً ومغلوباً ، ( وقال الكعبي : هي في فعله العلم بما فيه من المصلحة ) وفي فعل غيره الأمر به
The Fourth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: That He, exalted, is willing. And Abu al-Husayn and a group of the leaders of the Mu'tazila, such as al-Nazzam, al-Jahiz, al-‘Allaf, Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi, said: His will, exalted, is His knowledge that is beneficial in the act, and that is like what every rational person finds in himself: that his assumption or belief is beneficial in the act, or His knowledge of it necessitates the act, and Abu al-Husayn calls it 'al-dā'īyah' (the motive). And since assumption and belief are impossible with respect to Him, exalted, His motive is confined to knowledge of benefit. And it is transmitted from Abu al-Husayn alone that he said: Will in the witnessed (human context) is additional to the motive. And al-Husayn al-Najjār said: That is, His being willing is a privative matter, and it is not being compelled or overcome. And al-Ka‘bī said: It (will) is, in relation to His own act, knowledge of what is beneficial therein, and in relation to the act of another, command.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three distinct Mu'tazilite views on the nature of God's will. It begins by presenting the view of Abu al-Husayn and several other leading Mu'tazilites: that God's will is His knowledge of benefit (علم بنفع). They argue by analogy to human experience: a person's belief or assumption that something is beneficial moves them to act. Since assumptions and beliefs are impossible for God, His motive (داعية) is solely knowledge of benefit. Abu al-Husayn alone is reported to have distinguished will from the motive in human beings, but for God, will is identified with that knowledge. Next, al-Husayn al-Najjār defines God's will as a privative attribute (أمر عدمي): it is simply the absence of being compelled or overcome (عدم كونه مكرهاً ومغلوباً). Finally, al-Ka‘bī distinguishes between God's will regarding His own acts—which is knowledge of what is beneficial—and His will regarding others' acts, which is command (الأمر به). Thus, the passage surveys three different reductionist accounts of the divine attribute of will.

علم بنفع
داعية
أمر عدمي
مكره
مغلوب
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites held the same view on God's will.
Correction: This passage shows significant diversity: at least three different definitions exist among Mu'tazilite scholars.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Husayn, what is God's will?
A) His power
B) His knowledge of benefit
C) A privative attribute
D) A command

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites did not have a single definition of God's will; they proposed several reductionist accounts, each with distinct implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read three radically different definitions of God's will. Which of these definitions, if any, best describes how you understand your own will? Do you reduce your will to knowledge, negation of coercion, or command? How does that affect your sense of responsibility before God?
?
The Mu'tazilites tried to define God's will in terms of human categories. Where in your life do you try to fit God into your own limited understanding of will? What would it cost you to admit that God's will is beyond your full comprehension?
?
Al-Najjār defined will as the absence of being compelled. When was the last time you acted without any external compulsion, purely from your own desire? Can you point to a single choice you made today that was truly uncompelled? If not, are you living as a slave to circumstances rather than as a free agent created by a willing God?

The Asharite Proof That Will Is a Third Attribute
Proof that will is a separate attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument used by the Ash‘arites to prove that God's will is a distinct attribute separate from power and knowledge.

Core Idea
Will is the specifying principle that power alone cannot explain.

This lesson presents a classic kalam proof for the necessity of a distinct attribute of will in God. The Ash‘arites, joined by many Mu‘tazilites, argue that power and knowledge are insufficient to explain why one of two equally possible opposites actually occurs at a specific time. A specifying principle (mukhaṣṣiṣ) must exist, and that is will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'ضدان' and 'مخصص'.
2
Identify the key concepts
Focus on the terms: opposites (ضدان), specification (تخصيص), and preferrer (مرجح). Understand how they fit into the argument.
The argument relies on the equal relation of power to both opposites and all times.
3
Walk through the logical steps
Follow the argument: (1) opposites are equally related to power, (2) each opposite is equally related to all times, (3) specification requires a specifying principle, (4) power cannot be that principle, (5) knowledge cannot be that principle, (6) therefore will must be a third attribute.
The proof eliminates alternatives to establish will as the only possible specifying principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أصحابنا ووافقهم جمهور معتزلة البصرة : إنها صفة ثالثة مغايرة للعلم والقدرة توجب تلك الصفة تخصيص أحد المقدورين بالوقوع واحتجوا عليه بأن الضدين نسبتهما إلى القدرة سواء إذ كما يمكن أن يقع بها هذا الضد يمكن أن يقع بها ذاك الضد من غير فرق بينهما في إمكان الوقوع بها وكل واحد منهما فرض وقوعه بها فإن نسبته إلى الأوقات المعينة كلها سواء فكما يمكن أن يقع في وقته الذي وقع فيه يمكن أن يقع قبله وبعده فلا بد لتخصيصه بالوقوع دون ضده ولتخصيص وقوعه بوقته المعين دون سائر الأوقات من ثبوت مخصص يقتضيه وإلا لزم ترجيح أحد المتساويين على الآخر لا بمرجح هذا خلف وليس ذلك المخصص القدرة لاستواء نسبتها إليهما وإلى الأوقات كلها كما عرفت ولا العلم لأنه تبع الوقوع
And our companions (the Ash‘arites) said—and the majority of the Basran Mu‘tazila agreed with them: That it (will) is a third attribute, different from knowledge and power, and that attribute necessitates the specification of one of the two possible objects to occurrence. And they argued for it: Because the two opposites have an equal relation to power—just as this opposite can occur through it, that opposite can occur through it with no difference between them in possibility of occurrence through it; and each one of them, assuming its occurrence through it, its relation to all specific times is equal—just as it can occur at its own time when it occurs, it can occur before or after. Therefore, for its specification to occurrence rather than its opposite, and for the specification of its occurrence at its specific time rather than other times, there must be a specifying principle that necessitates it; otherwise, the preferment of one of two equals over the other without a preferrer would be necessary, which is absurd. And that specifying principle is not power, because of the equality of its relation to both and to all times, as you have known; nor is it knowledge, because knowledge is consequent upon occurrence.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a classic kalam proof for the existence of a distinct attribute of will in God. The Asharites, along with many Basran Mu'tazilites, assert that will is a third attribute (صفة ثالثة) separate from knowledge and power. The proof proceeds by examining two opposites (ضدان), such as an action and its omission. Both are equally possible in relation to God's power: power can produce either one with equal ease. Moreover, both opposites have an equal relation to all moments of time: a specific action could logically occur earlier or later than it actually does. Therefore, for one opposite to occur rather than the other, and for it to occur at a specific time rather than another, there must be a specifying principle (مخصص) that determines it. Without such a principle, one would have the preferment of one equal over another without a preferrer (ترجيح لا بمرجح), which is impossible. The specifying principle cannot be power, because power is equally related to both opposites and all times. It cannot be knowledge, because knowledge is a consequence of the occurrence (تبع الوقوع)—God knows something because it will occur, not vice versa. Therefore, the specifying principle must be something else, namely will. Thus, the argument establishes will as a distinct attribute necessary for the specification of one possibility over an equal possibility.

Key Terms
صفة ثالثة
تخصيص
مخصص
ضدان
ترجيح لا بمرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is merely about logical possibility and does not require a real cause.
Correction: The proof argues for a real specifying principle (مخصص) in God, not just a logical concept. It grounds the existence of a distinct attribute of will in the need for a real cause of specification.

Checkpoint
Why is power not sufficient as the specifying principle for one of two opposites?
A) Because power is finite
B) Because power relates equally to both opposites and all times
C) Because power cannot create opposites
D) Because power is a created attribute

Key Takeaway
The Ash‘arites prove will is a distinct attribute by showing power and knowledge cannot account for the specification of one possibility over an equal possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event, including your next thought, is specified by God's will alone, where does that leave your sense of personal autonomy? Will you surrender your illusions of control and acknowledge that even your choices are determined by His will?
?
The proof shows that power and knowledge are not enough to explain why one thing happens instead of another. Are you willing to admit that the specific details of your life—your successes, failures, and even your sins—are ultimately traced back to God's will, not to your own ability or foresight? If so, how does that reshape your gratitude, your repentance, and your trust in Him?
?
The specifying principle (will) is beyond your direct perception. Does this unseen reality make you uneasy, or does it draw you closer to relying on God's wisdom in every circumstance? What would it look like for you to live today as if each moment is precisely willed by God and not by chance?

Response to al-Tusi's Objection on the Specifying Principle
Objection and answer clarifying the role of motive in free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'arite response to al-Tusi's objection preserves their doctrine of free agency by distinguishing between a generic preferrer and a motive (dā'ī).

Core Idea
God's will is a specifying principle, but not a coercive motive.

Al-Tusi accused the Ash'arites of inconsistency: they affirm a specifying principle for creation yet claim a free agent can act without a preferrer. In this passage, the commentator provides the key distinction that resolves the tension—between the generic 'preferrer' (مُرَجِّح) and the specific 'motive' (داعية). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Ash'arite position on divine will and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection by al-Tusi
Begin by reading the passage to understand al-Tusi's claim that the Ash'arite view is self-contradictory. Note his reasoning: they require a specifying principle for one of two equals, yet they also say the free agent can act without a preferrer.
The objection is based on a conflation of two different senses of 'preferrer'.
2
Identify the key distinction in the answer
The answer clarifies that the negated preferrer is the 'dā'ī' (motive), not the will. This means the will itself is a legitimate specifying principle that does not imply compulsion.
The Arabic terms: مُرَجِّح (generic preferrer) vs. داعية (motive).
3
See the author's confirmation
Al-Iji's earlier statement confirms that he denies a motive, not a preferrer. This reinforces the consistency of the Ash'arite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وليس ذلك المخصص القدرة اعترض عليه الطوسي في تلخيص المحصل بأنه مناقض لما ذهبوا إليه من أن المختار يمكنه الترجيح من غير مرجح وجوابه : أن المراد بالمرجح المنفي لزومه هو الداعية لا المطلق حتى يشمل الإرادة أيضاً. وإليه أشار المصنف حيث قال مباحث الإرادة من الموقف الثالث : لا أقول لا يكون للفعل مرجح على عدمه بل لا يكون إليه داع.
His saying: (And that specifying principle is not power) — al-Tusi objected to it in his Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal that it contradicts what they (the Ash‘arites) hold, that the free agent is able to prefer one thing over another without a preferrer. The answer: that what is meant by the negated preferrer that is necessary is the 'dā‘ī' (motive), not the absolute preferrer, so that it does not include the will as well. And the author alluded to this where he said, in the discussions on will from the Third Standpoint: I do not say that there is no preferrer for the act over its non-existence; rather, there is no motive for it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. Al-Tusi objects to the Asharite proof that a specifying principle is necessary for one of two equals. He argues that this contradicts the Asharite doctrine that a free agent (مختار) can prefer one action over another without any cause (مرجح). The answer clarifies the terminology: the 'preferrer' (مرجح) that is denied in the free agent is specifically the internal motive (داعية), not any specifying principle whatsoever. Thus, the will itself remains a valid specifying principle—it is the decisive factor that tips the balance. The author (al-Iji) confirms this in his earlier discussion, stating that he does not deny a preferrer for the act but denies a motive (داع) that would necessitate it. Therefore, the Asharite position is consistent: will is a real specifying principle, but it is not a 'dā'ī' that would compel the agent. The objection arises from conflating the generic 'preferrer' with the specific notion of motive.

Key Terms
مختار
مرجح
داعية
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'arites deny any cause or preferrer for God's actions, leading to complete indeterminism.
Correction: The Ash'arites affirm the will (irāda) as a genuine specifying principle and cause. What they deny is a compelling motive (dā'ī) that would necessitate the act. The will is a real preferrer, but not a coercive one.

Checkpoint

A) The will (irāda)
B) The power (qudra)
C) The motive (dā'ī)
D) The knowledge ('ilm)

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite position is consistent: the will is a specifying principle, but not a coercive motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's actions—like why He created you or why He allows trials—do you sometimes feel He is acting without reason? How does the truth that His will itself is a specifying principle, not a coercive motive, change your perception of His justice and wisdom? Will you commit to trusting His will even when you don't see the motive?
?
In your own life, do you ever confuse having a reason to do something with being compelled to do it? How can the distinction between a 'motive' and a 'specifying principle' free you from feeling trapped by your own desires? What specific decision today can you make as a free act of will, not as a reaction to a compelling motive?

Proof That Will Is a Distinct Attribute
Al-Iji proves that will is a third attribute distinct from knowledge and power via the specification argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that divine will must be a distinct attribute from knowledge and power, based on the need for a specifier for contingent events.

Core Idea
Will is the specifier that chooses among equally possible alternatives.

Al-Iji presents a classic kalam proof that divine will is not reducible to knowledge or power. The argument hinges on the observation that power is equally related to opposites, and knowledge merely reflects what occurs; something must tip the balance. That something is will. In this passage, you will see how the argument unfolds step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical connectors (فلا بد، وإلا لزم).
The passage uses technical terms like تخصيص and مخصص.
2
Identify the key premise: equality of power
The argument begins by stating that both opposites are equally possible for power, and equally possible at all times. Grasp this foundation.
3
Follow the need for a specifier
Since equality cannot produce a choice, a specifier (مخصص) is necessary. This is the core of the proof.
The Arabic uses ترجيح أحد المتساويين.
4
Eliminate knowledge as the specifier
Knowledge is secondary to occurrence, so it cannot be the cause. Understand the direction of dependence.
Knowledge is a copy (حكاية) of the occurrence.
5
Conclude that will is the third attribute
After eliminating power and knowledge, the specifier must be something else, namely will. The passage also notes it's distinct from life, hearing, etc.
This establishes will as a distinct attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أصحابنا ووافقهم جمهور معتزلة البصرة : (إنها صفة ثالثة مغايرة للعلم والقدرة توجب) تلك الصفة ) تخصيص أحد المقدورين بالوقوع واحتجوا عليه ) أي على ثبوت تلك الصفة ) بأن الضدين نسبتهما إلى القدرة سواء إذ كما يمكن أن يقع بها هذا الضد ( يمكن أن يقع بها ذاك ) الضد ( من غير فرق ) بينهما في إمكان الوقوع بها ( وكل واحد منهما فرض ) وقوعه بها ( فإن نسبته إلى الأوقات ( المعينة كلها ( سواء فكما يمكن أن يقع في وقته الذي وقع فيه يمكن أن يقع قبله وبعده فلا بد ( لتخصيصه بالوقوع دون ضده ولتخصيص وقوعه بوقته المعين دون سائر الأوقات (من) ثبوت ( مخصص) يقتضيه ( وإلا لزم ترجيح أحد المتساويين) على الآخر ( لا بمرجح ) هذا خلف (وليس) ذلك المخصص ( القدرة لاستواء نسبتها إليهما وإلى الأوقات كلها كما عرفت ) ولا العلم لأنه تبع الوقوع ) أي العلم بوقوع شيء في وقت معين تابع لكونه بحيث يقع فيه لأنه ظله وحكاية عنه ( فلا يكون الوقوع تبعاً له وإلا لزم الدور فإذن هو) أي المخصص ( أمر ثالث يكون مغايراً للحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام أيضاً إذ لا يصلح شيء منها للتخصيص قطعاً ( وهو المطلوب
And our companions said, and the majority of the Mu'tazila of Basra agreed with them: it (will) is a third attribute distinct from knowledge and power, necessitating the specification of one of the two possible objects to occur. And they argued for it (the affirmation of that attribute) by saying that the two opposites are equal in relation to power, since just as it is possible for this opposite to occur by it (power), so it is possible for that opposite to occur by it, without any difference between them in the possibility of occurrence by it. And each one of them, if we assume its occurrence by it, then its relation to all specific times is equal; so just as it can occur in its time in which it did occur, it can occur before it and after it. Therefore, for its specification to occur as opposed to its opposite, and for the specification of its occurrence at its specific time as opposed to other times, there must be a specifier that necessitates it; otherwise, it would entail the preferment of one of two equals without a preferrer, which is a contradiction. And that specifier is not power, because its relation to them and to all times is equal, as you have known; nor is it knowledge, because it is secondary to occurrence, meaning knowledge of the occurrence of something at a specific time is dependent on it being such that it occurs at that time, because it is a shadow and a copy of it. So the occurrence is not secondary to knowledge; otherwise, circularity would follow. Therefore, the specifier is a third thing distinct from life, hearing, sight, and speech as well, since none of them is suitable for specification, categorically. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine will is a distinct attribute. The author begins by stating the position of the Ash'aris and the Basran Mu'tazila that will is a third attribute beyond knowledge and power. The argument proceeds: the two opposite possibilities are equal with respect to power, meaning power is equally capable of producing either. They are also equal with respect to times; any possible act could occur at any time. Therefore, for one opposite to occur at a specific time rather than another, a specifier is needed. This specifier cannot be power, because power is equally related to both. It cannot be knowledge, because knowledge is a copy of the occurrence and follows it, so it cannot be the cause of the occurrence. The specifier must therefore be something else, namely will. This will is also distinct from other attributes like life, hearing, sight, and speech, as they also cannot serve as specifiers. The conclusion is that will is a third attribute, which is what was to be proved.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
صفة ثالثة (third attribute)
علم (knowledge)
قدرة (power)
تخصيص (specification)
مخصص (specifier)
ضدين (two opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is reducible to knowledge or power; no distinct attribute is needed.
Correction: The argument shows that neither power nor knowledge can account for specification, so a distinct attribute of will is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the proof in this passage, why can't power serve as the specifier for one of two opposites?
A) Because power is not an attribute of God
B) Because power is equally related to both opposites and all times
C) Because power is dependent on will
D) Because power only applies to possible things

Key Takeaway
Divine will is a distinct attribute because it is the specifier that chooses among equally possible alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's will is distinct from His knowledge, meaning He freely chooses among equally possible worlds, then every event in your life is a deliberate act of His will. Do you truly live as if each moment—even your failures—was specifically chosen for you? Or do you secretly attribute some things to chance or your own planning? Will you today surrender the illusion of control and thank Him for His specific choices?
?
The proof shows that knowledge merely copies reality; it is not the cause. Have you ever assumed that merely knowing what is right would automatically make you do it? Where in your life have you let knowledge replace the act of willing? What specific step of will are you avoiding right now?
?
If Allah's will is absolutely free and not compelled by His power or knowledge, then He is not a machine. Do you sometimes think of Allah as having no choice, that He 'had to' create or 'had to' decree certain things? Repent from such deterministic thinking and worship the One who freely wills all that occurs—including your own repentance.

The Objection That Will Needs a Specifier and Its Answer
Objection that will itself would require a specifier, and the Ash'ari answer denying equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine will would require a specifier leading to infinite regress, and the Ash'ari response that will is essentially specific, thus avoiding the regress.

Core Idea
Will is not neutral: its attachment to one opposite is due to its own essence, not an external specifier.

In this passage, the author presents a key objection against the Ash'ari understanding of divine will. The objector argues that if will is like power in being equally related to opposites, then will itself would need a specifier, leading to an infinite regress of attributes. The answer is a decisive denial of that equality, grounding will's specificity in its own essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector claims that will's relation to opposites and times is equal, thus requiring a specifier.
Notice the phrase 'فإن قيل' which signals the objection.
2
Understand the problem
If will needs a specifier, then a fourth attribute is needed, leading to an infinite regress of attributes.
The regress is avoided only if the premise of equality is false.
3
Identify the answer
Read the answer beginning with 'قلنا'. The author denies the equality and asserts that will's attachment is by its own essence.
The key phrase is 'بل هي صفة تعلقها بأحدهما لذاتها المخصوصة'.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows how the Ash'ari avoid infinite regress by making will essentially specific, without needing an external specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل، الإرادة من حيث هي إرادة نسبتها إلى الضدين وإلى الأوقات ( سواء ) إذ كما يجوز تعلقها بهذا الضد يجوز تعلقها بالضد الآخر، وكما يجوز إرادة وقوع واحد منهما في وقت يجوز إرادة وقوعه في وقت آخر ( فيعود الكلام فيها فيقال : لا بد للتخصيص من مخصص مغاير للعلم والقدرة والإرادة فتثبت صفة رابعة ) ويلزم التسلسل . قلنا : لا نسلم ذلك ) أي تساوي نسبة الإرادة إلى الضدين والأوقات حتى يلزم التسلسل ( بل) هي صفة ( تعلقها بأحدهما ) ووقوعه في وقت معين لذاتها المخصوصة فلا حاجة إلى صفة أخرى.
For it is said: will, insofar as it is will, its relation to the two opposites and to times is equal, since just as its attachment to this opposite is permissible, so its attachment to the other opposite is permissible, and just as willing the occurrence of one of them at a time is permissible, so willing its occurrence at another time is permissible. Then the discussion returns to it, and it is said: there must be for specification a specifier distinct from knowledge, power, and will, so a fourth attribute is established, and infinite regress follows. We say: we do not accept that, i.e., the equality of the relation of will to the two opposites and times such that infinite regress would follow. Rather, it is an attribute whose attachment to one of them and its occurrence at a specific time is by virtue of its own specific essence, so there is no need for another attribute.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if will is like power in being equally related to both opposites and all times, then will itself would need a specifier, leading to an infinite regress of attributes. The answer denies the premise: will is not equally related. Rather, by its very essence, will is attached to one specific opposite and one specific time. Therefore, no further specifier is needed, and the regress is avoided.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
تعلق (attachment)
ذات (essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view of divine will necessarily leads to an infinite regress of attributes because will itself would require a specifier.
Correction: The Ash'ari deny that will is equally related to opposites and times. Will is essentially specific, so no external specifier is needed.

A) They accept the regress as infinite
B) They deny that will is equally related to opposites and times
C) They introduce a fourth attribute
D) They say will is not an attribute

Key Takeaway
Will is essentially specific, so no external specifier is needed, avoiding infinite regress.

Objection That Will Necessitates and Negates Choice, and Answer
Objection and answer regarding will's essential specificity and free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if God's will is essentially specific it seems to necessitate the willed act and negate free choice, and the kalam response that necessity-by-choice is compatible with and indeed fulfills choice.

Core Idea
Necessity by choice does not negate choice; it fulfills it.

This lesson tackles a classic objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of divine will: if God's will is essentially directed to one specific side of an act, doesn't that make that side necessary and remove any element of choice? The answer introduces a key distinction between coerced necessity and necessity that arises from choice. Understanding this response is crucial for grasping how divine freedom and determinism are reconciled in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objection in Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: 'فإن قيل' (for it is said) introduces the opponent's argument. The key claim is that will's essential specificity leads to necessitation (إيجاب) and negation of choice (سلب الاختيار).
The objector sees a contradiction between essential specificity and free choice.
2
Study the answer
Read the answer beginning with 'قلنا' (we say). The core response is that 'وجوب الشيء بالاختيار لا ينافي الاختيار' – the necessity of a thing by choice does not contradict choice. Notice the phrase 'بل يحققه لأنه فرعه' (rather it actualizes it because it is its branch).
This is a distinction between two kinds of necessity.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about the difference between something being necessary without choice (like coercion) and something being necessary because it is chosen. In the latter, the necessity follows from the choice and does not undermine it. Consider how this applies to divine will: God's willing an act makes it necessary in the sense that it will occur, but that necessity arises from His free choice.
This distinction is central to the Ash'ari conception of divine power and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ( إذا تعلقت الإرادة لذاتها بأحد جانبي الفعل في وقت معين وعلى وجه مخصوص (فيجب ذلك الجانب ) في ذلك الوقت على ذلك الوجه (ويمتنع) الجانب (الآخر) وحينئذ (فيلزم) الإيجاب و ( سلب الاختيار. قلنا : ) أي لأنا نقول ( وجوب الشيء بالاختيار لا ينافي الاختيار بل يحققه لأنه فرعه
For it is said: If will attaches by its essence to one of the two sides of the act at a specific time and in a specific manner, then that side becomes necessary at that time in that manner, and the other side becomes impossible. Then it entails necessitation and negation of choice. We say: that is, we say: the necessity of a thing by choice does not contradict choice; rather, it actualizes it, because it is its branch.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and its answer. The objector argues that if will is essentially directed to one specific side, it makes that side necessary and the other side impossible, thereby negating free choice. The answer distinguishes between two kinds of necessity. Necessity that arises from one's own choice (i.e., choosing something makes it necessary in the sense that it will definitely happen) does not negate choice. On the contrary, it is the very result of choice and thus confirms it. The act is necessary because of the choice, so choice is not negated.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
اختيار (choice)
وجوب (necessity)
إيجاب (necessitation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any form of necessity in God's actions would contradict His free will, confusing coerced necessity with necessity-by-choice.
Correction: Necessity that follows from choice (like a decision to act) does not negate the freedom of that choice; it is the fulfillment of it.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that will's essential specificity leads to necessitation and negation of choice?
A) By denying that will is essentially specific
B) By asserting that choice is independent of will
C) By stating that necessity by choice does not oppose choice
D) By accepting the objection and modifying the definition of will

Key Takeaway
The necessity that results from choice does not negate choice; it is the fulfillment of choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your own experience shows that choice leads to a kind of necessity, why do you resist believing the same about God's actions? What would it look like for you to fully embrace that God's will is both completely free and perfectly necessary?
?
This lesson says that necessity-by-choice actualizes choice, not negates it. When you struggle to accept a difficult divine decree, are you holding onto a false idea that God's will would be better if it were less specific? Are you willing to commit to trusting that God's choice is always better, even when it feels like a loss of your own control? What step can you take today to surrender to His wiser choice?

Will as the Inherently Specific Attribute: Refuting Infinite Regress
Defines will as the specifying factor and refutes infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school holds that divine will does not require a further specifying factor, and how the objection of infinite regress is refuted.

Core Idea
Will is inherently specific; it needs no extra attribute to choose opposites.

In this passage, the author defends the Ash'ari doctrine that God's will is a distinct and inherently specifying attribute. He first argues that the specifying factor cannot be knowledge, power, or other attributes, so it must be will itself. Then he addresses the objection that will is neutral and would require yet another attribute, leading to infinite regress. The answer is that will by its own essence attaches to one specific opposite, stopping the regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Notice the structure: premise, objection, answer.
The parentheses in Arabic indicate commentary interjections.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent claims that will is equally related to all opposites and times, so it cannot specify without a further factor.
This objection leads to infinite regress.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author denies the equality and asserts that will attaches to one specific opposite by its own essence (لذاتها المخصوصة).
This stops the regress.
4
Connect to broader theology
Understand that this defends the Ash'ari view of will as a sufficient specifying attribute, distinct from knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أنه تعالى مريد فلا يكون الوقوع تبعاً له وإلا لزم الدور فإذن هو) أي المخصص ( أمر ثالث يكون مغايراً للحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام أيضاً إذ لا يصلح شيء منها للتخصيص قطعاً ( وهو المطلوب، فإن قيل : الإرادة من حيث هي إرادة نسبتها إلى الضدين وإلى الأوقات ( سواء ) إذ كما يجوز تعلقها بهذا الضد يجوز تعلقها بالضد الآخر، وكما يجوز إرادة وقوع واحد منهما في وقت يجوز إرادة وقوعه في وقت آخر ( فيعود الكلام فيها فيقال : لا بد للتخصيص من مخصص مغاير للعلم والقدرة والإرادة فتثبت صفة رابعة ) ويلزم التسلسل . قلنا : لا نسلم ذلك ) أي تساوي نسبة الإرادة إلى الضدين والأوقات حتى يلزم التسلسل ( بل) هي صفة ( تعلقها بأحدهما ) ووقوعه في وقت معين لذاتها المخصوصة فلا حاجة إلى صفة أخرى.
The fourth observatory - the fifth investigation: concerning that He, exalted, is willing. So occurrence cannot be consequent upon it, otherwise circularity would follow. So it [the specifying factor] is a third thing distinct from life, hearing, sight, and speech also, since none of these is suitable for specification at all. And that is what is sought. For if it is said: will, insofar as it is will, its relation to the two opposites and to times is equal, for just as its attachment to this opposite is permissible, so its attachment to the other opposite is permissible, and just as willing the occurrence of one of them at a time is permissible, so willing its occurrence at another time is permissible. Then the discourse returns to it, and it is said: specification must have a specifying factor distinct from knowledge, power, and will, so a fourth attribute is established, and infinite regress follows. We say: we do not concede that — i.e., the equality of the relation of will to the two opposites and times such that infinite regress would follow. Rather, it is an attribute whose attachment to one of them and its occurrence at a specific time is due to its own particular essence, so no other attribute is needed.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine will is a distinct attribute that serves as the specifying factor (mukhaṣṣiṣ) for creation. The argument begins by stating that if occurrence were merely consequent upon God's essence, circularity would result; therefore the specifying factor must be a third thing beyond life, hearing, sight, and speech, since none of these can specify. The opponent objects that will is equally related to both opposites and all times, so it cannot specify without a further factor, leading to infinite regress. The answer refutes this by asserting that will is not neutral; its attachment to one specific opposite and time is due to its own particular essence. Hence no additional attribute is required. The author thus defends the Ash'ari position that will is inherently specific.

Key Terms
إرادة
مخصص
تسلسل
ضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That will is neutral and requires a further attribute to specify.
Correction: The passage shows will is inherently specific by its own essence, so no further attribute is needed.

Checkpoint

B) Because will attaches to one opposite by its own essence.
C) Because knowledge and power can specify.
D) Because infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Will, as a divine attribute, is inherently specific and requires no further specifying factor, thus avoiding infinite regress.

The Objection to Will's Specificity and the Core Answer
Objection and initial answer: inherent specificity leads to compulsion; answer: necessity by choice does not negate choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's claim that inherent specificity of will leads to compulsion, and the initial answer that necessity by choice does not negate choice but rather realizes it.

Core Idea
Necessity born from choice is not compulsion—it realizes choice.

The objector argues that if divine will is inherently specific, then the willed act becomes necessary and its opposite impossible, which would imply compulsion. But the responder immediately clarifies that necessity arising from choice is not a negation of choice; rather, it is the very fruit and realization of choice. This short exchange sets the stage for a deeper discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the conditional structure: if will attaches by essence to one specific side, then that side is necessary and the other impossible, leading to compulsion and negation of choice.
The objector uses the term 'إيجاب' for compulsion.
2
Understand the initial answer
The answer is concise: necessity that comes from choice does not negate choice; it actually realizes it, because it is the branch (far') of choice.
Think of choice as the root and necessity as the fruit.
3
See the structure
This passage is a simple objection-answer pair. The objector states a conditional consequence; the responder gives a definitional counter.
This sets up the more nuanced discussion in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ( إذا تعلقت الإرادة لذاتها بأحد جانبي الفعل في وقت معين وعلى وجه مخصوص (فيجب ذلك الجانب ) في ذلك الوقت على ذلك الوجه (ويمتنع) الجانب (الآخر) وحينئذ (فيلزم) الإيجاب و ( سلب الاختيار. قلنا : ) أي لأنا نقول ( وجوب الشيء بالاختيار لا ينافي الاختيار بل يحققه لأنه فرعه وهاهنا بحث وهو أن إرادة أحد الضدين إن كانت مغايرة لإرادة الآخر وكانت كل واحدة منهما لذاتها متعلقة بأحدهما على التعيين اتجه أن يقال : إذا لزم أحد الإرادتين ذات المريد لم يمكن له الإرادة المتعلقة بالجانب الآخر بدلاً عن الإرادة الأولى فلا قدرة بمعنى صحة الفعل والترك، وإذا لم يلزم جاز تجدد الإرادة وحدوثها وإن لم تكن مغايرة لها بل تتعلق إرادة واحدة تارة بهذا وتارة بذاك فإذا كان تعلقها بأحدهما لذاتها لم يتصور تعلقها بالآخر ويلزم الإيجاب، وما ذكره من أن الوجوب المترتب على الاختيار لا ينافيه إنما يصحح في القدرة بمعنى إن شاء فعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل كما سبق تصويره فتذكر.
For if it is said: if will attaches by its essence to one side of an act at a specific time and in a specific manner, then that side becomes necessary at that time in that manner, and the other side becomes impossible, and then compulsion and negation of choice follow. We say: the necessity of a thing by choice does not negate choice, but rather realizes it, because it is its branch. And here there is a discussion: if the will of one of two opposites is distinct from the will of the other, and each of them by its essence attaches specifically to one of them, then it may be said: if one of the two wills necessarily accompanies the essence of the one who wills, then he cannot have the will attached to the other side instead of the first will, so there is no power in the sense of soundness of act and omission; but if it does not necessarily accompany, then renewal and origination of will are possible, even if they are not distinct, but rather a single will sometimes attaches to this and sometimes to that. So if its attachment to one of them is by its essence, its attachment to the other is inconceivable, and compulsion follows. And what he mentioned — that necessity consequent upon choice does not negate it — is only valid for power in the sense of 'if He wills He does, and if He does not will He does not do', as was previously described. So remember.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a detailed answer. The objector argues that if will is inherently specific to one side of an act, then that side becomes necessary and the other impossible, implying compulsion (ijāb) and negation of choice. The initial answer is that necessity by choice does not negate choice; rather, it is a consequence of choice and thus affirms it. The author then expands the discussion (وهاهنا بحث) by considering whether the wills for the two opposites are distinct or not. If they are distinct and each necessarily accompanies the divine essence, then only one will is possible, eliminating power to choose the opposite. If they are not necessarily accompanying, then a single will could attach to either side, but if it attaches by essence to one, the other is inconceivable, again implying compulsion. The final clarification is that the earlier answer about necessity by choice applies to power defined as 'if He wills He does, if not He does not' — a power that includes both act and omission. Thus the objection is nuanced, but the core response is maintained.

Key Terms
إيجاب
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any necessity negates choice.
Correction: This passage shows that necessity arising from choice is compatible with genuine volition; it is the consequence and confirmation of choice.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that inherent specificity leads to compulsion?
A) He denies that will is inherently specific.
B) He argues that necessity by choice does not negate choice.
C) He claims that compulsion is acceptable.
D) He introduces a fourth attribute to resolve the problem.

Key Takeaway
Necessity that results from choice does not eliminate choice; it is the consequence and confirmation of choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can acknowledging the truth of 'necessity by choice' change how you view that situation, and what step will you take today to own that choice?

If yes, how does that change the way you pray for guidance? If no, what is holding you back from embracing this tension, and how will you seek to understand it better?

The Deeper Discussion: Distinct Wills and the Meaning of Power
Expanded discussion: scenarios of distinct and non-distinct wills, and the precise meaning of power that saves choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced analysis of whether two wills for opposites are distinct or not, and how the reply about necessity by choice applies only to power defined as the ability to choose.

Core Idea
The earlier answer holds only if power means 'if He wills He does, if not He does not'.

After the initial exchange, the author adds a deeper investigation. He examines two scenarios: whether the wills for two opposites are distinct or not, and shows that in both cases compulsion may still appear. He then clarifies that the earlier answer only works for a specific understanding of power—one that includes the genuine possibility of both act and omission.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two scenarios
First, if the wills for opposites are distinct and each necessarily accompanies the essence, then only one will is possible. Second, if they are not distinct but a single will attaches by essence to one side, then the other side is inconceivable.
In both cases, the objector's point about compulsion appears to hold.
2
See the consequences
In the first scenario, there is no power in the sense of 'soundness of act and omission' (صحة الفعل والترك). In the second, compulsion follows directly.
The author uses 'فلا قدرة' and 'يلزم الإيجاب'.
3
Grasp the final clarification
The earlier answer that necessity by choice does not negate choice is valid only when power is defined as 'if He wills He does, and if He does not will He does not do' — i.e., power that includes the ability to choose otherwise.
This definition saves freedom despite the necessity arising from choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ( إذا تعلقت الإرادة لذاتها بأحد جانبي الفعل في وقت معين وعلى وجه مخصوص (فيجب ذلك الجانب ) في ذلك الوقت على ذلك الوجه (ويمتنع) الجانب (الآخر) وحينئذ (فيلزم) الإيجاب و ( سلب الاختيار. قلنا : ) أي لأنا نقول ( وجوب الشيء بالاختيار لا ينافي الاختيار بل يحققه لأنه فرعه وهاهنا بحث وهو أن إرادة أحد الضدين إن كانت مغايرة لإرادة الآخر وكانت كل واحدة منهما لذاتها متعلقة بأحدهما على التعيين اتجه أن يقال : إذا لزم أحد الإرادتين ذات المريد لم يمكن له الإرادة المتعلقة بالجانب الآخر بدلاً عن الإرادة الأولى فلا قدرة بمعنى صحة الفعل والترك، وإذا لم يلزم جاز تجدد الإرادة وحدوثها وإن لم تكن مغايرة لها بل تتعلق إرادة واحدة تارة بهذا وتارة بذاك فإذا كان تعلقها بأحدهما لذاتها لم يتصور تعلقها بالآخر ويلزم الإيجاب، وما ذكره من أن الوجوب المترتب على الاختيار لا ينافيه إنما يصحح في القدرة بمعنى إن شاء فعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل كما سبق تصويره فتذكر.
For if it is said: if will attaches by its essence to one side of an act at a specific time and in a specific manner, then that side becomes necessary at that time in that manner, and the other side becomes impossible, and then compulsion and negation of choice follow. We say: the necessity of a thing by choice does not negate choice, but rather realizes it, because it is its branch. And here there is a discussion: if the will of one of two opposites is distinct from the will of the other, and each of them by its essence attaches specifically to one of them, then it may be said: if one of the two wills necessarily accompanies the essence of the one who wills, then he cannot have the will attached to the other side instead of the first will, so there is no power in the sense of soundness of act and omission; but if it does not necessarily accompany, then renewal and origination of will are possible, even if they are not distinct, but rather a single will sometimes attaches to this and sometimes to that. So if its attachment to one of them is by its essence, its attachment to the other is inconceivable, and compulsion follows. And what he mentioned — that necessity consequent upon choice does not negate it — is only valid for power in the sense of 'if He wills He does, and if He does not will He does not do', as was previously described. So remember.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a detailed answer. The objector argues that if will is inherently specific to one side of an act, then that side becomes necessary and the other impossible, implying compulsion (ijāb) and negation of choice. The initial answer is that necessity by choice does not negate choice; rather, it is a consequence of choice and thus affirms it. The author then expands the discussion (وهاهنا بحث) by considering whether the wills for the two opposites are distinct or not. If they are distinct and each necessarily accompanies the divine essence, then only one will is possible, eliminating power to choose the opposite. If they are not necessarily accompanying, then a single will could attach to either side, but if it attaches by essence to one, the other is inconceivable, again implying compulsion. The final clarification is that the earlier answer about necessity by choice applies to power defined as 'if He wills He does, if not He does not' — a power that includes both act and omission. Thus the objection is nuanced, but the core response is maintained.

Key Terms
إيجاب
اختيار
قدرة
ضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power must mean ability to do both acts simultaneously.
Correction: The passage shows that power in the relevant sense is the ability to do or not do as willed, not the ability to actualize opposites at once.

Checkpoint
According to the expanded discussion, in which scenario(s) does compulsion appear to negate power in the sense of soundness of act and omission?
A) Only when wills are distinct and one necessarily accompanies the essence.
B) Only when wills are not distinct and attachment is by essence.
C) In both scenarios described.
D) In neither scenario.

Key Takeaway
The earlier simple answer only saves choice if power is understood as the genuine ability to will or not will; the deeper discussion shows the subtlety of the objection.

Active Knowledge and Specification: Philosophers vs. Ash'aris
Philosophers' objection and Ash'ari reply on active knowledge as specifying factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris reject the philosophers' claim that active knowledge can serve as a specifying factor.

Core Idea
Active knowledge is not automatically a specifying factor; its relation to opposites is neutral.

In this passage, we encounter a key debate in Islamic theology: whether God's knowledge alone can determine which possible world becomes actual. The philosophers argue that God's active knowledge, being a cause, suffices. The Ash'aris counter by appealing to a basic rational intuition: knowledge relates equally to both alternatives, so an additional will is needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Start by reading the objection carefully. Notice how they distinguish between passive and active knowledge.
Passive knowledge follows the known; active knowledge precedes it.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the difference between passive (infiʿālī) and active (fiʿlī) knowledge. The philosophers claim active knowledge can specify because it causes the known.
3
Understand the Ash'ari reply
The Ash'aris respond by claiming necessity (ḍarūra): knowledge and power relate equally to both sides of an opposite pair, so neither can specify.
This is a direct denial of the philosophers' claim.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if knowledge could specify, there would be no need for the attribute of will. The Ash'aris preserve will as a separate attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما قال الحكماء : لا نسلم أن كل علم فهو تبع للوقوع وإنما ذلك في العلم الانفعالي ( التابع لوجود المعلوم، وأما العلم الفعلي الذي كلامنا فيه فإنه متبوع وسبب لوقوع المعلوم فيصلح أن يكون مخصصاً كما اخترناه في الباري سبحانه وتعالى ( والأصحاب ) في جواب الحكماء ( يدعون الضرورة في استواء نسبة العلم والقدرة إلى (الطرفين فلا يكون شيء منهما مخصصاً وإن كان العلم فعلياً.
And perhaps the philosophers say: we do not concede that every knowledge is consequent upon occurrence. That is only true of passive knowledge, which follows the existence of the known. As for active knowledge, which is our topic, it is precedent and a cause for the occurrence of the known, so it is fit to be a specifying factor, as we have chosen regarding the Exalted and Transcendent. And the companions (al-aṣḥāb), in reply to the philosophers, claim necessity in the equality of the relation of knowledge and power to the two sides, so neither of them is a specifying factor, even if knowledge is active.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') and the response of the Ash'ari school (al-aṣḥāb). The philosophers argue that not all knowledge is consequent upon its object; only passive knowledge (al-ʿilm al-infiʿālī) follows the known. Active knowledge (al-ʿilm al-fiʿlī), which is God's knowledge, is precedent and a cause for the existence of the known, so it can function as the specifying factor (mukhaṣṣiṣ). The Ash'aris reply by appealing to necessity (ḍarūra): the relations of knowledge and power to both opposites are equal, so neither can specify, regardless of whether knowledge is active or passive. This maintains that a separate attribute of will is required for specification.

Key Terms
علم فعلي
علم انفعالي
مخصص
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Active knowledge can specify because it precedes its object.
Correction: The Ash'aris hold that even active knowledge relates equally to both opposites, so it cannot specify without an additional will.

Checkpoint
Why do the Ash'aris reject the philosophers' claim that active knowledge can specify?
A) Because all knowledge is passive.
B) Because knowledge relates equally to both opposites.
C) Because power is the only specifying factor.
D) Because active knowledge does not exist in God.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris maintain that knowledge, whether passive or active, has a neutral relation to opposites, thus requiring a separate attribute of will for specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though your own knowledge of right and wrong is neutral until you intentionally commit to action? Where in your life do you assume that simply knowing something is enough to change your behavior, and what does that reveal about your reliance on your will?
?
If God's perfect knowledge does not force His hand to create, but His will freely specifies, how does that challenge your view of your own responsibility? Are you making excuses for your sins by saying 'I knew better' without examining whether you actually willed to obey?
?
The Ash'aris argued that even active knowledge has equal relation to both opposites. If you claim to know God's commands but fail to act, is your knowledge truly active or merely passive? What would it mean for you to transform your passive knowledge into active obedience today?

The Eternity of God's Will: Argument from Infinite Regress
Ash'ari proof that God's will is eternal via impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that God's will must be eternal, based on the impossibility of infinite regress of wills.

Core Idea
If God's will were created, an infinite chain of wills would result—an impossibility.

The second investigation addresses the nature of God's will. The Ash'ari position is that it is eternal. The key proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if will were created, it would depend on another will, leading to an endless sequence. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text stating the proof. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it were created... then infinite regress.'
The term 'tasalsul' is key.
2
Identify the premises
The proof assumes will is dependent on the Chooser (God's essence). If created, it needs another will to bring it about.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since infinite regress of wills is impossible, the original will cannot be created; it must be eternal.
This is a standard Ash'ari argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : إرادته تعالى قديمة إذ لو كانت حادثة ولا شك أنها مستندة إلى المختار الذي هو ذاته تعالى لاحتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى مستندة إلى إرادة ثالثة وهكذا ولزم التسلسل في الإرادات الموجودة وقالت المعتزلة أي الجبائيات وعبد الجبار ومن تابعهم من المعتزلة : إنها حادثة قائمة بذاتها لا بذاته تعالى.
The second investigation: His will, exalted, is eternal, for if it were created, and there is no doubt that it is dependent upon the Chooser (al-mukhtār) who is His essence, it would need another will dependent upon a third will, and so on, and infinite regress would follow among existing wills. And the Mu'tazila — i.e., the followers of al-Jubba'i, 'Abd al-Jabbar, and those who followed them from the Mu'tazila — said that it is created, subsisting in itself, not in His essence.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage introduces the second investigation (al-baḥth al-thānī) concerning the attribute of will. The author presents the Ash'ari proof that God's will is eternal (qadīma): if it were created (ḥāditha), it would depend on the Chooser (God's essence), but as a contingent event it would require another will to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of wills. This is impossible. The Mu'tazilite position is then stated: the will is created, subsisting in itself (qā'ima bi-dhātihā) and not in God's essence. The passage thus sets up the doctrinal divide on the nature of divine will.

Key Terms
قديمة
حادثة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will could be created without causing an infinite chain because God could will it directly.
Correction: The Ash'ari argument shows that if the will is a contingent event, it requires a cause. That cause cannot be God's essence directly (since the will is an attribute of essence, not an external creation), so it would need another will, leading to regress.

Checkpoint
What does the Ash'ari argument conclude if God's will were created?
A) It would be impossible for God to have will.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress of wills.
C) It would still be eternal.
D) The Mu'tazila would be correct.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof against a created will rests on the impossibility of an infinite chain of wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, what does that mean for your excuses that 'I'm not ready yet' or 'I'll change later'? In which specific area of your obedience (prayer, honesty, purity) are you delaying submission by assuming God's will can accommodate your timetable?
?
The argument says an endless chain of created wills is impossible. Think about your own endless cycle of intentions: 'I will repent, but first...' 'I will be better, but not yet...' Are you caught in an infinite regress of your own making? What created 'will' are you relying on instead of bending to the eternal will of your Creator?
?
This proof confronts the idea that God's will could change to match your shifting desires. When you beg God for something you want, do you truly want it only if it aligns with His eternal will, or do you secretly hope His will bends to yours? What complaint in your heart about your circumstances reveals a disbelief in the eternity of His decree?
